THE RED PAGE
Brother Copas —“Q.” (Dymock’s.) A very
pleasant story of some realistic Brothers
in an imaginary “College of Noble Pov-
erty.” There is a flavor of the classics
in their talk, and Brother Copas re-
cites portion of his translation of
Pervigilium Veneris in Swinburnian
verse.
Lord Bellinger —Harry Graham (Dymoek’s).
Clever satire of the middle-class peers
of England.
Down our Street —J. E. Buckrose (Mills and
Boon). Humorous suburbanites. Mrs.
Bean is an attractive ci'eation, some-
thing like Mrs. Wiggs of the Cabbage
Patch.
The Bermondsey Twin —F. J. Randall
(Dymock’s)." Farcical complications
which arise though a long-lost twin
(with a sense of humor) turning up and
impersonating his smug, successful
brother.
The Lead of Honor —Norval Richardson. A
well-made story of love and adventure,
somewhat better than the usual Ameri-
can mushy novel.
Love and Treasure Laden —Will Brooke
(Dymock’s). A plain tale of varied ad-
ventures at sea and in Australia 50
yeai’s ago. There is something about
gold mining, river navigation, pearling
in Torres Strait, and an encounter with
the famous Bob Towns; but not much
about love.
Shaw
George Bernard Shaw was satisfied at an
early age that he is a gi-eat man. He hasf
no false modesty, and no regard for any con-
ventions which would prevent him from pro-
claiming that fact aloud. A natural desire
to obtain the benefit of his greatness in his
own time led him to play fantastic tricks
and throw startling gibes at the public.
Talked about, laughed at, abused —eventu-
ally he was heard. George B. Shaw did
not wait to be discovered; he discovered him-
self, and has never since allowed himself to
be hidden. And now he is the most fre-
quently discussed person in the literary
woi’ld.
It would be small comfort for such a man
to know that after death he would be dealt
with by his biographer in five volumes. He
could have been his own Boswell if he had
had time; in fact, he has thrown out a large
amount of biogi-aphical matter in newspaper
articles, and interpreted himself liberally in
prefaces. However, he has done well in
catching his biographer young, filling him up
with material, and helping him with explana-
tions. '
Archibald Henderson, who is responsible
for George' Bernard Shaw: His Life and
Works (Sydney Book plub), is an American
professor who has already written a biog-
l-aphy of Mark Twain, and some essays on
Ibsen, Maeterlinck, Shaw and others whom
he styles Interpreters of Life (Duckworth).
His “authorised critical biography” of Shaw
is a bulky vqlurne (sold here at 255.), illus-
trated with many portraits, caricatures, etc.
Hendei-son seems a l-ather earnest person
who has taken Shaw tod seriously. He has
x’epeated himself frequently, and made the
book too big. Its principal value is that, in
addition to the record of Shaw’s, progress
through life, it contains most of the inter-
esting things said about Shaw, and a large
amount of autobiography.
Born on the 26th July, 1856, of a
Protestant family in Dublin —the father a
narrow-minded, impecunious merchant, the
mother an accomplished musician—with no
schooling worth mentioning, and a little
business training:—
I crossed St. George’s channel into the
decadence of the mid-nineteenth-century Eng-
land of Anthony Trollope, and slowly made
my way to the forefront of the age—the
pei’iod of Ibsen, Nietzsche, the Fabian So-
ciety, the motor-car, and my own writings.
The progress was slow, for in nine years,
1876-1885, he received altogether £6 for his
literary efforts; his clothes turned green, and
his shirt cuffs were cut to the quick. Mean-
while he was educating himself and living
on his mother, who obtained a small income
by teaching singing.
I did not thi-ow myself into the struggle
for life: I thi'ew my mother into it.
From his mother he says that he derived his
brains and character —“which do her credit”
—and that thorough knowledge of music
which made him later on the ablest musical
critic of his time.
In that apprenticeship period he wrote
five -novels, which were persistently refused
by publishers until H. H. Champion, now
of the Melbourne Book Lover, printed
“Cashel Byron’s Profession” in the Socialist
paper, To-Day. Champion admired the story
so much that he stereotyped the pages and
published the book at Is. Henley, Steven-
son and others praised it, but the public re-
mained indifferent. . /
Shaw’s novels do not count for much, but
in them are to be found the first drafts of
many ideas and characters used subsequently
in the plays. Some of these ideas are
original, and Henderson claims that G.B.S.
arrived independently at conclusions which
he is said to owe to Nietzsche and others.
Shaw admits his debt to many people
Samuel Butler of Erewhon, Karl Marx,
Sidney Webb, etc.
I am an expert picker of other men’s
brains, and I have been exceptionally fortu-.
nate in my fi-iends.
He read Shelley and became a vegetarian;
he heard Henry George and turned to So-
cialism. He induced Sidney Webb to join
the Fabian Society, and began a fiery cam-
paign which has had a considerable influence
in British politics. By force of will the
“nervous, shrinking novice” made himself an
efficient speaker, and orated from platform
or cart-tail. He was wx-iting art criti-
cism then and living “like a‘greengrocer and
unlike a minor poet.”
I have lived instead of dreaming and feed-
ing myself with artistic confectionery.
It is in that fact of living energetically that
Shaw’s success lies. He is a Force by vir-
tue of wit derived from Celtic forebears, and
audacity, which he cultivated, being used
incessantly to propagate ideas. His phil-
osophy, wherever it may be derived, is
boiled down to this: —
Life is no “brief candle” for me. It is a
sort of splendid torch which I have got hold
of for the moment, and I want to make it
burn as brightly as possible before handing
it on to future generations.
His policy is stated moi-e than onqe by his
characters: —
Take care to get what you like or you
will be forced to like what you get.
The only really simple thing to do is to
go sti'aight for wfiat you want and get it.
By slashing attacks upon the false gods of
Respectability, by trenchant di’amatic criti-
cism which helped to destroy conventional
standards, by paradox and epigram, he got
what he wanted —the figure. of “G. 8.5.”
prominently before the public. Then he
bi-ought on his own plays. In these there
are types new to the stage; people as they
ai - e essentially, but not as they parade the
streets; sometimes crude and blatant, but
alive with the ideas and wit of Shaw. In-
ferior as works of dramatic art, they are ex-
cellent for propaganda purposes. And they
are refreshing to read, titillating the brain
like an electric shampoo. By supplying
full stage directions and explanatory pre-
faces for the reader, he has given modern
drama a chance of success as literature.
Though this book is mainly autobiography,
Henderson’s work in piecing it together, and
in his running commentax-y, is valuable to
anyone who wishes to understand that de-
lightful phenomenon—G.B.S.
C. K. Allen writes from Oxfoi-d (Eng.)
about the latest Shaw play:—
The other day I saw “Fanny’s First
Play,” at the Little Theatre, with Lillah
McCarthy in the chief part. According to
the rule of the theatx-e, the play is pro-
duced anonymously, but the identity of the
author is unmistakable within five min-
utes of the ring-up. In the induction,
Fanny, the Girton daughter of a father
whose whim it is to live in the eighteenth
centui-y, is pi-ivately producing her first
play, at which she has secured the presence
of four eminent critics. After one of them,
Trotter (no other than A. B. Walkley, who
is reported to have given sittings, as it
were for his portrait) has made a prelimin-
ary fool of himself in an interview with the
coltish authoress, the play proper begins.
Bobby Gilbey and Margaret Knox have
been pitchforked into a kind of semi-en-
gagement by their acutely respectable shop-
keeping parents. Bobby, in company with
a daughter of joy, bashes a policeman’s hel-
met, and gets 14 days. The daughter of
joy calls on Bobby’s parents and explains
the situation in an excruciating interview.
Meanwhile, things have been happening
to Margaret. . She gets spiritually intoxi-
cated at a revival meeting, and goes into a
music-hall in search of experience: there
she makes the acquaintance of a French
naval officer, finds herself in a dancing-
saloon, and subsequently in a mix-up with
the police: in short, 14 days. Naturally
there is a cross-ruffing game of concealment
between the parents; but, behold, Margaret
has bumped hard up against real life, and
she is going to conceal nothing. TheiVare
mutual disclosures between Bobby and Mar-
gai*et; thei-e is a discussion between the
parents, wherein it appears that the dis-
grace has lent a pleasurable flavor of
wickedness to the Methodist existences of
the husbands, who are brought up with a
jerk by the sepulchral Mrs. Knox: there is
an enormous centi’e-stage oration by the
Frenchman, contrasting French .and Eng-
lish women, to the advantage of the latter:
and there is Juggins, the Gilbeys’ butler,
who posseses the common-sense of the party,
and in the approved style of the chauffeur in
“Man and Superman,” and the waiter in
“You Never Can Tell,” turns out to be a
duke’s brother,' and finally marries Mar-
garet, who t has long cherished a secret pas-
sion for him. Lastly comes the epilogue,
in which the critics deliver their judgments,
all too supeidatively silly to be anything but
a Shavian parody on Shaw.
And so the guns of the critics are spiked.
Naturally, they don’t like it. Grein, for
instance, calls the anonymity cowardly, and
declines to walk into the ingenious tnouse-
trap. After all, you don’t refute criti-
cism, merely by anticipating it: and some
of Pshaw’s self-judgments come very near
the mai’k. It is all admirable fooling: we
laughed as only Pshaw can make us laugh
—a hesitating giggle, and then the uncon-
trollable crescendo as the consummate ab-
surdity sinks in. And the laughs were
helped by the admirable acting, for Pshaw
does know how to select a cast. But while
his sense of incongruity is as incomparable
as ever, there is a distressing lack of the
old substantial, stentorian Pshaw. The
B. P. can’t altogether do without him yet,
for he hasn’t exhausted that splendid source
of fun, himself. But was there not a time
when he had something besides fun?
Wells, “Marie Claire,” and
Om Henry
“Meriden”: About the respective merits
of Marie Claire and The New Machiavelli,
Mrs. Jerome (“Red Page” 18/5/T1) exaiis
the former, to the disadvantage of Wells s
book; but to my mind The .New Machiavelli
is infinitely the finer work, simply because
it possesses those characteristics which Mrs.
Jerome attacks. Its triumph is in its
masterly analysis of a man’s developing
mind: in the story of Richard Remington
we are shown, instead of the stock emotion
of the usual novel-puppet, a mirror of that
period of half-guessed mysteries through
which all men must pass. To greet such a
delineation with the outci-y "Vulgar!” is
surely, in these days, an anachronism —a
relic of those sorry times in which the hu-
man body itself became “indecent” to the
unwashed monks of the Thebaid.
Aiarie Claire affects me merely as an ap-
peal to the ear and eye: it conjures
up pale and delicate word-pictures, each
as inconclusive as a dream....lt is a
"bleached” story, one washed free from the
sinful color of life. Here and there we are
led almost to the brink of some momentous
event, only to turn the page and find that
nothing happens, or will happen, or can hap-
pen. After the briefest glimpse of daylight
and strife we find ourselves back again in
that placid moonlit garden of trimmed rose-
bushes which is the soul of Marie Claire... .
Much of Wells’s book is “hard” ; but it is a
deliberate hardness, an emotional reserve:
you can still feel the pulse beating below.
The same cannot with truth be said of the
“softness” of Marguerite Audoux. That is
spontaneous: the “exquisite expi’ession of a
temperament” “curiously detached” from
reality. Many may seek no further than
uie pretty little walled-in flower-garden of
such a temperament: but to those who live
a fuller life there is more enchantment in
those “worlds beyond the wall” of which
Wells has written —the realities of the hu-
man soul.
* * * *
“Jean Sibi”: One fears that few will
agree with Mrs. Helen Jerome’s hectic appre-
ciation of Marie Claire. I bought the book,
and tried to uplift myself into that innocent
heaven where everything is so delight-
fully respectable. Marie Claire left me
as hopelessly mundane, suburban and as
prosaical as before. One could not see in
the book any excuse for writing mad eulo-
gies in feverish adjectives and fussy nouns.
One only saw a procession of animals, peas-
ants and religieuses stalking through the
volume like decorous phantoms, carefully
pausing at the commas, sitting down at the
full-stops, and determined never to intrude
on the prodigious margins. Doubtless those
dear ghosts have a certain beauty and divine
spirituality to rhapsodes, though I am more
impressed by the fact that their orderly pro-
gression through Marie Claire cost me 3s.
6d. to watch and produced intolerable ennui
during the performance.
As to the booming of the book by Octave
Mix-beau and a literary cotei-ie, one can say
that one’s admiration for those people is
tempered by the knowledge that French lit-
terateurs are notoriously fickle and unre-
liable in their literary judgments. I can-
not forget that the poetry of Stephane Mal-
larme was denounced by the foremost critics
of his generation, and was subsequently so
acclaimed by Paris and Fi-ance that he was
called Poete des Po&tes, and received the
adulation of pei-sons so different in their
literai-y “likes” as Adolphe Rettd and
Francois Coppee. I can also recall that
Barbey d’Aurevil'ly led the herd in journal-
istic philippics against the Parnassians,
which included, among other great persons,
Paul Yerlaine and that brilliant, burn-out
amorist, Catulle Mendes. Those facts well
support the suspicion that French critics
will call the genius of to-day the nonentity
of to-morrow, with the same conscienceless
aplomb as they will make the witless ob-
scurity of Monday the adorable artist of the
following week. For that reason one must
slightly discount the ardent “spruiching” of
Octave Mirbeau and Co., without forgetting
also that the exploitation of an alleged “lit-
erary saint” means no inconsiderable addi-
tion to their own prestige. They gain in
popular estimation in their fiery “flotation”
of Miss Audoux, and, like thorough-going
Gauls, are not above exciting attention to
their efforts by bowing with the lady to their
own applause.
* * * *
“Dele” objects to Dyson’s depreciation of
O. Henry : —-
Granted that O. Henry descends to word-
juggling, he rises to word-painting of no
mean order. At times he is a willing prey
to the fascinations of slang—argot—jargon :
but always because slang, as Gelett Bur-
gess has said, is “the illegitimate sister of
poetry.”
Both stand for lavish luxuriance in trope
and involution, for floriation and adorn-
ment of thought. It is their boast to
make two words bloom where one grew
before. Both garb themselves in meta-
phor, and the only complaint of the cap-
tious can be that, whereas Poetry fol-
lows the accepted style, Slang dresses her
thought to suit herself in fantastic and
bizarre caprices; that her whims are un-
stable, and too often in bad taste. But
this odium given to Slang by superficial
minds is undeserved.”
This was written of American slang,
which comes not only from the mouths of
a nation of artists in the verbal graphic,
but largely from the keen, intensive brains
of slick newspaper men of the type ot
George Ade, Gelett Burgess, Will and Wal-
lace Irwin, and O. Henry himself. To that
extent it is a hot-house plant, susceptible to
the cold breath of alien criticism —but in
its own atmosphere it blooms with a tropic
luxuriance all its own. Of that slang, Aus-
tralia knows little or nothing. The nation
whose idea of superlative excellence is con-
veyed by the nonsense-word “boshter” has
yet to devise such expressions of praise as
“the king-pin,” "the snowy-petailed mar-
guerite,” “the star-bright looloo,” which at
the least are tolerably ingenious imagery.
Truly, too much of it palls—but how little
of "boshter” suffices to pall also. The only
ingenious slang that has appeared in Aus-
tralian print is in Edward Dyson’s own
book, "Fact’ry ’Ands” ; and how much of it
is the factory hand’s, and how much is Dy-
son’s, let those declare who, .. in search of
local color, have mashed the Melbourne fac-
tory girl on Alexandra Avenue, and listened
to her artless discourse upon men and
things. It must be admitted that O. Henry
is a master of the mechanics of his marion-
ette-made slang, but at times the figure
works so passing well that one dare swear
it were alive. But slang, grotesque and
exotic as it is, is no garb for immortality,
and the very bricks with which O. Henry
built so many of his literary structures—
and there stands in his name a city of di-
versified architecture—bear in themselves
the germ of crumbling and decay. He cut,
perhaps, no marble: but the tale of bricks
was faithfully told.
“Colac” asks for the names of some useful
books on Socialism. In the new “Home
University Library” (Is. 3d. per vol.) there
is a good monograph on The Socialist Move-
ment, by J. liamsay Macdonald, with a list
of other books worth studying. The fol-
lowing might be added to Macdonald’s list:
Jensen, The Rising Tide (Worker Office,
Sydney), Is.; Kelly, Twentieth Century
Socialism, 95.; Le Rossignol, State Social-
ism in N.Z., 65.; Shaw, Socialism and Su-
perior Brains, Is. 3d. George’s Progress
and Poverty, which turned Shaw and many
others to Socialism, is still valuable.
* * * *
“Bluenose” wants books which will help
in self-education on general questions.
Knowledge is Power, by Philip Gibbs, pub-
lished by E. Arnold, London, at 3s. 6d., is
perhaps the best guide. The range of
subjects is wide, and the tone as little prig-
gish as possible. Scientific Ideas of To-
day, by 0. R. Gibson (Dymock’s, 65.), ex-
plains in simple language the nature of mat-
ter, electricity, light, heat, etc. —so far as
their nature is understood. Seeley’s
“Library of Romance” includes some en-
tertaining volumes at ss. each. One on
Modern Invention, by A, Williams, will put
the reader almost up to date on such mat-
ters as wireless telegraphy, liquid air, air-
ships, submarines, advanced photography,
etc.
* * * *
The Australian Onlooker, a new quarterly
whose first number is dated March last,
states that it “adds another effort to the
already long list of journals whose main ob-
ject has been the furtherance of genuine
literature in this country.” Such an honor-
able intention will excite sympathy, and
may excite a flow of half-crowns to the man-
ager’s address —19 Queen-street, Melbourne.
2s. 6d. is the annual subscription.
It must be understood that in the opinion
of the Onlooker “genuine literature” is
something different to that “form of litera-
ture which is a degenerate outcome of
Lindsay Gordon, and which may be termed
the Cup and Stirrup School, inasmuch as
its muse is mainly vocal upon the subjects
of malted liquor and the turf.”
This organ of the Superior Person is of
the same size as London Punch. Its at-
tempts at humor are feeble; but it has some
good verses by Enid Derham, a translation
from De Banville by A. T. Strong, and a
sensible review of Turner’s First Decade of
the' Australian Commonwealth by O’Dowd.
It is not safe to prophesy on the strength or
the weakness of first numbers. The On-
looker has begun low enough to leave plenty
of room for rising higher.
* * * *
Randolph Bedford writes:—
Mr. Albert Dorrington included in a story
of an alleged Bully Hayes, published in Life,
40 lines or so descriptive of pearling
in Torres Strait, which seemed familiar.
I at last discovered that these 40 lines or
so had been lifted bodily from one of a
series of pearling articles which I wrote
for a syndicate, including S. M. Herald, in
1906. And in the current Life I find more
of it, mixed up with inaccuracies of Mr.
Dorrington’s own. A typographical error
in the S. M. Herald's article drops him into
the trap. I wrote “Binghis,” meaning York
Peninsula aboriginals; S. M. Herald made
it “Burghis,” and now Mr. Dorrington
talks of “a. half-caste Burghis man.” There
are _ other inaccuracies, which convict him
of ignorance of the Strait and the pearl
fisheries.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Poet’s Window.
From out the window of his soul,
As from a tower, he peers.
He sees the misty cloud-wracks roll;
He looks across the years.
He watches, from the niche on high,
The peoples of the world go by.
He hears the clamor of the street;
He hears the city’s roar;
The roll of wheels, the tramp of feet
Along the river shore;
He sees the river, dark and wide,
Co winding through the country-side.
Along the lawns, beneath the trees,
Trim youths and maidens, pass
By bordered plots and terraces,
And on the smooth-cut grass;
And children’s voices at their play
Float to his window day by day.
The dainty ladies, gaily clad,
More brilliant than the blooms;
The poor, the rich, the gay, the sad ;
Gilt youths and smooth-faced grooms;
The laborer, heavy-foot and slow—
All pass his window, to and fro.
From Death, the Harvester, he gleans,
Through contemplation long,
A spoil of many fields and scenes
Wheteout he weaves his song;
Mot heeding profit of his pains,
Nor counting his uncertain gains.
VICTORIA.
G. G. A. COLLES.
Going On for Eighty Year.
Mother, I’ve been dreaming, dreaming, dreaming
all the eventide
Of a cottage in the country over on the Tambo side.
You and I were there together, we who love each
Other so,
Seated ’midst a many flowers, in the evening’s after-
glow.
There were wrinkles on your forehead, and your hair
was strewn with grey,
But your cheeks had caught the color of the slowly
dying day,
And you smiled as one grown gentle in the face of
conquered fear ;
“You are happy,” 1 was saying, “going on for eighty
year.”
And your voice! “Do you remember, lad of mine
who loves me so,
How you used to tell your father in the days of long
ago—
‘We will buy the old place, daddy, where your little
laddie grew,
With a'heap of things for mammy, when I’m just as
big as you’ ?
Ah! you little thought you hurt him with your
simple prattle then ;
But you’ve kept your childish promise, and I’m glad
we’re back again.”
And I watched you looking outward with a smile
that hid a tear—
i Is there sometimes joy in sadness, going on for
j eighty year ?
, But I’m waking, mother, waking, now the long hot
day is dead,
And my heart is aching, aching for the vision that
has fled,
For the dear old Tambo River with its ever-running
rune, '
For the old, white roofs of Beulah in the pathway
of the moon,
For the seat among the flowers, where we lingered,
you and I,
When our lives were rosy-colored as the sunset in
the sky ;
But the four walls of the city hold me as a captive
here,
And, my mother, you are lonely, going on for eighty
year.
Ah! the hope that kept us working—Beulah yours
until the end,
Mine the river flats for tillage and the mountain
flocks to tend.
But a stranger reigns ac Beulah where the Spring
doth linger yet,
And we dwell at Parting-places in the valley of
Regret. , . ,
Oh! ray heart-strings seem to harden, and I dare no
longer think ;
Christ who sweated in the garden had no cup like
this to drink ;
| But if strength of love availeth, mother, be of better
cheer ;
| You shall yet find joy of being, going bn for eighty
year.
QUEENSLAND.
MILKY WHITE.
Coffin Ships.
Old iron tanks that are rolling ever,
Back and forward upon the main;
Sent to sea with the hope that never
Into port they will steam again.
Decks a-wash and their plimsolls under;
Bilge pumps running to keep them free.
Day by day on their ways they blunder;
Staggering blindly upon the sea.
Rusted cranks which are weakly turning
Save when they race in a drunken way;
Certain death is the greaser earning,
A racing shaft and a broken stay.
So they shoulder the big seas under,
“How do they do it?” It’s hard to say.
The gale is rising. We pause and wonder
Where they’ll be at the close of day.
J'.S.W.
FRANK JESSUP.
Idols.
While I may forward look and back
A-down a vista lit with love,.
I shall not break my heart for lack
Of riches, nor seek joy thereof.
For when 1 look into your eyes
And see your heart’s gold shining there,
I know love is a richer prize
Than they that worship riches share.
Their idol is cold gold, but mine
Warm flesh and tender thought, and breath
Made beautiful with speech divine,
That fears no bitterness in death.
Yet, since my love is all in all,
I tremble lest a rough word shake
The altar and my idol fall; . . .
For then indeed my heart would break!
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.

SOME ADVICE TO LITTLE WILLIE.
The Speech at the opening of the N.S.W. Parliament mentioned with joy that the Labor Government had borrowed all the money it needed within the State.
AUNTIE DAILY PAPER: “You must keep on being a good boy, Willie, and always be kind to the nice fat gentleman, who. is really a poor
widow and a lone orphan. And when you do anything for him you mustn’t ask him to pay ; you must be a nice good boy and
ask him to lend you the money, and then you’ll be able to supply him with interest all your life. And he’ll be so glad that in time
he may adopt you for his own little boy.”

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

THURSDAY, JUNE 8, 1911.
IN THE CAUSE OF PEACE.
When the other day the Acting-Premier of
N.S.W. referred to the trouble caused by
men who frivol with other men’s wives, and
gave it as his opinion that they should be
gaoled, not because such misconduct is mor-
ally wrong, but because it enrages the in-
jured party, he opened up a line large vista
in front of the reformer. There are many
maddening things in the world, but there
is probably nothing that causes quite so
many murderous thoughts as the political
catch-cry. Our party government system pre-
scribes that Liberals and Laborites shall
range themselves on opposite sides in every
big question that comes up for discussion.
The average man knows little about the
matters at issue; consequently he recog-
nises his incompetence to argue them
except in the home circle, where his
wildest pronouncements are, of course,
incontestable. Outside that circle he
falls back in self-defence on some
plausible gag supplied by his party leaders
or party newspapers, and exploits it. with
slight variations, whenever he is challenged
as to his views on this or that problem of
the hour. The gag is always designedly
irritating to the other side. Thus the rank
and file of “Liberals” swelled their chests
and gave “Home Rule” and . “Freedom” as
their reasons for voting “No” to the Refer-
enda proposals. Inferentially anyone who
favdred a “Yes” vote was for bondage. In
“The New Machiavelli.” by Wells, the cen-
tral figure is quoting from the diary of his
well-meaning but hopelessly stupid mother:
D. would not go to church, and hardened his heart
and said wicked and infidel things, much disrespect
of the clergy. The anthem is tiresome! ! !
That men should set up to be wiser than their
Maher !
“Dreadful little tangle of misapprehensions
and false judgments,” is Wells s exasper-
ated comment on the lady’s effort. It pre-
cisely fits the average political gag, and the
manner and spirit in which it is exploited.
* * * *
An Empire Day festival made its first ap-
pearance on the calendar recently, and was
boomed by an undistinguished old British
person of leisure named Meath. Certain
Australian citizens, mostly politicians and
professional men in need of self-advertise-
ment, banded themselves into leagues, and
took to speechifying in the streets and in the
State schools on May 24 —folks like B.
Smith, M.P., and Judkins, of Melbourne,
being particularly wordy and effusive on
these occasions. At a time when the blither
about Imperialism was at its height, Aus-
tralian “liberalism” formally appropriated
this Empire Day, in a manner of speaking,
thus again demonstrating its unique girt
for "cottoning on” to the most futile of Old-
World movements. Presently Britain start-
ed to speak of the Boer war as "a mistake.”
The Enited Kingdom began to view Mate-
king Day and its rejoicings, and the old
daredevil talk about singeing Kruger’s whis-
kers, in their proper perspective. And, sim-
ultaneously, Empire Day started to wilt.
In the United Kingdom, S’Africa, Canada
and India it w r ould seem to be now practi-
cally extinct. In Australia, however, a
political party having fathered the function,
there is a disinclination to admit that the
thing which was acclaimed immortal has
gone the way of all fluff. So a multitude of
blackguardly motives are attributed by “Lib-
eralism” to all who decline to go through
certain ceremonies yearly in the presence of
a piece of bunting wdiich has eight
times out of ten been imported from
Germany. Thus one Smithurst, of Syd-
ney, wrote to the papers last week blandly
propounding that those who neglected to
hoist “the grand old Union Jack which had
braved,” etc., over their dwellings on Em-
pire Day, but instead hoisted an Australian
flag, were unworthy of the esteem of respec-
table citizens. “Liberal” papers in Syd-
ney, Melbourne and elsewhere went several
steps further, and stated bluntly that the
man who omitted to celebrate Empire Day
was one who yearned for “the disruption of
the Empire”—a traitor, a biter of the
hand that had fed him, and a hundred other
opprobrious things. And immediately, in
all those centres which take people like
Smithurst and papers ■ like the Argus
seriously, the landscape became a mass of
citizens snarling fierce charges of mad “dis-
loyalty” and “revolutionary views,” and
counter-charges of lying and dishonesty, to
the imminent danger of the public peace.
Of course no sane Australian wants to dis-
rupt the Empire. The Empire-disrupter
is the same old fraud as W. E. Johnson’s
myth, who was represented as advocating an
Australian fleet because he wanted to see it
“blow the British Navy out of the water.”
The type that refuses to “celebrate” Empire
Day does so for all sorts of sound reasons,
such as: (1) Because he hasn’t time, (2)
because he lias watched Joseph Cook or
llenley celebrating, and has a sense of the
grotesque, (3) because it reminds him of
old-time hat-smashing and bar-looting or-
gies he would rather forget, and (4) be-
cause he lacks the music-hall instinct. His
attitude is perfectly logical, but it is never
accepted as such by the people who have as-
similated the “Liberal” catch-cries on this
subject, and his portion is licensed insult
till the excitement simmers down.
The “Liberal’s” muddled frame of : mind
concerning the enemy was never exemplified
better than during the Dreadnought cam-
paign. Dreadnausea is now recognised to
have been a mere party manoeuvre, designed
to put Fisher out of office, and Reid in.
No one in his senses considered that loyalty
to Ed. VII. entered into the controversy.
Nevertheless, the well-liked monarch was
dragged into the fray, and there he stopped.
It was the custom of George Reid and
other Dreadnought orators, on being re-
quested to state their objections to an Aus-
tralian fleet, either to call excitedly for
“three cheers for His Majesty,” or to ask
the audience to rise in unison and sing “God
Save the King.” This laid the contraver-
sialist out flat, as indeed it would have de-
feated a Demosthenes. Also it impressed
on a muddy-minded populace that all who
weren’t for George Reid were in some
vague unexplained way hostile to Edward
VII. There were thousands of backyard
rows in 1909 over Brown’s inability to ex-
plain to Smith that, while believing in an
Australian fleet, he (Brown) did not neces-
sarily want to see the King of England de-
throned ; and Jones’s dogged, unreasoning
assertion that all who were against Dread-
nausea were necessarily also “disloyalists,”
made innumerable Nationalists rage.
In a really civilised community all this
shouting and insult would be condemned to
death. If a youth so much as addresses a
strange young woman in the street he is
seized and dealt with by the Law. “Abus-
ive language” is an offence; so is the em-
ployment of any of a thousand other means
of provoking people. But no one does a
thing to the citizen who infuriates his fel-
low man by publicly accusing him of trai-
torous conduct, gross ingratitude, and a
yearning for Blood. The accusations are
never true. They are arrived at by a pro-
cess of rotten logic, a striking instance of
which occurred in the Anti-Socialist cam-
paign, where it was argued that some La-
bprites were Socialists, and that certain So-
cialists were atheists and advocates of free
love, wherefore all who supported the Aus-
tralian Labor party were in favor of abol-
ishing religion, destroying the marriage tie,
and bursting up home life. It enrages a
respectable citizen to be publicly accused of
sentiments like these, almost as much as it
enrages him to find a friend making love to
his wife, and certainly far more than it does
to curse at him. _ So the political insult, if
only because it is the most frequent and
virulent of modern insults, is one of the
first things a Government which seeks to
promote peace and good feeling in the com-
munity should lay itself out to abolish.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Progressive Land Tax Held Valid.
In unanimously declaring the land-tax valid,
the High Court did the obvious thing. As
Justice Higgins put it: —
A great part of the argument for plaintiff must
seem to an outsider grotesque. Counsel had taken the
Acts and had examined ever}' nook and corner with
microscopic care in order to find, if possible, some
provision which had transgressed the Constitution in
any particular, even the most insignificant, and they
had applied great industry and ingenuity to demons-
trating that if such and such a provision was treated
as invalid the remainder of the Act would be “sub-
stantially different” from what Parliament intended,
and must be invalid also. It was not pretended
that the impugned provisions affected the' plaintiff,
but if the plaintiff could show that the whole of the
leg'slation was bad because of some provision which
did not concern him, he would be free from ob-
ligation to pay tax. Into such barren intellectual
gymnastics they were forced in this case, and pro-
bably in cases to come. In this case, however, he
was glad to find that whatever their individual
opinions might be as to the proper principles of
severability, all the members of the Court agreed in
the view that either no invalidity had been shown,
or that, if there was any invalidity, the invalid pro-
vision could be severed from the rest of the Acts
which remained valid.
Nevertheless, it is plain that the Federal
Government still has a host of land-tax
troubles before it. The judgment of C.J.
Griffith foreshadows some provisions which
may be declared invalid in cases to come,
viz., (1) the acquisition clause, which em-
powers the Commonwealth to acquire land
at an owner’s valuation plus 10 per cent.;
and (2) the section which provides for the
taxing of the absentee shareholder’s interest
in land. Justice O’Connor said in the lat-
ter connection: —
Technically, no doubt, the holder of shares in a
company had no interest in the lands of the com-
pany ; but, looking at the substance of the matter,
every owner of a share was interested in the lands
of a company proportionately to the number of his
shares, just as a partner was interested in the lands
of a partnership. The Legislature, disregarding the
provisions of _ common law, imposed a tax according
to the real interest of the shareholder, taking the
number of his shares as a measure of his interest.
It might be that the section was open to the objec-
tion that it was not a valid exercise of the power of
taxation.
The acquisition clause was borrowed from
Maoriland, which is untrammelled by a Con-
stitution that habitually gets in the way of
popular legislation. It has proved there the
most effective and economical device for pre-
venting landholders from defrauding the
Government by undervaluing their property.
The owner has necessarily a pretty accurate
idea of the value of his land. By requiring
that he shall put his own price on it for
purposes of taxation, the Government does
him no wrong, causes him the minimum of
hardship, and avoids a vast amount of liti-
gation, valuers’ fees and expense generally.
The public interests are safeguarded by the
fact that. a landowner undervalues his es-
tate at his own peril. As to the other sec-
tion, apparently the Court may hold on some
future occasion that, while a tax on land is
valid, a tax on an absentee shareholder’s in-
terest in land isn’t. The logic of the matter
is beyond the layman’s comprehension. Cer-
tainly such a conclusion will be a. violent
and unpleasant shock to the bulk of the
Commonwealth electors. Already there
looms over the horizon a fresh blizzard of
litigation, in which the people of Australia
stand to have at least two of their cherished
proposals blown right out with heavy costs
for the sole benefit (1) of the local land
monopolist, and (2) of an oversea capital-
ist who, for the most part, has as much per-
sonal interest in Australia as he has in the
fortunes of Iceland. It is a dreary prospect:
the only cheerful feature is the fact that
such happenings tend to make thinking
people more and more favorable to unifica-
tion—-the system of government which Brit-
ain, S’Africa and Maoriland enjoy.

A New Bill and a Good One.
An interesting characteristic of the Indus-
trial Bill which was introduced in the
N.fcS.W. Legislative Assembly last week is its
family likeness to the Arbitration Act of
181)9. The grouping together of industries,
the making of trades unions industrial
units, the principle of collective bargaining
were all essential features of the old Act.
The Wages Board system is, in its present
form, admittedly hopeless. If Wages Boards
were suffered to go on increasing as they
have increased in the last couple of years,
every barrister in the "country would shortly
have a job as chairman* of one of theSe in-
stitutions, and hall- the population would
spend the daylight hours giving evidence be-
fore them: The grouping system should eli-
minate that abuse. Beery’s proposal is an
improvement on the old arrangement. Under
B. It. Wise’s Act the Court had power to
refer all technical matters to Boards of ex-
perts—virtually to Wages Boards. The
Court’s duty, in theory, was to deal broadly
with rates of wages, hours of work, prefer-
ence to unionists, piece work and appren-
tices. In practice Justice Cohen attempted
to handle personally all the details which he
might have referred elsewhere, with the re-
sult that the Court became hopelessly con-
gested. The difference herein between the
old Act and the new is that whereas in the
former case reference to Boards was option-
al, in the latter it is compulsory. The re-
adoption of trades unions as industrial
units means, amongst other things, that none
but organised Labor bodies will be entitled
to apply for an award. Under the Wade
Act any 20 persons could apply, one effect
of which was to pave the way for the form-
ation of bogus unions. The principle of col-
lective bargaining, now restored, should
never have been dropped. Under that system,
within three years of the passing of the
original Act, more than half the industries
in N.S.W. were working under industrial
agreements voluntarily arrived at by the vari-
ous organisations and registered in the Court.
C. Griffith’s wholly unexpected decision
that a voluntary agreement could not be
turned into a common rule—in short, that
before there could be a common rule there
must be a dispute and litigation—disturbed
65,000 workers, and materially assisted to
bring about the breakdown of the Act. To
the best features of the old Bill Minister
Beeby has added a number of excellent new
ones. Conciliation committees have long
since justified themselves in Canada. They
are based on the presumption that working
men don’t throw up decent jobs out of mey'f
fractious waywardness, and that, therefore,
if some person in authority—a qualified um-
pire, so to speak—is on hand to consider the
merits of each fresh dispute as it crops up,
and before tools have actually been downed,
much trouble may be averted. The pro-
vision allowing unions to strike, under cer-
tain circumstances, amounts to a challenge
to the horse-sense of the Labor Party’s foes,
the Socialists. The irreconcilables, in com-
mon with other workers, are offered every
inducement to give the Arbitration principle
a fair trial. If, having once registered, they
strike, they will be fined, and the money
collected by means of a garnishee on their
future earnings. On the other hand, they
can refuse to register, and exercise man’s in-
alienable right to cease work when he feels
like it. In the Minister’s opinion, self-in-
terest will ultimately drive these wild
workers to the Court, as the place where,
after all, they can do most financial good
for themselves. On paper it is by far the
likeliest-looking industrial measure that has
.vet been presented to Australia. But. in the
last resort, its success must, of course, de-
pend on the sort of treatment it gets. There
is no industrial legislation that incompe-
tent or unsympathetic administration won’t
swiftly slay.

The Rent Dragon.
Nothing in the programme of the N.S.W.
Labor Government is of more bread-and-
butter value than the proposals to use the
resources of the State to provide the people
with cheaper lodging. In this respect Ma
has hitherto shown up miserably, with the
result that every burst of prosperity has
seen the rent-payers of Sydney and suburbs
mercilessly fleeced. Even if it has to bor-
row—though there is no need for that—the
State can command money at a cheaper rate
than anybody else, and can therefore fulfil
the functions either of landlord or of build-
ing society on better terms than anybody
else. So long, however, as the State has
a revenue from land, and especially so long
as it receives a penny from the sale of pub-
lic estate, there should be no lack of coin
for the purpose. It has been a scandal that
millions of land-sales money has been used
as ordinary revenue, and scattered in police-
man’s wages and suchlike things, for which
the country has nothing at all to show. This
present Government having resolved to choke
that scandal, every penny of money derived
from the sale of one lot of assets will for
the future have to be applied either to the
reduction of existing debt, or to the purchase
of other assets. And no better or more
proper use could be found for it than in con-
nection with the housing of the people. Ac-
cording to Statistician KNibbs, last year the
N.S.W. “revenue 7 from sales alone—exclu-
sive of rentals —was £944,164; and if for a
start even half that amount were, applied to
this purpose, 1000 or 1200 workmen’s homes
could be provided in a twelvemonth, and the
investment could thereafter be fairly relied
upon to pay 4 per cent, and a sinking fund.
But equally as important as the provision of
workmen’s homes for rental is the financing
of men to secure homes of their own. Al-
ready the State, through the Government
Savings Bank, assists men to secure farms
by offering them loans at low. interest with
easy terms of repayment; and a roof is just
about as necessary to the man who toils in
a city factory, or store, or office, as a farm
is to the farmer. In South Afiatralia, where
a State Bank has been at work for 15 years,
a man can borrow £5OO to build a house, and
by paying £3B 3s. 6d. a year—say 14s. , a
week—he can become the absolute owner in
20 years. What is done in Adelaide in that
way can certainly be done in Sydney. If
that part of the community which isn’t Fat
is not a lunatic of the worst kind, it will
only need to have the facts made plain
to rush the scheme—and the main fact at
present is. that it is easily possible to buy
a home within 20 years for a less payment
per week than the private landlord now ex-
torts by way of rent. If the borrower ac-
tually threw his bargain up at the end of,
say, 10 years, he would be in pocket ; but
there is no reason why he should throw it
up. Until a few years ago surrender values
were unknown in connection with life insur-
ance ; to-day they are universal. The same
principle might easily be applied, to this
other form of home-insurance or invest-
ment ; and that would wipe out the objec-
tion of the man who says that, though he
may be able to pay to-day, he doesn’t know
whether lie will be able to pay to-morrow,
and though lie may be here this week, his
work may take him somewhere else next
week. For, if he couldn’t pay to-morrow,
his surrender value would keep the roof
over his head, while if he was forced to
leave for some other district, he could draw
a modified surrender value in cash, and the
property, reverting to the State, would be
available for some other tenant on reduced
terms that would almost command a pur-
chaser.

The Gorged Town.
Victoria in 1901 contained 1,201,070 people,
of whom 496,079 were in Melbourne, and
704,991 were outside Melbourne. Roughly,
the proportions were: Melbourne, 41 per
cent. ; outside Melbourne, 59 per cent. In
1911, according to the census returns just
“provisionally” counted, Victoria contains
1,315,000 persons, of whom 591,830 are in
Melbourne, and 723,170 outside the swollen
city. The proportions now are: Melbourne,
45 per cent.; outside Melbourne, 55 per
cent. In 10 years Melbourne’s population
has increased by 95,751; and the population
of the rest of Victoria by only 18,179. Dur-
ing those 10 years some millions of pounds
have been spent by the State in a frantic en-
deavor to buy land for the people to settle
on; immigrants have been sought; and all
sorts of coddling has been given to “rural
interests.” Yet the country stands still, and
all its record of big harvests and high prices
has served only to make Melbourne bloat
with a dropsical plethora of people. There-
fore Victoria needs to stand five yards away
from itself, and, contemplating itself ear-
nestly with its strongest pair of spectacles,
ask: Why won’t people go and work in the
country? And then it will find the answer in
two facts. The first is that the squatters,
who gobbled the best of Victoria with an il-
licit appetite many years ago, have never dis-
gorged, and until the Labor party passed
the Commonwealth Land Tax, no one tried to


make them disgorge. Therefore the ma
who. during the past 10 years, lias thought
of cultivating the land, has been driven out
of Victoria because of the impossibility ot
finding cultivable land at a reasonable price,
or within reasonable reach of a market, ilie
second fact is that no man could find work
in the country unless (in most cases) he
would accept the housing of a dog, the work
of a slave, and the wages of a Hindoo. Ihe
Melbourne Argus, examining the causes ot
Melbourne’s bloat, finds them in the slack-
ening of country railway-building, and in the
jumping of city business because of rich har-
vests. But these are the least of the rea-
sons. Wherever a man goes, he goes be-
cause there is something to go after. If
he drops a line into the sea, it is not in a
spirit of scientific inquiry, but because he
expects to catch a fish. If he digs a hole
in the ground, he wants gold and not mun-
dic. And the reason why men haven’t gone
into the country in Victoria is because there
is so little to go there for. There is hardly
any land available which will keep a man
in comfortable existence, for the squatters
and tfie squires have got the best of it, and
will either not pass it on at all or only do it
at an extravagant price. And for the la-
borer or “hand,” country life is generally a
slovenly sort of slavery. Oust the squatter,
and provide a decent schedule of wages and
conditions for rural workers, and then Vic-
toria will have no further need to fret that
the belly swells while the rest of the body
stays lean.
Australia Must Look After Herself.
Dear Bulletin, —A friend —once a pro-
fessor in an Australian University—who
now resides in Switzerland, but Still has a
deep interest in the Commonwealth, writes
thus from Neuchatel: —
Imperial defence is all right on paper, but, will,
never be any good in practice. Recently I had occasion
to talk to a very high Continental military man
in respect to that matter. At first he would not rip
out, but on my assuring him that I would like to hear,
his candid view he gave it to me. Here it is:—»,
“You know England is detested on the whole
Continent. Attempts are made at times to popu-
larise her, but they don’t go down. One hears of
friendly speeches in Paris, in Berlin, in Vienna —-
they arrange ententes cordiales, etc., but they are
only on paper. The people—l mean the country—
won’t have the English at any price. King Edward
came round and tried all kinds of alliances, in the 1
vain hope that one or the others of these allies might
be induced to have a go at Germany. But did they?
No, of course not. They knew quite well that they’d
have to pay for it while England remained all right
across the Channel. And Germany kept on building
her ships. Now she has set Austria going, and
between the two they’ll get ahead of Great Britain.
For all I know they may be already up to her. It
is really nonsense to think that Great Britain could
keep up with them, considering that Austria and Ger-
many have about two and a-half times as many in-
habitants as Great Britain, The masses will tell in
the end.
“Besides, look at British society: a large number
of them titled foreigners! Then look at its navy:
It has ships ; but where are the men ? Germany, as well
as Austria, has the men and reserves for every ship.
They know where they are, and with their systems
of compulsory service they can get as manv more if
they care to send them along. The British helped
Spain against Napoleon. They stuck to Gibraltar.
They helped Turkey against the Russians, and stuck
to Cyprus. They assisted Egypt, and stuck to it.
There are dozens of other cases which may
be cited in support of my argument that Europe
would not turn a finger if Britain came to the end
of its tether. We suffer them on the Continent be-
cause they bring money, but we only suffer them.
As to the Britisher himself, he is arrogant and ignor-
ant. Owing* to his rotten system of secondary edu-
cation, a number of the Continental Universities
won’t allow students to matriculate on English cer-
tificates. Ignorance tells in war time—on the wrong
side, though.
“Look abroad. India is none too safe. The
colonics will find out in war time that they need not
expect any help from England. She wants every
gun to protect herself. The Australians are quite
right in looking to themselves as their best friends.
America, you say! The President isn’t 1 America.
There are millions and millions of Germans there,
and millions of others who wouldn’t support England
against Germany. The other way round —yes!
“Arbitration! It will prove the same as King
Edward’s alliances. They came to nothing. It was
merely another attempt to run in Germany, but she
simply said ‘No,’ and there ended that affair. She
knows she has the trump cards, and she is going to
play them. What cards, you ask me! Well, Ger-
many doesn’t want any war. She,' along with
Atutria, wants peace to expand her trade — and she
does it with a vengeance. Meantime the two coun-
tries are increasing their navy and army with all
the speed they can.
“Tne end ? Germany will soon be in a position to
stop all oposition. Indeed, she could do it now. This
is the secret of the Emperor William’s love for
peace, for peace gives him the chance to put his
nets round Austria and to build a navy which need
not fear to meet Great Britain on the seas. ‘Brit-
annia rules the waves’ is merely a saying now. She
is winded, and the refusal to agree to arbitration, re-
ceived recently from Germany, shows this only too
well.”
So much for the military man. I tried to argue
against his views—but my replies did not strike
home, because he is right. Britishers have no friends
on the Continent, and as to Britain’s chance against
Germany and Austria, I would not care to back her
in cash, though if cash would do it I’d cheerfully
put my hand in my pocket. What this man said I
hear daily from others. I travel a great deal and
meet many.
If you can do it, back Australia’s navy and army.
In these two lies Australia’s only salvation. The
ship that is not in Australian waters will be of no use
to her when the struggle’ comes. Great Britain will
not be able to help her —she’ll want help herself.
For this reason I am glad that Labor men have gone
to England. They cannot be so easily caught with a
title as to overlook Australian interests.
That is the message of a man who, besides
having no anti-British prejudices, left Aus-
tralia with a deep affection for it and its
people-—a. man, moreover, of a singularly
judicial temperament, who lives where he
can not only collect evidence, but come to a
cold judgment upon it.—Yours, C.R.W.
Government by Humbug.
Between them N.S.W. Acting-Premier Hol-
man and Works Minister Griffith last
week managed to throw some more mud on
the party-Government system. Griffith
understood that his colleagues approved of
free ferries; and personally Griffith didn’t,
though he added that, of course, he would
carry out the will of the majority. There-
upon Holman burst out into a long state-
ment to the effect that a Minister’s duty
was to carry out a Government’s policy, and
not to criticise it, and if Griffith wasn’t
prepared to accept that dictum, his place
was outside the Cabinet. Prom his natur-
ally-Conservative point of view, Holman
was probably right; but it is an absurd point
of view all the same. No ten or twelve
intelligent men can be expected to have the
same opinions on every detail of a policy ;
and if they pretend to, they are merely act-
ing the hypocrite. Griffith evidently as-
sumed that he was talking to intelligent
men, who wouldn’t expect any 10 or 12 other
men to be mere machine-made fac-similes of
one another; and he didn’t pretend to be a
fac-simile of the rest. So far from discredit-
ing the Ministry, this attitude distinctly
strengthened it in public estimation. It
stripped off a lot of humbug and make-
believe, and frankly represented the Govern-
ment as consisting of intelligent entities,
each with a brain of its own, each man
throwing his individual opinion into the
common stock, each man urging whatever
view he judged from his own personal ex-
perience and reflection to be right, and yet
accepting the verdict of the majority in the
end, a'nd loyally carrying it out. If pre-
cedent be against Griffith, and with Hol-
man, so much the worse for precedent and
Holman, and so much the worse also for
the party which allows precedent and Hol-
man to lead it by the nose into sham and
dissimulation.
From the Dark Ages.
“If a man has committed a fraud, let him remain
in gaol an indefinite period. Let him remain there
at work until the heads of a special Board appointed
should tell him that he had done a fair thing, .that
he had worked for, say, four years, and had earned
by his industry the sum of £lOO or £l5O towards
wiping out the effect of his crime. Now he may go.”
A voice: Would that do away with the sentence
of the Judge?
“In most cases I should be in favor of an indeter-
minate sentence, to which, perhaps, a maximum
limit could be applied by the Judge.”— Holman,
2/6/’ll.
“I am in favor of sending a man to gaol who com-
mits adultery with a woman living with her hus-
band.”—-Holman, 3/6/’ll.
A born reactionary is YV. A. Holman, of
N.S.W., and the lurther he goes the more
anomalous he appears as head of an alleg-
edly-progressive administration. The idea of
forcing a thief to return an eye for an eye
to the person he has despoiled, goes back to
the troglodyte period. In those days pilfering
Was an offence against the individual alone;
and the latter was at liberty, if he felt
Wronged, to seize his dub and take it out
of the robber’s hide in the event of the rob-
ber having nothing worth distraining on in
ills cave. The law of retaliation was fol-
lowed by that of expiation. At a later period
the thief was compelled, by personal service
or btherwise, to repay the citizen he had de-
frauded. The modern world has given these
crude old methods the go-by, and crime is
now regarded, as it should be, as an offence
against the community. In any case the
Holman scheme of expiation is demon-
strably impracticable. A criminal bricklayer
would take years to amass a sum which an
equally guilty playwright might earn in a
few weeks. Briefly, penal institutions are
not designed to wring from their inmates
dividends for the benefit of folks whose pro-
perty the police have failed to protect; and
the Government which sets out to fix the
duration of sentences by the convict’s abil-
ity to earn money must stultify itself 150
times a day. Equally retrograde is the
notion of making adultery a criminal
offence. It is nearly as old as the scheme
of forcing a thief to stay in gaol till he has
worked off the amount of his theft, and con-
siderably more discredited. In the most
licentious days of the Roman Empire laws
were promulgated which visited adultery
with “the vengeance of the sword and pun-
ishments of the utmost severity.” Yet Dio
Cassius noted that in Rome alone, shortly
after the law of Septimus Severus, there
were 3000 charges of adultery. The gaols
of fifteenth century England were full of
adulterers. Stephen, in his “History of
the Criminal Law of England,” mentions
the case of Thomas Cotton and Dorothy
Thornton, who “spent four years in Staf-
ford Gaol in great misery’*’ for this offence.
The whole case for proceeding criminally
against a man >on such a ,score is based on
the ancient and exploded theory that the
wife is the husband’s chattel, like his dog or' >
his umbrella—a fact which is driven home
by the circumstance that gaol is never advo-
cated for men who, ruin women under a pro-
mise of marriage, or, who. take away other
men’s sweethearts—deeds which are as
anti-social on the one hand, and as exas-
perating to the wronged party on the other,
as any adultery. If the N.S.W. Government
feels it simply must manufacture a new crime,
and incidentally fill the prisons to overflow-
ing, it would do better to prescribe gaol for
those who infect others with venereal dis-
ease. More misery is caused, both to the
individual and the community, by this prac-
tice than by any offence in the Crimes Act.

A Cold Country Sample of Sosh.
That Commission which is inquiring as
to ways and means of carting Mel-
bourne about speedily and effectively,
has just received a report,. drawn up by the
manager of Manchester’s electric system,
which practically answers all the conun-
drums that the Commission was set to an-
swer, besides offering a powerful hint as to
the advantage of municipal over private own-
ership. Manchester, in 1895, decided to buy out
the then existing company, though it was
admittedly pretty good as companies go.
Since that was done the city has spent nearly
£2,000,000 on the repair of old and the con-
struction of new lines. Against that outlay,
there is now a sinking fund amounting to
£284,511, while the renewal and depreciation
fund has reached £422,200. The governing
policy laid down when the council took
charge was (1) to benefit the travelling pub-
lic by providing better and cheaper transit
facilities; (2) to benefit the ratepayers
through contributions out of profits; (3) to
benefit the employees by granting better con-
ditions of service. And this is how Man-
chester’s council has done it: —
(1) The public has been given increased travel-
ling facilities without regard to the mere earning
of profits; wherefore, though passengers increased
from 23 millions in 1902 to 159 millions in 1910,
the miles. of track rose in a still larger measure —-
from 20 to 181 —-and tile car mileage from
nearly two millions to 17 millions. Also the fares,
ranging from -Jd. for three-quarters of a mile and
Id. for two miles to sd. for 9 i miles, have been re-
duced an average of 40 per cent, below those charged
by the old private eo. Were those old fares in force
now, the public would be paying £500,000 per
annum more than it does for the same amount of
service.
(2) The payments out of profits towards relieving
the city rates vary from £20,000 in 1902 to
£75,000 last year, and the payments have already
aggregated £527,000. Besides all this, the Tram-
way Department pays rates ,on its track and build-
ings amounting to £34,000 a year, as compared with
£BOOO a year paid by the company.
(3) The employees’ hours have been reduced from
70 to 54, and their wages raised. Guards get 68
per cent, more and drivers 47 per cent, more than
the company paid.
As to the wages, the nominal increase ap-
pears to be not quite so much as that; but
as the municipality provides free uniforms,
free rides to and from work, one week’s holi-
day on full pay, and half-pay during sickness
for one month, and a variable amount after-
wards, the men are in reality 68 and 47 per
cent, better off respectively. Further, as
other concessions became possible, the muni-
cipality instituted reduced fares for school-
children, reduced long-distance fares, and
free travelling for blind persons; and,
finally, to supply an obvious public demand,
it introduced a parcels delivery system which,
though only a few years old, already gives
a profit of £2500 per annum. All these
things Manchester has done beyond the pos-
sibility of denial; and seeing that it pos-
sesses 181 miles of track to Melbourne’s 37,
and has a population of 800,000 as against
Melbourne’s 500,000, it looks as if the Mel-
bourne. Commission need look no further than
England’s Cotton centre for all the guidance
it requires on the question of street traffic.

Pioneers of “Wet” Farming.
“J.E.W” describes the beginnings of El-
wood Mead’s “wet” farms in Northern Vic-
toria :
Settlers are dribbling into the holdings along the
Waranga-Mallee channebvays around Rochescer, es-
pecially to the east. The class of men may be
stamped as medium in most things, but superlatively
earnest. They may lack the stamina of the earlier
pioneers, but the set purpose is there. They mean
business, and what is more, most of them have saved
money under Cold Land conditions, which would
make the average- Australian swear luxuriantly.
They are clear-faced and clear-headed and optimistic.
Much good land has been allotted to such men along
the Waranga Channel, and adjacent to the irriga-
tion sucker-channels, while not far away, and near
enough to be irrigated for the greater part when
required, lies the Cornelia Creek estate, recently
Sliced. This is now carrying 82 settlers and their
dependents, as against the one homestead previously
occupied by old dead-and-gone George Simmie,
M.L.C.
Judging by the results on the Restdown and Wyuna
settlements adjacent, the “dry” farmers on Cornelia
Creek blocks should do well if the seasons continue
propitious. Some of the blocks on the erstwhile
Simmie estate (which has never been cultivated to
any extent worth mentioning) are reputed to be third-
rate land, merely fit for grazing, and poor at that.
But one never can tell. One of the Restdown blocks
went begging; no one would touch it. After a time
a local lad and his sister applied for it and got it
dirt cheap on 31 years’ terms. Behold! The first
year these two took several thousand bags of wheat
from their block, and realised enough to pay off
the whole balance of purchase money. Over 200
would-be settlers applied for the 82 blocks on Cor-
nelia Creek. They showed comfortable bank bal-
ances in almost every instance—in fact, the Chair-
man of the Land Board vowed that never had there
been such an affluent battalion of land-hungry appli-
cants in his experience in the State. Baulked in
their desires to get land at Cornelia Creek, many
are now applying for the smaller “wet” farming
blocks along the channel-ways, especially to the east
of Campaspe.
Excellent though the eastern blocks are at Nan-
nulla, perhaps the pick of the irrigation blocks are
at Bamawm, eight miles and more to the west of
the Campaspe. Vine-growing, citrus fruit raising,
and various branches of intense culture, as well as
dairying on scientific lines, may be practised with
all chances of success. A township is being sur-
vevod amidst the blocks, and land-sharks are already
sniffing round.
One thing stands to the Murray Government’s dis-
credit, and that is the lack of housing accommo-
dation on the holdings. Excepting for the farm
homesteads on the sliced* areas, there are no houses.
Hence the settlers are having their first taste of
the simple life in Australia even as the Dioneers did
of old. It will not hurt them. Tent life is
healthy—and cosy, if proporlv catered for. Of
course there are many little Cold-Country ways that
the newcomers will have to forget ere they become
properly acclimatised. The Cold-Country way of
killing pigs at home, for instance, ■ and carting the
dead carcase to probable- purchasers. Dead
swine are not welcome wares in the height of sum-
mer. But, in one matter, at any rate, the settlers
may give a lesson to the Australians. Each pays
for his own drink. They don’t “shout.”
There is- hardly a newcomer who hasn’t money to
tide him- over a bad year or two. Some have been
tenant farmers in the Cold Land. Englishmen pre-
dominate. Irish families ; (the sturdiest-looking of
the lot) are dribbling in, with a Scotch heart or two.
Yankees are few to date, and inexperienced. A
number of new-chum would-be settlers who arrived
last year have taken billets with district farmers to
get experience.
To-day the promises for the season are ideal.
Grass has been growing so that you could see it mov-
ing, thanks to the heavy unseasonable February rains.
Thus a rattling dairying season was assured. No
irrigation will be required till the height >of next
summer, at least. With so good a start, the suc-
cesses of Cohuna and Lancaster settlements, where
modest fortunes are being made on holdings of 20
acres, are likely to be repeated in the new ventures.
On the Bamawm rich lands a second Mildura is ex-
pected to rise, and Rochester promises to be a city
of importance.
It is significant that it is an ex-Australian
Governor of Bombay who has ordered the
local Turf Club to limit the number of its
racing days, and substitute the totalisator
for the “bookmaking evil.” This he does
with the purpose of diminishing gambling
among the white military and officials, and
the colored “toffs” of the Presidency. As
for the colored masses, the new gambling
edict doesn’t practically apply, seeeing that
they have no money to lose. Now, Governor
Sydenham Clarke gained his knowledge
of turf-betting in Victoria at a time when
the mitigated evil of the Tote was under
warm discussion, and when the S. Aus.
Commissioner of Police had issued a report
as to the decrease in the number of em-
bezzlements and thefts in that State since
the Tote was legalised, and bookmakers
were warned off. He saw the Tote at work
in S. Aus., and he heard the roar of the
leather-lunged parasites of racing in Vic-
toria and N. S. Wales, where, by the grace
of ear-bitten legislators, they still lay the
odds. And his experience of the two sys-
tems of gambling has led him to legalise
the machine and illegalise Ikf.y in Bombay
—for the nood of the community. Are the
Wowsers listening? And are the legisla-
tors?
—
The Orange Pup in Melbourne has been
yapping again, and, though it has as usual
yapped up the wrong tree, this time there was
something to yap at, if the terrier had only
known where to find it. Brother Snow-
ball, M.L.A., told a meeting of Protestant
Alliancers that the Customs Department re-
fused to let the “leading Nationalist paper”
from Home —good old Rome —be landed in
the Commonwealth, which astounding state-
ment was referred to Minister Tudor for
verification. Tudor explained that the
G.P.O. had once lodged a complaint against
an Italian paper—the Asino —on the ground
that its matter was “blasphemous and inde-
cent” ; but when a cony, stopped at the Cus-
toms, was inspected at the head office, no-
thing “officially objectionable” was found in
it, wherefore no hostile action was taken.
That ended the matter, so far as the Cus-
toms was concerned. But the P.M.G. ad-
mitted that an Italian publication, of which
he professed to be profoundly ignorant, had
been prohibited from passing through the
post. In short, the Postal Department
pleads guilty to putting its veto on a paper
which, according to the Customs Depart-
ment, was “officially unobjectionable” in the
only copy that the latter Department ever
scrutinised. So it remains with the P.M.G.
to show where the “indecency” or “blas-
phemy” of the vetoed Italian newspaper
comes in. In this free-thinking country the
fiery Yarra-banlc and Sydney Domain'ora-
tors are at liberty to say the rudest things
about the Almighty, and can mock at Re-
ligion and its various ministers until further
orders. Even grave agnostics are apt to
turn an honest penny by demonstrating the
futility of holiness at lecture halls on Plea-
sant Sunday afternoons. Therefore it is
certainly up to the Commonwealth P.M.G.
to account for his Department’s seizure of a
“blasphemous” newspaper, the more especially
as it was “blasphemous” in Italian, and the
ungodly language had to be explained to the
P.M.G. by an interpreter. A terrible lot of
blasphemy has been warbled by Italian
opera singers in Australian theatres at one
time or other, but the audience put a harm-
less interpretation on the words, and the re-
sult was a double encore in many cases.
Bearing this fact in mind, one would like
to know who supplied the Postmaster-Gen-
eral with the blasphemous interpretation,
and whether the interpreter’s views were
subseouently confirmed by a duly-qualified
irreligious person.
A big deputation from the Australian Cham-
bers of manufactures, which interviewed
Acting-Prime Minister Hughes last week,
got a few unpleasant truths poured into its
ear. One of the things it asked for was
a prohibition of the admission of the drugs,
etc., that contribute to race suicide. “Just
so,” replied Hughes, in effect ; “this Gov-
ernment in in complete accord with you
there. _ But so far the sole effect of such
prohibitions has been to stimulate the manu-
facture of the articles in Australia. It is
the same all round. We stopped the im-
portation of a certain alcoholic liquor in
bottles bearing labels saying it was of dis-
tinct medicinal benefit. We even confis-
cated the labels. The merchants imported
it in bulk, got labels printed here, and dis-
tributed the goods broadcast. Again, we
stopped certain pills imported under a mis-
leading label. The importers now comply
with the Commerce Act, but put the labels
on here and sell them as before. It is en-
couraging, isn’t it? We absolutely pro-
hibited the introduction of a certain article
because it was alleged to be a cure for al-
most every ailment: it is still sold every-
where, and as it isn’t imported it must be
manufactured here. Again, we blocked olive
oil which was adulterated with rapeseed.
peanut, and other cheap oils; now the mer-
chants get it in bulk, pure, and adulterate
it themselves. _ It. is the same with mus-
tard, lard, limejuice, turpentine, pepper,
neatsfoot oil, and a dozen other articles. We
would keep these substances pure, but we
have not the power—and when we asked
for more power a month or two ago, the
Chambers of Manufactures decided that we
shouldn’t have it.” Probably the manu-
facturers made a mental note to vote differ-
ently when those questions are put to them
again. If they didn’t, their requests look
a good deal like bunkum and hypocrisy.

The Helot’s Cur.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A casual laborer on the wharves recently died in
the Melbourne Hospital from hunger and exposure,
and the Lost Dogs’ Home saved from a vagrant life
a fox terrier which had excited compassion by its
eager quest of the dying man, its master. Now a
lady of Adelaide has written offering to provide a
home for the terrier if they will send it to Ade-
laide by the express at her expense, and attach a
docket to the dog, recounting its history. No doubt
if thfr terrier has not yet been given a comfortable
kennel it will find its Canaan in Adelaide.— Argus.
In hospice bed they laid the Son of Sorrow,
A slave no more to be,
For when the world was flusht with dawning
morrow,
The Helot’s soul was free.
Yes here, in open day, a son of Labor,
If honest truth be said,
While Life went by with merry lute and
tabor,
Passed out for want of bread.
But stay! No hasty word of blame unkindly
Must pass our lips to-day ;
For though men sinned, perchance they acted
blindly,
In hapless human way.
And though the Lab’rer all untimely per-
ished,
To Hunger fierce a prey,
Yet, in a cosy crib, securely cherished,
His dog is safe to-day!
And should you ask us—“ Say, is this the
fashion
Wherein your toilers end?”
We yet can boast —“We do not lack compas-
sion ;
His terrier’s found a friend !”
“Was he not sent with short descriptive
docket
To some sweet, gentle soul,
Who found the cash within her kindly pocket
To'meet the canine dole?”
And though men’s record in the case before
us
Is dark with blot and blur.
Yet we can chant in sweet forgiving chorus —
ü ßehold, they saved the cyr!”

NOTICE TO AGENTS.
Owing to Coronation Day falling
on Thursday, 22nd inst., “ The
Bulletin ” for that date will be
published on Wednesday, 21st.
Felton, old “Jimmy” Tyson’s favorite
property, and his home so far as he ever had
any one in particular, is to be auctioned in
farms at Toowoomba (Q.) on June 29. Ty-
son, having a few thousands burning his
pockets, looked over the Darling Downs close
on 40 years ago, picked Felton as the place
he wanted to buy, and set his wits to work
to get it. The results he won from it made
him fonder of it than ever. The subdi-
viders are offering remarkably liberal terms :
the purchaser of a block pays 15 per cent,
down, and then he needn’t pay any more
principal for 10 years.
James Ashcroft, Wellington’s (M.L.) old
coroner and official assignee in bankruptcy, who
died there not long ago, always had a soft spot in
a big heart for the man who “filed his shovel,”
holding strongly to the opinion that the default
to pay was generally due to anything but ras-
cality. He was continually impressing this view
upon creditors, and many a bankrupt had to
thank him for totally unhoped for conditions.
One time he was examining a country carter who
stated that the main cause of his bankruptcy was
the loss within a short period of four horses
valued af £4O or £5O each. Turning benignly on
the creditors, the assignee said : “ Well, gentle-
men, you know it's hard enough to work off one
dead horse, let alone four.” The meeting agreed
that it was, and that’s the last the carter heard of
his indebtedness.
The youths whom the Australian Military
College will train up in the way a soldier ought
to go are mostly a fine lot of young Australians.
The giant of the present crowd is a Claremont
(W.A.) youth, by name Selby. Though only 17,
he stands 6ft. 3in. in his socks, goes 40in. round
the chest, and is built in proportion. George
Capes, a Victorian youth, runs him close, being
6ft. 2in. high, 38in. round the chest, and 18 years
of age. College-made officers in older countries
frequently turn out snobs, but these youths don’t
threaten to shape that way. The post of first
Cadet Captain falls to E. H, Biden, a brainy lad
from the Ma State. ,
