ously the Indeterminate Sentences Board is
urging the Government to set apart 200
acres of the Werribee Park estate and build
a reformatory thereon. The Indetermin-
ate Sentences law came into force nearly
three years ago, in July, 1908, and already
some 40 persons have been sentenced under
it —and that is all. The whole essence of
the Indeterminate Sentence policy was re-
form —the correction and training of youth-
ful first-offenders, and the regeneration,
where possible, of habituals. But as no
provision has been made for the regenera-
tion, the Act has become a dead letter. Now
the usual foreign expert is to be brought to
the rescue. Fifty experts cannot, how-
ever, produce the reforms, which cost
money. All they can do is to tell the Go-
vernment what it ought to do. But Parlia-
ment has already done that; it did it when
it passed the Indeterminate Sentences law.
The whole trouble is that the Parliament’s
instructions have been allowed to slide. The
Weary Government wants a scheme that
won’t cost any money, or any work —and
there isn’t any such scheme. So, until the
Administration is changed, it is useless to
import a man to propound schemes. Vic-
toria doesn’t want him anyhow; what it
does want is a Government that isn’t too
lazy to do the bullocking work which is
necessary to give effect to the schemes that
have been propounded and authorised
already.
+
The black-and-white ants that infest law
courts and “chambers” ate a hole through
the bottom of another piece of Wade’s “Re-
form” legislation the other day. In the
late Government’s Act dealing with news-
papers and libel, it was provided that a per-
son who considered himself aggrieved might
come along and demand of a newspaper the
name of the writer of the allegedly-offensive
article. One or two such applications had
been side-stepped on one ground or another;
but at last Justice Ferguson had to
say something definite on the subject. And
what Justice Ferguson did say was that the
Act didn’t oblige the Court to order the
newspaper to let the cat out of the bag;
therefore, the matter being left to the discre-
tion of the Court, the Court wouldn’t order
the cat to be let loose unless the other party
showed that he would be prejudiced in his
action if he couldn’t see the cat. And as
the application to view the cat is, in 99 cases
out of 100, a mere fishing expedition, there
isn’t much left of that particular provision
of Wade’s reform.
♦
Within the last week or so the jumpy sub-
ject of ramshackle boilers has been shot into
the -Ma State’s eye by two explosions and a
letter from the Engine Drivers and Firemen’s
Association, pointing out that there are quite
a lot of steam bombs installed in busy cen-
tres. Anyone requiring a boiler may buy
any old second-hand thing and work it at
any pressure he likes to risk; so that when
a boiler is condemned and shot out of one
shop, a more frugal soul usually buys it and
puts it into commission somewhere else, with
out the Law raising as much as an eyelid.
Mine boilers have to be tested periodically
by competent men, under Government au-
thority ; but the little manufacturer in the
back iane, or the farmer in the backblocks,
is allowed to threaten life and Proputty
with a shaking cylinder of rust until his
nerve gives way, or his engine-driver waits
on him with his hair standing on end, and
reports that Sheol is imminent. If Bruce
Smith really wants to see an Anarchist in
the' flesh, he might call round on one of these
boiler-owners some day when steam is up.
But it would do far more good if an in-
spector called round with a new Land
Boilers Inspection Act.
Most ludicrous is the false pathos with
which the Anti-Labor daily seeks to stir up
sympathy on behalf of the absentee Austra-
lian, unjustly smitten, so it is claimed, by
the Federal Government’s land-tax. For
instance
Parliament has struck a blow at all capital owned
outside of Australia. Now, even if it be granted
that an Australian, after, perhaps, many years of
hard pioneering work in the Commonwealth, is not
fairly entitled, having made his money, to live
where he chooses, this certainly does, etc.
It suggests the flat, bald query: How many
Australians, who have achieved fortunes in
this country by pioneering, settle down
abroad ? On all the available evidence the
answer is: None! It is not the Abbey
Dangars, Horsfalls and Horderns, of
N.S/W - ., nor the original Clarkes, Buck-
leys and Manifolds, of Victoria, nor the
Barr-Smiths, of South Australia, who take
up their permanent abode in the United
Kingdom as soon as they amass sufficient
money. In practically every case the con-
firmed absentees are the non-working
descendants of old-time land-snatcliers
or rum-vendors, euphemistically known as
“pioneers”—the Coopers, of N.S.W., the
Wilsons, of Victoria, and so on. A cer-
tain amount of more or less legiti-
mate pathos might be squeezed by a skilful
hand out of the case of an industrious
ancient mercilessly taxed by a tyrannous
Government, because he elected to pass his
evening elsewhere than in the Common-
wealth. But to apply the gag to the oldster’s
descendants, many of whom have never seen
Australia, or done a vestige of pioneering
anywhere, is trenching closely on the
idiotic.

Although “The Dollar Princess” is still
declaring dividends at the Sydney Royal, the
managing directors have decided to with-
draw the script on Friday, and -on Satur-
day “The Balkan Princess” will be floated on
the exchange of public opinion. Judged by
the prospectus, the new Princess will be
worth early investigation.
i at * *
If a solvent Sydneyite has not visited
“Jack and the Beanstalk” there is a prima-
facie case against him of being an incorri-
gible Wowser. The show has not many more
nights to run at Her Majesty’s, and citizens
who desire to evade a humiliating slur must
therefore take speedy action.

of the advt., “it is gay”—and surely the
advt. should know.

Sydney Adelphi continues to do fair busi-

thriller, “Driving a Girl to Destruction,” is
almost ready for its run; but the starting
date had not been announced up to the time
this Bulletin dived through the presses in
search of its pink overcoat.

The musical Columbia Comedy Trio and
the juggling Kavanagh Boys are heading
the hill at Sydney Tivoli. Fanny Powers
came back last week with a few new imita-
tions, and brought the Australian Dartos
with her; the advertisement respectfully
mentions both turns in capital letters. Other
performers still available are Eunice and
Zena (girl instrumentalists), Blanche Char-
meroy (of Paris and the piano), and Ted
Kalman. The introduction and finale to the
first part are still being performed with great
solemnity, the ladies responsible cleverly
managing to keep the audience from over-
hearing the words of the ancient ritual.

On Monday, the 12th, the programme at
Sydney Lyric will be strengthened by the
Vitagraph Co.’s representation of Dick-
ens’s “Tale of Two Cities”—the thrilling
story of the French Revolution. This great
production, among the best of the world’s
photo-plays so far produced, will be shown
in addition to the ordinary programme with-
out any increase in the price of admission.
** * *
The star item this week at Sydney Lyceum
is the “Life and Death of Robert Emmett.”
Robert was a fine figure of a man, and the
dress of the period was evidently designed
by someone who had a good leg, or thought
he had. The feature next week will be a
“Trip to Hobart,” showing the beauty spots
of the Apple Island and all the best places to
visit. Dickens’s “Tale of Two Cities” will
also be shown.
** * *
Sousa’s band at the Glaciarium is mak-
ing large audiences swing in rhythm to its
bracing marches. Occasionally Wagner,
Weber and Tschaikowsky are introduced for
the good of the higher musical soul, but
Weber’s “Invitation h la Valse” and the
“Lohengrin” fantasia do not find the re-
sponse that follows the stirring “Stars and
Stripes,” or “Washington Post,” or even
that embroidered futility, the “Hobomoko”
march. Among the soloists, Herbert Clarke
has been blowing some radiant notes from
his treasured cornet, and Miss Zedeler left
the audience in frantic admiration for her
violin melodies. The soprano voice of Miss
Root was rather lost in an immense glorified
crypt like the Glaciarium, though her sing-
ing of “Annie Laurie” gave her an immedi-
ate hold on the affections of the crowd. As
for Sousa himself, it is hard to tell whether
the veneration for the man is not aroused
by the conspicuous cleverness of his methods.
Anyhow, there is no doubt about the goods
he delivers.
sis * * *
In “Our Miss Gibbs,” at Her Majesty’s,
the very English smile of Miss Blanche
Browne—a smile crowded from end to end
with pearls—gives no evidence of diminish-
ing, although she has been scattering it in
the same opera in Australia and elsewhere
ever since Miss Gibbs first went into busi-
ness. Melbourne has settled down to a pro-
longed indulgence in Gibbs, which is a kind
of habit, like opium-eating or sipping gin.
** * *
Melbourne is understood to be taking the
useful moral of “The Woman in the Case”
seriously to heart, and the Wowsers have
not felt .called upon to unduly exert them-
selves since the Fitch play went on. Men
perceive that the lesson taught is the advisa-
bility of avoiding sirens with hair like a
flaming lucifer, and are carefully searching
for this particular tint in rich auburns,
with the deliberate intention of abjuring it.
So far the hunt for the missing hair is un-
availing. Really, as a fascinator, Claire
Forster does not strike Melbourne as likely
to satisfy a cultivated taste. She might be
a success in a private bar frequented by
jockey boys and shilling bookmakers, but
scarcely anywhere else. However, in an im-
personal way, the appeal of a thoroughly
bad woman in a remarkably good gown is
practically unlimited, and Royal audiences
are keeping the house warm these chill even-
ings.
** * «
With “A Woman of Pleasure” looming as
next Saturday’s attraction at the Melbourne
King’s, this journal lias only to dismiss “The
Executioner’s Daughter” with a few well-
chosen words of regret, spoken on behalf of
the students of the foolish in drama, who
will be sorry to lose this tumbled creation of
liill-Mitchelson’s brain. Quite apart from
its unbridled fancy, the play presents in
Felice Roche one of those grand villainesses
whose faces are paintings, and whose pasts
are problems. Felice is capable of any-
thing. Her crimes range from arson to at-
tempted murder, and her moderate faults em-
brace lying, deceit and petty larceny. As
an artist in sin she is at the top of her class ;
even Satan might take lessons from her with
advantage to himself and his many disciples.
* * *
Reginald Wykeham is fairly established
in that numerous and rapidly increasing
class, the theatrical managers of Australia.
His initial venture at Melbourne Princess’s
encourages him to go further, in the hope
of faring no worse. “Seven Days” has
drawn well, if not amazingly. Maud Chet-
wynd, as _ the young wife galvanised by a
solitary nip, is a remarkable enlargement of
the gossamer Maud who not so very long
ago frisked with the Royal Comics. She is
now quite a good all-round actress—any-
how, she’s an all-round actress. Her version
of a conventional young middle-class matron
infused with the spirit of a Martini cocktail
is commended to Willie Judkins and those
whom it may concern as a harmless and
edifying jag. All “Seven Days” wants to
round it off is a concerted effort on the part
of the famished household to bake Officer
Flannigan for lunch. The comedy run ter-
minates prematurely on Friday night. On
Saturday night Harry Plimmer and Mrs.
Brough give events at the Princess’s a
serious turn with “Nobody’s Daughter.”
** * *
This week’s programme at the Opera
House is twin-brother to last week’s bill.
R ; A. Roberts is still “Ringing the Changes”
with the aid of a dummy and several shifts
of. clothes. _ The two Lillies repeat their
mirror illusion, and Godfrey James continues
to raise his voice two or three miles above
his hat. Also, Peggy Pryde is the same old
Peggy, whose whimsical mouth still flouts the
early-closing legislation.
m * *
“Henry VIII.” has been running at West’s
Building, Melbourne, pursued by an enter-
taining bunch of other films. Among the
latter were some interesting scenes in Al-
giers, and a lively drama of Yankee extrac-
tion. Crowding into this picture are des-
peradoes, gamblers, and an “Injun,” with an
overpowering crop of virtue and lank, oiled
hair. The Red, it is seen, is the hero of
the piece, and, sure enough, he dies in the
melee to show his regard for a kindly boss.
** * *
The Prahran Lyric (Vic.) is offering
varied fare. In addition to the usual com-
edy and travel subjects, the Lyric people
show weekly editions of “Pathe’s Gazette,”
French and Italian dramas in color, and pic-
tures of star acrobatic turns, as well as the
instructive industrial films. Each Friday
evening, also, grand opera is screened, with
suitable music supplied by the band. This
week the Friday feature is Gounod’s “Faust,”
and next week there will appear a pictorial
veision of “I Pagliacci.”

In particular, these modern American dram-

some and incredibly supple, continue their
feats on the pillar, and Reichen’s clever dogs

AT POVERTY POINT.
“0.K.”: It is quite reasonable for musical en-
thusiasts to argue that the visible presence of Sousa
is distracting to those who would concentrate their
minds on the tumult of Sousa’s Band. They can
escape the distraction only by closing their eyes
or turning their backs, which latter alternative
might entail worse distractions. And why is this
thus? Because custom demands that the conductors
of concert orchestras shall display themselves to the
audience like conductors at theatres, whose presence
in the'midst of the instrumentalists and in front of
the singers is indispensable. Yet, the concert-
orchestra conductor were better placed in a signal-
box at either corner of the platform, waving his in-
structions to performers ranged on the bias, so to
speak. A view of Sousa and his movements is un-
necessary to a critical enjoyment of his band’s
achievement. At the theatre we don’t demand a view
of the stage-manager and ballet-mistress directing a
fine spectacular performance which owes all the
charm of its effect to their rehearsals. “Open your
ears and shut your eyes, and hear, what Sousa will
send you,” would be the Press’s exhortation to the
public, if the public were listening for novelties in
concerted music sounds. But the public is just look-
ing for Sousa, the creator of the band, and is fed
with personal paragraphs accordingly.
Bland Holt and suite are a-wing for Australia
again. The betting is 10 to 1 that Bland has seen
things which be feels impelled to confer on Anti-
podean audienpes across the fierce, white footlights.
“Bowbell”: Gilbert’s talent—or genius, if you will
■ —had its limits. He could not put an ounce of
genuine human feeling in his writing, and never
touched the heart at all. He was somewhat eminent
as a Greek scholar, and perhaps that made him too
metallic. His numerous and excellently-written
comedies had no spark of the touch of nature that
makes the whole world kin, except when he borrowed
it from George Eliot, with his Dan’l Druce. His
best work was in pieces that failed through this lack
of humanity. Credit him, on the other hand, with a
noble and invariable cleanness, like that of Scott,
Dickens and Thackeray. He could not pen an evil
line, although a few of his conceits might make
ladies squirm a little, as when the Arcadian, in
“lolanthe,” is half man and half fairy. As a pro-
ducer, he had all the knack of his model, Tom Rob-
ertson. The run of the Gilbert and Sullivan operas
would never have started but for Gilbert’s “pro-
ducing,” his inexorable and arbitrary stage-manage-
ment. What looked like impossibility and fatuity
must be done under his unerring judgment. The
trinity of Gilbert, Sullivan and Carte was matchless.
Mrs. Carte, known professionally as Miss Lenoir
(maiden name, Miss Black), was invaluable, too;
she stood next to Gilbert in the producing. Gros-
smith was the acting backbone, but Gilbert drilled
him to the finest point, allowing him no initiative.
He was implacable. At the premiere of the “Mikado”
Mrs. . Carte said to Gilbert: “Wonderful! Barring-
ton is singing in tune.” Gilbert: “Only the ner-
vousness of a first night.”
H. HENNINGHAM,
MUSICAL
DIRECTOR
FOR “SEVEN
DAYS,” AT

“Busk”: Reviewing Gilbert and Sullivan in Aus-
tralia, the first mention must be given to Vernon.
Above all shone his Bunthorne and Ko-Ko,
though his Lord Chancellor and Major-General Stan-
ley. were good. Vernon brought Ko-Ko from
Japan. Verdi’s Grosvenor, in “Patience,” was
happy. No performer who saw him could
get out of an imitation. AndrSe Navaro’s Lady
Jane was powerful—“ Sing hey, Gooday!”—and Celia
Ghiloni was not far behind. But of all the ladies
Nellie Stewart, in “Princess Ida,” was best. Alice
Rees was the Patience in all innocence and sim-
plicity. Bob Brough’s Lord Chancellor was match-
less, and Thornton’s almost equalled Vernon’s.
Ryley shone as the Gondolier. Riccardi is not for-
gotten as Sir Joseph Porter, and Lauri’s work in
that was his best bit of Gilbert. Finally, Vernon’s
Grand Inquisitor, in “The Gondoliers,” flashes across
my mind.
The late W. S. Gilbert was guilty of one dramatic
criticism. Asked what he thought of a great actor’s
“Hamlet,” he said: “Splendid performance. Funny,
without being vulgar.”
“Petramos”: We had sauntered in from Poverty
Point to a Bourke-street bar on the day Henry
Irving’s death was announced. Sorrowfully address-
ing his fellow-“artists,” the hero of many melo-
dramas thundered cheerfully: “Well, boys, another
of us gone."

Wondergraph, Ltd., an Adelaide picture concern,
is encouraging local talent, and now shows the
“Wondergraph Weekly,” comprising all-Adelaide in-
cidents, photographed by H. Krisehock, who, a few
years ago, didn’t know a camera from a crowbar.
He was clerk in Quiz office when he first experi-
mented with a hand-camera. He quitted Quiz when
the- Critic, under McKain, began to illustrate lav-
ishly. Now he serves S’Langdon’s Advertiser, and
is in the first flight of Press photographers—and
there are not many in that flight. The inquest after
the gruesome Towitta tragedy was held in a shed.
Detective Priest, holding a piece of hair between his
fingers, was seeking to prove that it was human,
when he was staggered by Krischock’s, “One moment,
please.” The fiend escaped whilst the Court was con-
sidering to what extent its dignity had been mal-
treated.
Joe Blascheck, the monologue entertainer lately
returned to Australia after many years, graduated
for the profesh at the old Dagonet Club, Melbourne.
The Dagonet had a number of bright young men on
its roll some 15 or 20 years ago, _ and several of
them are now something to speak of in the theatrical
world. Blascheck was one leading light; another
was Jo Smith, who of late years has done respectable
work as an Australian dramatist. Albert Whelan,
the gifted versatile mimic and actor, gave some of
his first performances when he was a Dagonetian;
and among other young bloods of the club were
F. W. Newby (who took up Wilson Barrett’s run-
ning in England), Reg. Robins (nowadays scenic
artist for Anderson), and Bert Levy (the black-and-
white artist who went to America and made his mark
as a lightning-sketeher on the vaudeville stage).
Alfred Deery, the tall and spry young Australian
baritone who "more than holds his own among the
importations of “The Gay Gordons” Co., is said to
be leaving shortly to try his luck in the land the
importations came from.
“0.K.”: How beautifully untrue to fact are some
of the bouquets which a theatrical management
casts at its own show! For instance, Clarke and
Meynell’s florist a while ago supplied the interesting
allegation that “The Gay Grisette” gave Robert
Courtneidge a great lift in business. The story was
that Courtneidge, after leaving Australia, took “The
Gay Grisette” in hand, and toured it for many years.
“It was an enormous success, and with the money
derived from this source he embarked in managerial
enterprise in London, where, with ‘The Arcadians,’
he has reached the very top of the tree.” And he
owed it all to “The Gay Grisette.” Now, the truth
of the matter is that when Courtneidge tried his luck
with the “Grisette” many years ago, he had no suc-
cess with it. It lost him all the money he could
afford. In after years he had better luck with “The
Blue Moon,” but his first notable hit in musical
comedy management in London was “The Dairy-
maids,” after which he scored more honor than finan-
cial glory with “Tom Jones.” Then came “The
Arcadians,” which has passed its second year at the
Shaftesbury Theatre, and earned a pile of money for
him. When that charmingly uncommon opera has
exhausted its present vogue in London, he will pro-
duce a Japanese musical play, which is to be a
gorgeous thing, and has been in rehearsal for some
time. It will come to light in September probably.
“Ajacks”: Recent “Poverty Point” par. mentions
that Howard Edie is doing well in S’Africa. I knew he
would do well somewhere. Last time I saw him he
was one of the two members of the Howard Edie
Dramatic Co., doing the backblocks of S.A. The
play was “The Bells,” and I fancy Edie played 15
parts. I am sure he played eight, but the other
seven were confusing, because Edie would sometimes
swerve round quickly and answer his own remark,
and it was difficult to keep count. There were bells
behind scenes. When both Edie and his assistant
were on the stage, I think Edie rang the bells with
his foot. The audience didn’t much care for tragedy,
and yelled for something brighter, whereupon the
versatile actor told them of the Irish actor playing
“Hamlet.” Hamlet’s audience tired of the bard, and
demanded “Pat Molloy and the Roisin’ Shtep.”
Hamlet shouted: “Damn yer ois! Wotcher expect
for toppence?” The audience (Edie’s) hardly ap-
preciated the point of the story, but it liked the
fearless use of the “Damn,” and thereafter was his
very good friend.
“C. Ockney”: If “Ancient Pistol” (B. 18/5/’ll)
can possess his soul in patience for a few months, he
will in all probability have the opportunity of en-
joying once more. his “rousing, supremely-actable
Henry V.” It is one of Lewis Waller’s best parts,
and Waller is due here next year. By-the-way, what
a pity that “Monsieur Beaucaire” will not be avail-
able to its original exponent! If there is anything
more satisfying than Waller’s exquisite performance
of this part, the-present scribe would be glad to
know of it —and then he wouldn’t believe it.
“Carmine”: “Perdita” Robinson, by Hoppner, has
been added to the Melbourne Art Gallery, at a cost
of a trifle of £3OOO, paid out of the Felton Be-
quest. “Perdita’s” maiden name was Mary Darby;
the surname had been arbitrarily changed from
McDermott by some ancestor. Born in Bristol, she
was married at 16, under the vain delusion that
Robinson had coin. Finding he had none, she went
on the stage, by Garrick’s advice, and did extremely
well as Juliet and Perdita, and in numerous other
parts. Yet she left the stage at 22, having become very
friendly with the Prince of Wales of those days be-
fore he was of age—so very friendly that he gave her
a post-obit on his father for £20,000. She never
seems to have attempted to use it. In later years
she wrote poetry and novels, and the books had a
large sale through the princely aroma —which, by-
the-way, she was fond of introducing obliquely. A
heap of lies have been told about her ; in fact, it is
hard work to disentangle the truth —except that she
was very beautiful and was painted often by such as
Reynolds, Gainsborough, Romney, Hoppner, and
Cosway.
“L.H.P.”: With so much Sousa in the air, why
doesn’t someone put on one of his operas—“El Capi-
tan” for preference? New York saw the entertain-
ing work in 1896, and London in 1899. It is the
best of his dozen operas. De Wolf Hopper, “the
’Murkan comedian,” was the star of “El Capitan,”
which breaks delightfully fresh stage ground, the
locale being Peru during the Spanish conquest in the
15th century, and naturally the clothes-man and the
scene-painter have a glorious chance. Chas. Klein,
author of “The Third Degree,” wrote the libretto,
which is wedded to some of the sprightliest of the
March King’s music.

“Philoyid”: I wrote of the two remarkable Yid-
dish theatrical performances given last year in the
Melbourne Temperance Hall, under the direction of
Weisberg, a Yid actor and manager, from N’York.
Success has emboldened him to obtain a professional
Yid co. from New York. They will appear once
a week for six months in the Temperance Hall
(Melb.), and will be seen also in Sydney. The
rdpertoire is extensive; there are peculiar Yid dra-
mas, such as adaptations of “Leah the Forsaken” and
the opera of “The Jewess,” by the Hebrew com-
poser Halevy, with the adapted plays of such au-
thors as Shakspeare and Schiller. Although last
year the houses were pretty good, the shows were
very poorly advertised. Now some capitalists are
in it, so there will doubtless be proper advertisement.
Quite a rush may therefore ensue, as the New
York actors and actresses will peg in with an
energy like that with which the Sicilian players
startled London.
Southern Cross (W.A.) talks of having a theatre,
and the local Times says: “The concern is to be run
on very strict lines. No gentlemen without socks
will be admitted, and the management reserves the
right to fumigate anyone before admittance when
deemed advisable. It will thus be seen that the
venture deserves every success, and that it is sure to
fill a long-felt want.”
The benefit to the Hagans at the Melbourne Royal
was marked by the production of a playet by a Mel-
bourne pressman,, Alfred Buchanan. The piece,
“Straws in the Wind,” enacted by two or three
members of the Anderson co., has a rather worn
plot, but its satire is keen. The principal character
is a politician, Edmund Bulstrode, who in one scene
is represented as discussing with his secretary cer-
tain political missives and invitations—among them
a request for his presence at a dinner “to meet the
Archbishop of Melbourne and Mr. John Wren.”
“Grant 0. L.”: Mick Jones was a good milk-carter
but a rotten actor. Occasionally a suburban touring
co. would give him a small part and a chance to
make an ass of himself. Vhen the Montague Swaggers’
All Star Combination came round to Mick’s suburb,
he was allotted the part of the faithful old body-
servant in the Russian thriller, written by Swaggers
himself, and entitled “To the Death.” On the first
night, in the great scene where Swaggers, as the
travelling Englishman, fumes to his factotum about
the villainous Grand Duke’s insult, Mick stood limply
before his boss with bent knees and open mouth, like
an exhausted cab horse. This attitude was under-
stood to represent rapt attention. “I must fight
him,” screamed Swaggers to Mick. “This insult
must be wiped out in blood!” And right there a
brisk, business-like voice from the gallery said:
“Mick, leave ’arf a pint o’ blood t’morrer!” ,
GEORGE MARLOW. PURVEYOR OF

“The Minister for Grace and Elegance”: Was con-
sidering the repulsive lumpishness of a toe-dancer’s
feet the other night, and wondering sympathetically
what the poor thing did for her dreadful corns, when
a whispered argument started in the seat ahead.
The gentleman contended that the performer’s foun-
dations had been deformed by dancing; the lady was
firmly of the opinion that she had plugged the toes of
her dancing stockings with wadding. I looked at the
laborious stilted circling of the rigid skirted figure,
and decided that, however it was accomplished, it
wasn’t worth while. The ballet dancers who used to
flutter about the stage on their toes with one of the
bygone Italian Opera Cos., made the painful style
of dancing look easy and pleasant; the quick, bird-
like run from the back of the stage to the footlights
used to astonish and delight the audiences —it was as
if the dancer had suddenly been lifted on to a pair
of noiseless skates, so graceful and effortless did it
appear. That was the perfection of art. The oc-
casional toe-dancer, who elevates herself nowadays,
gives a sort of ugly endurance test. Whether the
lumpiness of her feet is due to corns or wadding I
don’t know; but they certainly are not decorative,
and I am going to take some steps about it —to
music, of course.

The police-court at Millicent (S.A.) held
the other day—following a recent English prece-
dent—that “a single damn was sufficient founda-
tion for a charge of indecent language.” And no
doubt it swore the witnesses on the Sacred Book,
as usual —the book of the hundred damns.
Australia is getting large, unsolicited tes-
timonials from a German named Broeg, who
represents Count Castell, the head of the pencil
firm of Faber. Broeg, who hails from Nurem-
berg, paid two visits'to the Commonwealth, and
became so enamored of its climate, resources and
prospects that he appointed himself Australia’s
Spruicher-in-Ordinary to the German nation.
At Nuremberg and Berlin allegedly huge audi-
ences are reported to have listened to his lectures
with wolfish appetite.
“ Percival Hardcash ” (from Stockholm):
Driven out of Australia by Socialistic legislation
—and other things—l said to myself : “ Now let
me never be reminded of the cursed country
again.” So I went ski-ing at Sartsliog, in Swe-
den, and one day saw a pink streak in the snow.
Picking it up, I found it was the best part of an
unmentionable Australian paper. Can I never
get out of way of the vile thing ! I kept the rag
—a mere oversight—and I dare say it made me
mention Australia. My friend was a Swedish
banking magnate. “How pitiful,” said he,
“ that so large a country should have no material
from which to make iron and steel! ” “Ah! ” I
said. “ How did you discover our secret ? ” Oh,
he had met many travelling Australians— “ and,
besides, if you had the raw materials, of course
you would use them, and, as you don’t, they must
certainly be right.”- I sniffed, and smelt the
perfume of the Stinking Fish still upon his
clot lies.
“Snooks”: I have just been reading
The Lone Hand’s account of the semi-starved
women-workers of Sydney. It grieves me that
such things should be ; but what grieves me most
is the thought of all those women wasting in
Sydney. In Queensland there are hundreds of
good farm-homes just starving for women, and
not lonely Outback homes either. Plenty of
selections and farms would be better cultivated if
there were a wife and kiddies about; but there is
no inducement to a bachelor to beautify or im-
prove the place unless there’s money in it. And
there’s no money in home improvement. In fact,
one gets tempted to lease to a Chow and reside in
a town where women can at least be seen some-
times. The plain, unpleasant truth is that the
starved white-worker prefers to stay in the town
where she can see the gorgeous window-displays
of unattainable frills and fashions.
The Oldest Inhabitant has a good half-
hour :
Apropos Melbourne’s ancient pubs (B. 11/5/’ll), In
1854 the only beer-pump on the west side of Swanstou-
street, from Flinders-street to Collins street, was in the
Queen’s Arms Inn, on the nor’-west corner of Flinders-
lane ; it was kept by Tom Monahan. On November 7,
1837, John Batman gave £59 for the corner half-acre, and
£6O for the half acre adjoining, which constitutes the
corner of Collins-street. The next pub on that side, and
in that year, ’54, was the Royal Oak, kept by W. Finlay.
It stood three doors from Little Collins-street.
There was none other until you reached the Rail-
way Hotel, three doors north of Bourke-street,
and kept by James Neales, a member of the Garrick,
and, I think, an old pro. Three doors north of Little
Bourke-street was the Turf Hotel, kept by Billy Lang,
afterwards the racing man. Further up, at the corner of
Lonsdale-street, was the Britannia, kept by J. Farmer.
On the south-west corner of Latrobe-street was the
Travellers’ Rest, kept by Michael D’Arcy. This
D’Arcy proposed Graham Berry on the old hustings in
Swanston-street, where the new portion of the Town Hall
now stands. The City Police Court stood there then ;
the hustings were on the space between the court and
the old Town Hall. There was a vacancy for East Mel-
bourne, and Edward Cohen and Pat O’Brien were candi-
dates. Governor Barkly meanwhile had given Richard
Heales a dissolution, arid Cohen and O’Brien, the House
being on its deathbed, agreed to propose absentee C. H.
Ebden for the seat. D’Arcy, however, spoiled the
scheme and proposed Berry, who was in the crowd. He
got the show of hands, too, and as Ebden’s supporters
did not demand a poll, Berry was declared duly elected,
I saw him take his seat that night. In a couple of days the
House was dissolved. Berry got in for Collingwood at the
general election, and Cohen for East Melbourne (with
Ambrose Kyte for colleague). O’Brien, of Kincora, was out
of it. Away up towards a’Beckett-street was the Farm-
ers’ Home, by J. Stone. On the opposite side, where the
Cathedral Hotel now is, were Walker and Wright, grocers.
There was no pub nearer than John Jordan’s Rainbow,
on the north corner of Little Collins-street (Jordan was a
big M.U.0.0.F. man). On the south-east corner of Bourke-
street was the Royal Mail, by H. Callow, afterwards kept by
Charles C. Skarratt, who became a Mount Morganaire.
The opposite corner was then William Hickinbotham’s
carpet warehouse, afterwards converted into the Royal
Admiral Hotel, and kept by a City Councillor, John
Walker. Next Hickinbotham was the grocery shop of
Michael Dawson, who became Town Clerk Fitzgibbon’s
father-in-law, and died a very wealthy man. Young and
Jackson’s public house was originally The Princes Bridge
Hotel ; that half-acre was also purchased by John Batman
for £lOO.
“Gwy”: Doubtless the chairman of the
Bank of N. S. Wales would have been surprised
and pained had one of the shareholders arisen at
the meeting the other day and told him that he
was interfering in other people’s business. Dr.
Mackellar was replying to a question, and, from
the nature of his answer, some notice had evi-
dently been given of it. Probably the answer was
directed against criticisms which have from time
to time been passed on the bank’s treatment of its
officers, considering what a great and prosperous
institution it is. He referred to the addi-
tions that had been made to the pen-
sion fund, and remarked that the directors
considered these contributions were better
than “ ephemeral benefits.” Thus he admitted
in effect that the bank could afford to do more for
its officers ; but it would be a bad thing to put it
on their screws. The clerk might be tempted to
spend it—in fact, there is no saying to what
limits he might go in the excess of his joy.
With all deference to Dr. Mackellar, this grand-
motherliness is all rot, and it only dodges the
real question. Provision for old age is excellent;
but if one thing is more exasperating than
another to a full-grown man, it is to have an
employer, with this false benevolence and con-
cern, telling the employee of the comfortable old
age be will have if he will trust his future to the
benevolent employer. The average employee
would prefer to get something out of the stock-
ing now, and look after his own future. No one,
for example, stands at the elbow of the directors
of the B.N.S.W. when they are at their clubs
and deprives them of the “ephemeral” joy of a
good dinner, although it might be a lot better for
their digestion.
THE HORNY-HANDED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The hairy palmist gazed into
The hand the youth held out to him:
“The toilers’ curse has smitten you.
You wield a shovel for your bread.”
The wan youth shook his weary head.
“You hold the plough? You use the pick?
You swing a hammer? Pound up paint?
You pile the hay upon the rick?
You are a miner? Here’s the taint
Of graft.” The youth observed, “I ain’t.
“Then why these palms with half-inch hide?
Why do these fingers horny grow?”
“I am an usher,” he replied.
“I get my hands all toughened so
Through clappin’ actors at the show.”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
THE SCHOOLROOM OF THE MIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The great study of the actress is modern life —
the life of this world of men and things which it is
her part to portray. If she is to make her work
vital and broad, her knowledge of the world must be
vital and broad. She must keep in touch with every
part of it. —Ethel Barrymore.
Brains,' beauty, study, diction, dress
These days cut little ice
As factors in a stage success ;
The mummer pays Fame’s price
By methods which are more or less
Abhorrent to The Nice.
See where, in yonder foetid slum,
Fair Ermyntrude de Vere
Foregathers with an ancient “hum” —
Nay, do not rail or sneer,
Or turn oh her, with .visage glum;
She learns her acting here 1
No modern hero is equipped
To play a sailor’s part,
Who has not personally shipped
Before the mast. The heart
Of ev’ry audience is gripped
By his resultant Art.
No dock or penitentiary,
Or haunt of persons bent
On deeds of criminality,
But holds its complement
Of pros, acquiring painfully
Their craft’s embodiment.
You’ve noticed how each city bar,
From morn to even-fall,
Contains its pet dramatic “star,’’
Now bright, now faint and small: —
The mimes crowd in from near and far
For Art’s sake —that is all.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

“ Button-stick ” waves his sporran :
The man who dropped into the “ Melbourne Chatter ”
(B. 25/5/T1) with a millinery item had better drop out
again and dig up his facts. He prophesies the abolition
of the kiltie for several reasons. He says it’s a.fool of a
dress for military purposes “in a climate like ours.” Is
our climate wor.-e than the Egyptian deserts? Or India
in the hot weather? Yet the kilties managed to flap
along there. The Mutiny was about as tough a testing
as men or uniforms were ever put to, and the cake-and-
kiltie regiments showed amongst the lowest averages of
sick (apart from wounded, of course). Again, the man-
that a Highland Brigade at Magersfontein
had to lie on its tummy in the sun, and that the sun,
“ beating down on the unprotected calves,” tied such
knots in the muscles that the whole brigade “was ren-
dered unserviceable for months.” Now, the Highland
Brigade wore stockings which cover the calves. That’s
a minor point, I admit, and the men did get sun-burned
in the back portions of the knees. But they had lain in
the same position and had a similar roasting at Modder
Rivera few days before, without being “ rendered unser-
viceable for months.” And, again, Magersfontein was
fought Dec. 10, and on Feb. 3 four of the same kiltie
regiments were scrapping at Koodoosberg—the first in-
fantry to be used by Roberts in his big move, too. And
a few days after they marched 30 miles and waded into
the Paardeberg shindy without a break. Nothing very
unserviceable about that, eh ?
The Gov.-General is to pack up his luggage
when the present lease runs out, and move on
from the mock-castle which was built for the use
of N.S. VV. State Governors, unless Holman
and his colleagues can be turned from their
present purpose. The fine grounds bite a
large slice out of the Botanic Gardens, and
the Labor Government comes to the sensible con-
clusion that it is time the public had the use of
them, since they are put to practically no use at
all now. The building will come in handy for
public-library purposes. The official rubber-
stamp might probably have still reposed undis-
turbed, and a horse or two might have grazed
unmolested on these matchless slopes of the har-
bor, if it hadn't been certain that other bites will
presently have to be taken out of the Domain.
The Sydney Hospital must be extended, and
there is only one way to extend it: so things
will be evened up by throwing down the fences
that now warn the public off one of the most pic-
turesque parts of the harbor foreshores, and the
G.-G. will have to be housed in some place where
keep-off-the-grass notices won’t matter so much

“ Brass Button ” : There are very few of
the first-class deep-sea boats now which are not
fitted with wireless, except some of the Maori-
landers. The recent feverish haste in the matter
was mainly the result of a whisper that voluntary
action would show great wisdom. Somebody got
the alarming tip that a little Act was being
framed,' something to the effect that evert/ steamer
carrying passengers—the Sydney Ferries ex-
cepted, T suppose —would have to rig up the
spark. This is a good deal more than the inter-
state cos. had been plotting out; so the word was
passed for all hands to get at it, that the
Government might be induced to sit back. What-
ever the cause, there was a stampede for sparks,
and the first time my marine residence is assaulted
by Providence, I’m now in the happy position of
being able to call up a cab.
* * * *
Neptune has been good to Sydney dock-
yards this last year. First, the French mailboat
Salazie came in with its engine-room mashed up
after a breakdown ; then the Eastern argued with
the Salamander Bank, near Brisbane, and the
hospital expenses came to £30,000; now the
Prinz Waldemar is getting its broken nose
straightened for four figures after a coral reef ex-
perience in the Caroline Group. These big
smashes are thinly-disguised blessings to the ship-
wrights of a country which, for want of a duty,
imports its vessels from a land of low wages and
older and therefore sometimes better-organised
industry. Every crippled ship that thus struggles
into an Australian harbor for repairs helps to stun
the Stinking Fish, and gives the shipwright a
chance to drop a steel-plate of proof on the
ignorant, abject being who habitually decries the
Australian artisan and trie-; to drive him to
become some other country’s artisan.
“The Shark”: So the Sydney Clerks’
Union and the Sydney Trades Hall are ponder-
ing whether the article or apprentice system
should be approved in connection with clerical
labor, and niade a subject for the Wages
Board. I shall feel sorry for the future
generation of clerks if it is. I have just
finished five years under articles to a
man I detested from the first week ;
but there was no escape for me.
He never had the spirit to kick
me out, although he received
enough provocation. No doubt
he recognised that my services
were cheap, unwilling though
they were. And that is the
reason the apprenticeship system
has continued so long. Free ser-
vice, generally with a substantial
premium, just suits most employers.
The Clerks’ Union will find that
employers will consider the indenture
proposal only too readily. It will also find that, on
account of the “experience” the lad will acquire,
the remuneration will be less. But do you think
that employers will go out of their way to inst ruct
the lad ? Not much ! Take solicitors’ clerks :
you will find plenty of them with a better general
knowledge than the articled specimen, notwith-
standing the opinion the latter commonly has of
himself. And that's another objection. It breeds
snobbery.
* * . * *
Through his superabundant sympathy for
the deserted wife and his desire to mulct the
defaulting husband in damages, costs, alimony, or
whatever else he can throw at him, Victoria’s
Chief Justice MLadden lately made an order that
is liable to have consequences. It occurred in the
case of Beer v. Beer, in which the female Beer
asked for a decree because the male Beer had gone
to “ the West” permanently and finally. Knowing
the malty-named gentleman, she didn’t bother
about alimony; she was able to look after herself,
and merely wanted to get rid of the bad liquor.
In his wisdom Madden, however, declined to give
her a decree till he learned more about Tangle-
foot; then, finding he was in a billet worth £4 ss.
a week in Perth, his Honor directed the iady to
amend her petition by claiming alimony, and pre-
sent it to him that day four weeks. It doesn’t
seem to have struck Madden that most women
divorce deserting husbands because they want to
marry someone else—the mere getting of a decree
doesn’t make them any less or any more deserted
—so by thus postponing this lady’s matrimonial
freedom for a month he is probably running a
risk of fracturing two loving hearts, bursting up a
proposed happy home, and kicking a prospective
marriage tie into oblivion. All of which feats are
understood to be the peculiar prerogative of Sosh.

The impressions of James Edmond :
We struck Marseilles on a dav that looked like a page
torn out of Paradise. And Marseilles is worth striking.
The Cold arrived near Paris, a gale of it. In London the
. com ariiveu ueai ran®,
ram rained, and it was very wet rain indeed. Also the
snow snowed, and it was cold white snow. The wind
blew, and the hail hailed, and the blizzard blizzed, and
great was the bliz thereof.
Much of the West End of London has been recon-
structed since I was a boy, and it is magnificent. As for
the places where the great, mass of people live—Wap-
ping, Rotherhithe, Whitechapel, Hoxton, Limestone,
Bermondsey, Barking, Blackwall, etc., and the by-ways
of Westminster, Islington and Battersea—l fancy perdi-
tion must be better. I spent two days exploring them
and didn't see a cheerful face except when the owner was
coming out of a public-house. Liquor is cheap, and men
and women drink in the pubs promiscuously.
I hunted up Louis Brennan’s place, but didn’t find
Brennan. He is no longer with the War Office. His tor-
pedo was a thing which could be fired wnh absolute
accuracy for two or three miles ; dug modern guns cat ry
so far that vessels no longer come within that range of
one another. So the old Whitehead torpedo, though it
is much more of a gamble, but has a very long range, is
on top again. In all Brennan’s stupendous factory I
found one confidential clerk and a boy. His experimental
mono-rail line isn t working just now, yet mee
engineers who swear bv it, and all the printed reports
about it seem favorable. In the old countries, where
most of the railways are built, railway people view it
with horror as a device which may one day drive them
to the cost of general reconstruction, and new countries
look on it with suspicion because the old ones don’t take
it up.
I looked in at Kidderminster to see the place where I
spent some early years. It isi a bleak,. black, muddy
little town of allevs and crooked by-ways. And Liver-
pool, which is bigger than Sydney, hasn’t a wide street
or a straight street in it. The five buildings opposite
my very comfortable hotel were (l)a factory with two huge
chimneys,(2)apublichouse,(3)apublichouse,(4:)an eating
house with a beer license, (5) a public house. It was the
day after Good Fnday and the number o hatless u.i-
tidy, diner v women with bruised faces and blackened c\ es
was amazing. In Liverpool a public holiday is a day
when you spank your missus. Here the old buildings
are black, the middle-aged ones are getting black, and
the new ones are smudged.
Manchester is the same, only more so. Glasgow is
much the same, only it has the lower Clyde at hand, and
the lower Clyde is a paradise in fine weather. I struck it
in a roaring 'gale with blasphemy on its wings-did 22
miles by tram car to see the beauties of Loch Lomond,
but, owing to mist and driving rain, I saw nothing. It
was on such a day the Devil got drowned and Hell became
full of water. . .......
In Edinburgh the New Town is beautiful and the Old
Town hasn’t been cleaned or repaired since the days of
Queen Mary. And many of the inhabitants haven’t been
cleaned or repaired since then either.

THE “LOVE” CHILD
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Wanted kind person to adopt healthy baby boy, one
week old, of refined parentage. —Melbourne paper.
Ma is refined.
Ma had the fun without the fee
And but a half maternity—
Ma is refined.
Pa is resigned—
To be a father for a week.
Then the “ kind person ” he must seek.
Pa is refined.
Ma is resigned.
She takes the creature of her womb,
And sends it slowly to the tomb—
And so refined !
Oh ! for the coarse
And handy p irent who protects
The little ehi.d his love selects !
Or for the horse !
Or for the bear,
Jealous of anyone that helps
Or hurts her clumsy lumbering whelps,
Or finds her lair !
Eagles that bind
The thorny twigs to make the nest—
That fly home with a bloody breast —
Are not refined.
The winter wind
Assaulting earth with icy shock,
The clumsy python on the rock,
Are not refined.
But, bear in mind,
The slayers of the sacred lust
Who give their nursling to the dust —
They are refined.

When the mostly-Labor Broken Hill
council made a muddle of things, a few months
ago, the Fat press held up the tangle as a shock-
ing result of “ Labor domination.’’ Now there is
considerable trouble brewing in some of the
toniest councils in the Ma State, and the Blue
Mountains Shire Council, which is anti-Labor
beyond the average, seems to be in for special
trouble. The auditor recently gave it a mild
slating for a number of shortcomings, whereupon
the snail-like body retired to its shell, and has
been sitting in it ever since. But, as it was so
fervently anti-Labor, the “great” dailies have
of coarse carefully abstained from noticing any
bad smell. It begins to be suspected that more
than half the representatives of the Backbone of
the Country are doing things which the Act never
contemplated, and are not doing things that the
Act (nominally) insists should be done ; so the
Works Department is likely to soon have a merry
time trying to comb out the burrs.
Acting-Chief Sec. Flowers’s decision that
N.S.W. picture shows may deal only with sacred
subjects, and may take up a collection only after
the service, is all very well as far as it goes ; but it
doesn’t go far enough. To guarantee respecta-
bility, Mr. Flowers should insist that any
announcements or explanations should be made
only by ordained ministers. This would help a
poorly-paid profession to put a little extra butter
on the bread, and might even enable some of
them to shut up their marriage shops.
NOT MUCH ELEVATION.
SHORT UN: “I hadn't much to start with, but
I was well brought up.”
LONG 'UN: “ Yes, but not far.”
Red Tape, Junr.”: Our department is
hooraying. The staff was working day and
night, and the pressure was such that our new
Minister-in-Charge, Holman, was scandalised.
He decided that something had to be done, and
this is how he did it. With one blow tie re-
solved to turn the chief clerk into Assistant
Under-Secretary, but hastened to inform the
newspaper Johnnies that this involved no addi-
tion to the staff, nor would it increase the depart-
mental vote on the Estimates. It was the
simplest solution ever; arid I now suggest that
Mr. Smith, who comes next, be appointed
Deputy Assistant Under-Sec., and that the man
next to him be gazetted Sub-Assistant Under-
Sec., and so on. Thus the Department will be
able to get through the same work in half the
time, and everything will be “reformed” and
the garden Will be beautiful.
* * * *
Uncle Cohen, who held a bill of sale over
a Sydney burgher’s property, recently realised on
it by forcibly removing the chattels. For this
proceeding he was sued by the owner, who was
awarded £25 damages for trespass, on the ground
that the money-lender was not registered under
the Act. This case has made various opulent
clerks in banks and Government offices do some
hard thinking. They lend their own (or some-
body else’s) money to fellow-employees at high
rates of interest, on personal security, bills of
sale, pro. notes, etc. They dare not register under
the Act, and if they strike an enlightened snag
who declines to pay up, knowing the fatal flaw in
the lender’s position, they must put up with the
loss. Still, their profits are large enough to stand
an occasional bump; and, anyway, bumps are
very few, because 99 per cent, of the customers
are in deadly fear of advertising their hard-
upness.
* * * *
A N.S.W. North Coast medical man’s
warning:—
This year for the first time dengue hns leaped right
down from the Queensland border to the Clarence. The
autumn just closed was by far the worst mosquito year
the coast has experienced ; and the mosquito is blamed
for carrying the dengue germ. Indeed, there were cases
of so-called influenza in Sydney this year which were
desperately like mild dengue. The mosquito also carries
the disease which a Brisbane medico first diagnosed a
few years ago as filaria ; it was then ascertained that
most of the inmates of the Brisbane Hospital were vic-
tims to it. So far, there has not been a great deal of
research work carried out in the North in any of these
diseases, partly because, when dengue strikes a place
there are not many who can spare time to investigate!
As it has got well into the Ma State now, there may he
some attempt to treat it as a Federal matter, as should
done; with the cattle tick also. It is said, by the way,
that a liberated cattle tick will never crawl in any other
direction than south or west.

All the honors paid to coming and depart-
ing Governors these times fade into insignificance
in comparison with early Australian tributes to
viee-regality. Think of what happened when the
foundations of the Commonwealth were being laid
at Botany Bay ! Australia’s first two stone churches
oTthe Anglican persuasion were at Sydney and
P&tTamatta respectivelv. and were named by
an order of Governor King in 1802. The one
was dedicated to St. Phillip, as a delicate compli-
ment to Governor Phillip, the original Excellency;
whilst the other was named St. John’s, in memory
of Sir John Hunter, the successor to Phillip. The
Parramatta church, completed in 1803, was a
quire-tony edifice, with two towers to it. The
earlier religious services in the ancient village
were held by the Rev. Johnson in a carpenter’s
shop-an appropriate place of Christian worship,
anyhow. But the church that came 12 years later
was felt to be a place where even the Naic \st might
get spiritual improvement. The Sydney Gazette
of April, 1803 stated that “many ladies of the
first respectability were present” at the inaugural
service, and probablv, had the tabernacle been
christened St. John Hunter’s, the attendance of
“ ladies of the first respectability ” would have
been even larger.
It has been left to Malvern “City," a
newly-glorified suburb of the overblown city of
Melbourne, to invent the foolishest stunt for the
entertainment of a Governor. His Ex. Fuller
(the volunteer captain who is going to teach Aus-
tralia how to soldier) was last week “presented
with the freedom of the city,” which aping of a
British custom, with little modern meaning in the
Cold Country, is absolutely meaningless in Aus-
tralia. Ihe dog-tax man is just as free of the
city of Malvern as the imported Governor tfho
lives at Stonington. But the inane thing was
done; and the Mayor of Malvern, to make rhe
ridiculous show still more ridiculous, eked it out
with feeble jokes about married men and latch-
keys.

Even his former colleagues now pour notes
of interrogation over Victoria’s railway expert
Tait. Thus, Chief Commissioner Fitzpatrick
recently told the Railways Standing Committee
that the alleged deficit of .£50,000 a year which
Tait asserted the suburban lines produced had
almost disappeared, and would most likely disap-
pear- altogecher by the end of the financial year.
Doubtless the overcrowded suburban lines are
paying now—if they aren’t they never will—but
to recover wholly from an annual loss of £50,000
in less than 10 months was too much, and’the
Committee smiled thoughtfully.
j Sardonyx : Why are the parsons mak-
I i n £ such a hullabaloo about this JVe tcmere
decree ? If a man and woman are duly married
according to 13ritish law, their children are ipso
facto legitimate, and no foreign promulgation can
affect that status. What the members of any
particular church may think about the matter is
.of no consequence so long as they only think.
But if they proceed to say that, for instance, So-
and-so’s children are illegitimate, ,by reason of a
certain Papal deliverance, they can be dealt with
in the ordinary way for slander. Also, there’s
nothing but bosh in the assertion that if a
Catholic husband should weary of his Protestant
spouse, he could plead an awakened conscience as
an excuse for deserting her. No authority exter-
nal to a nation; can relieve any member of that
nation from his civil obligations, and the Law
would get up and hit the gentleman with the
conscience just as hard as if he hadn’t a scrap of
such a thing about him.
N.S.W. Education Minister Beeby’s deci-
sion that all State waifs are to be sent to farm
homes has impressed the Victorian Government.
Notwithstanding that only lately Victoria
adopted the now-discarded Sydney idea of a
training ship, and put the John Murray, formerly
of the Loch line, in commission, Attorney-
General Brown is in favor of following N.S.W.
right out, and it is likely the Cabinet will come
round to the same view. Already Ballarat has an
orphanage farm home of 50 acres. Formerly old
mining ground, and regarded as useless, the patch
supplies the orphanage with all the vegetables,
milk, butter and eggs it needs, and showed a sur-
plus profit last year of £577. This experiment is
such a success that it thunders to Beeby to go on,
and to the Victorians to follow as fast as they are
able.
* * * *
There are not many Chinese fruit-sellers
in Adelaide, but there are some, and one of them
was astonishingly candid in court last week. He
had beaten a price-cutting rival about the legs
with a pole, and when accused of murderous in-
tent replied: “I no want to kill him; want to
break his legs.” The court enjoyed the case so
immensely, even to the extent of making puns on
defendant’s name, Ah Chee, that that sneeze
seemed quite surprised when the case suddenly
took the serious form of a fine of £lO and costs.
Ihe heathen’s cheerful admission concerning
his neighbor’s legs recalls an interview the writer
once “did ” with Way Lee, a big Adelaide mer-
chant, who had just returned from China. Thp
Australian atmosphere was full of strikes at the
time, and the interviewer asked if there were any
labor unions in China. Plenty. Any strikes?
Plenty. And what did China do? Oh, it sent
around a magistrate, who inquired, and said so-
and-so. Yes, but supposing the parties wouldn’t
accept the award ? The largest guffaw in the
continent burst from about the .middle of Way
Lee’s face. Then he drew his forefinger across
his throat, said “Cluck,” guffawed again at the
absurdity of the last question, and remarked
laconically, “Plenty men China.”

MORE MEMORIALS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The soldiers whom Napoleon worked,
We’re told, were very well content
If batons in their knapsaclc3 lurked ;
But here the swaddy is intent ,
On finding: there a monument,
And in the field will take no risk
Unless assured an obelisk.
Each town and hamlet has its stone
To glorify heroic Brown,
Or gallant Tompkins, overthrown
By brutal Boer, who must bow down
To Britons’ might and England’s crown,
And learn by force (the blinded fool !)
The biessedness of British rule.
In Melbourne fully half a score
Of grim war monuments are seen ;
But more are wanted, more and more,
Big piles with little ones between,
And needles tall on every green.
Let stones the high and by-ways choke,
Till all the land is stony broke !
Brave M’lnerney will desist,
And martial Madden cease his pleas
When pyramids to pierce the mist
And columns tall as Pyrenees
Restrict pedestrian liberties,
And for each thousand warrior gents.
We have two thousand monuments !

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Quidam”: Anyone who wants to create a
commotion among white cockatoos (and pretty
well everyone who has a maize crop wants to do
something dreadful to the white invaders) should
try the dodge of first capturing one in a trap, a
difficult but not impossible task, and then darken-
ing i the captive’s complexion with black lead or
something similar. The awful horror of the mar-
auders when the liberated black brother arrives on
the scene is manifested by their hurried scorch for
the horizon, over which, accompanied by a fearsome
and unanimous screech, the whole brigade will dis-
appear about 1J seconds later ; and for some material
period even the prospects of an eternity of corn-
fields will not tempt them back. For the purpose
of promoting rapid emigration the method can only
be compared to that of painting the figure of a man
in phosphorus paint on a gum tree by a nigger’s
camp. This scientific expedient, applied by a
jackeroo per medium of a tin of cockroach-exter-
minating paste, has been known to deprive an Out-
back Queensland station of its colored supernumer-
aries for upwards of 12 months. What the jackeroo
would have got had the boss sheeted home the crime
was a question that caused the successful scientist
many misgivings.

“Condamine”: “Austra” (B. 4/5/T1)
quotes the Queensland Agricultural Journal as
saying that in some cases of snakebite a ligature is
useless. What the article did say was that a bite
on the wrist or lower leg should be ligatured above
the elbow or knee respectively, as .the presence of
two bones in the lower portions of the limbs renders
a ligature there useless. As to the danger of
strychnine, the warning applies only in ease the
snake which tastes you is non-venomous. 1 have
never known permanganate (effective also in cases
of adder-bite) to be dangerous under any condi-
tions. ’
“Eddyson”: An accident at the Rival
Miners had disabled our braceman, and so scared
the -whim-boy that he threw in his resignation. In
this extremity Crowther, the boss, was constrained
to employ Ragan on the brace, and his son “Jawn”
to pilot the old roan round the whim ring. The
Ragan was from a distant spud “farum,” and
wholly unskilled, but his confidence was enormous.
He went on the night-shift, and at about three in
the morning came rattling at Crowther’s hut. “Are
ye awake, sor?” said the new braceman. “Have ye
the bit av a ball iv sthring beside ye?” “What the
devil do you want with a ball of string?” growled
the manager. Ragan put up a pathetic cry. “Thim
fellies down the hole,” said he, “have been yellin’
be the hour fer a packet iv candles, ’n’ how I’m
t’ reach ut down to them widout the sthring, .1
don’t know.” “Why, you blighted ass, put them in
the bucket!” roared Crowther. Ragan went away;
but he was back in an hour, knocking diffidently.
“Av ye plaze, sor,” he whined, “here’s King cursin’
all the saints in glory t’ come up frim the well be-
yant. Where d’ ye be lcapin’ the ladder at all?”
“Ladder !” cried Crowther. “Haven’t you got goat-
sense? Pull him up with the rope.” Ragan re-
peated his wail. “’Tis no use,” he whimpered.
“I’ve been thryin’ t’ do ut this ha’f hour, ’n’ there
ain’t an inch iv shkin lift on ayther hand iv me.”

“H. J.E.”: I can add a piece to this
argument concerning mind-over-matter cure for snake-
bite ; the incident came under my own notice in
India. Chunder was repairing the thatched roof of
his dilapidated residence one evening, when he sud-
denly dropped to the ground, howling that he had
been bitten by a snake. His sahib, a retired civil
officer, knowing the yellow streak in the Indian, de-
clared the undoubted snake punctures to be a rat bite,
and told Chunder to get out. Chunder begged and
implored to be treated for snake, while the boss
cursed him for a fool. The bluff came off, and when
Chunder was leaving the room, satisfied that he had
nothing worse than a rat wound, the sahib called
him back and said he would treat him for snake-bite
if he liked. Chunder thought it might be as Well,
but he didn’t want to be a trouble, since there was
nothing the matter with him. “No trouble,” said
the boss, sarcastically, and used the customary anti
dotes. Chunder was quite well in a couple of days,
and started on the thatch again. This time he used a
stick, and as he was poking about he disturbed a
cobra. With one affrighted yell the nigger fell
headlong from the roof, and died with all the
symptoms of cobra-bite.
* * , * *
“Gip”: Will someone please, invent a
rabbit-trap that will do its work without causing
bunny so much needless pain? Those now in use are
altogether too sharp and powerful, and I have known
of one to hold a dingo till it starved to death. Almost
invariably fur and flesh are torn away, and a length
of generally broken bone exposed; and unless the
traps are looked to every few hours, a big percentage
of the rabbits get away, minus half a limb. Some
trappers try filing the jaws of the traps, others pad
them with a rag (mostly with a utilitarian view,
I confess) ; but that shouldn’t be necessary. Couldn’t
some inventive mind think out a trap that will not
cut, but still hold its prey ?
“Jack Jay”: Maoriland’s handsome song-
bird, the tui, which had fled to the heart of the bush
before the gun-hog- and the fear of man generally,
has begun to return to the settled districts in small
numbers. This is partly due to increased confidence
in public protection, and partly because of the plen-
tiful supplies of honey in the flowers of the Aus-
tralian gums planted by many of the settlers. In
these eucalypt clumps the stranger is now frequently
to be seen in his black clothes and white choker
(hence his other name of “parson bird”), and
with his sweet, deep, bell-like note he more than pays
for his board and lodging.
VENUS DE SILO.
“Machete”: Dr. R. P. Jones, medical
officer at Samarai, puts down the prevalence of beri-
beri among Papuans to the rice which is the staple
kai-kai of many of them. Though this is only an
old theory revived, the doctor, who has had plenty
of experience of beri-beri in the Philippines, has a
good deal of evidence on his side. Beri-beri, which
is the peculiar scourge of the rice-eating nations of
the Orient, first appeared in Papua with the institu-
tion of indented native labor. The food of natives
carrying or working for miners, largely consists of
the easily-transported rice; and native vegetables
are hard to come by, especially in the foot-hills.
The native living on the. food to which he has been
accustomed all his life, never contracts beri-beri.
On the Gira River, where many imported rice-fed
boys died of beri-beri, there was no recorded in-
stance of the taro-eating northern natives suffering
from the disease. In the same way, white men,
with their fairly varied diet, very rarely afford a
case. The doctor seems to think that the extra
preparation given to “white rice” cuts out “sub-
stances necessary for normal nutrition of the nerve
tissues, while the defaulting elements are in super-
abundance.” What all that means is not quite
clear, but he seems to have barked his shins against
a lot of hard facts.
* * * *
“O. and E.”: It happened close to
Jacob’s Ladder, round the point from the Blowhole,
at Kiama (N.S.W.). The stadium was a shelving
rock, fronted and fringed with variously-colored
masses of seaweed. The large, brown crab came out
for his afternooon stroll, and I watched him with
interest. So did another. The crab moved along with
a philosophical air, when, like a flash, a couple of
long feelers, colored like the surroundings, shot out
from under the lower rock, and the crab was grabbed
and thrown on his back. Then the octopus appeared
with eyes bulging like door-knobs from his nest of
shells, and lay flabbily over his prey. But the prey
wasn’t taking any, and with a mighty effort righted
himself and laid hold with his nippers. There was
a sudden squeak as from an enraged parrot, and I
wondered where it came from. But the battle, like that
of Burns and Johnson, was too unequal, so I went
to the assistance of the crab. Catching a claw of
Burns, I hauled him and Johnson right up on to the
rock among the winkles, and Burns scored quite a
lot of points. Just as victory seemed assured, how-
ever, my fool fighter shed the limb I was holding,
and I fell into a pothole and broke my best pipe.
Was just up in time to see the bubbles arise from
below, while two tiny baby crabs rushed down to
look after their doomed parent. It must have been
the octopus that squeaked, as crabs don’t. Somehow
1 felt sorry for that crab —it was horrible.
“Petramos”: I am the man with the
musical spiders. Ravages of horrible earthquakes
have left extensive cracks in the ceiling of my
room, in which I play the piano. I play Chopin.
I maintain that if ■he heard me he would design
another Funeral March. Every time I play the
spiders come out. They are black, with furry legs.
1 am convinced that the fur is in imitation of the
manes adopted by famous musicians. I wrote a
composition, which I entitled, “The Plea of the
Orphan Fly.” The spiders were visibly affected.
They did not retire immediately I stopped, but re-
mained and held a mass meeting. It was evident
that a resolution was unanimously carried. That
was a week ago. To-day my room is full of flies.
Just now the corpse of a starved spider fell from the
ceiling with a faint, feathery thud.

“The Calathumpian Deacon”: The pas-
sionate plea entered by Sydney D.T. recently for the
keeping open, at endless expense, of the bar-bound
North Coast ' (N.S.W.) rivers, reminds me of the
cheerful cattle-owner I met in Sydney, and his tale
; of how he once got a shipment of horned beasts out
of the salted wet. His little mob were penned on
the forward deck of, a coaster, which struck heavy
weather, and arrived off the bar at a most unprom-
ising time. The captain had a good look at the
entrance and decided it was too dangerous to risk.
At this point, the cattleman, who could see the value
being knocked off his live-stock as they slipped on
the cinder-strewn deck, lurched up to the master-
mariner, and, explaining that he was anxious to get
his beasts ashore, offered to bet him £5 to nothing
that he couldn’t get in. “Right!” said the sporting
sailor, decisively, and with a whirl of the spokes
sent his tub like a fat steeplechaser at the water-
jump. By the grace (or oversight) of Providence,
they cleared the sand safely, and the grateful stock-
owner promptly paid up. The bar harbor is more of
a gamble than a serious commercial proposition.
“Pick”: “Condamine” (B. 27/4/T1) has
been led to doubt his moke’s homing capacity simply
through his own impatience. Like a dog, homing
pigeon, bee or any animal that instinctively finds
its way in strange country, or at night, a horse will
always make a few circuits to get his bearings be-
fore striking for home; but when he once starts off,
it is safe every time to bet drinks that, if left un-
guided, he will get there by the shortest possible
route, whether he has been across that country be-
fore or not. “Condamine,” by-the-bye, found his
own way so very easily after losing faith in his
“turkey ” that I suspect he was bushed in the home
paddock, within coo-ee of the sliprails, and probably
the mistrusted prad was still hunting round for one
of the cows that “Condamine” had missed.
THE HANGMAN’S JOKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“These are the drops,” the chemist said.
The customer said “Ta!
To-night I’ll early go to bed;
This dashed insomnia
Has made me weak as any cat!
Ah, well! I will not stop.
The dose?” (He hovered on the mat.)
The chemist said, “One drop!”
“’Old on!” broke in the furtive man
Who waited in the chair;
His fingers through his beard he ran
With a reflective air.
“If them there drops don’t send you off,
You come erlong to me.”
He gave a dry judicial cough
And nodded solemnly.
“And who are you ?” the chemist sneered
And o’er the counter leant.
At both the anxious patient peered
To find out what it meant.
The stranger, grinning through his grime,
Said in his whiskers, “Er—
I am —that is t’ say—well, I’m
An ole practitioner!
N.S.W.

“Condamine” : Gilligan was about full of
the shed, and wanted the sack. He tried all the
usual methods in vain, and when he commenced
hacking the wool the boss merely threatened to
prosecute him for damages. Then a brilliant idea
entered Gilligan’s head. He asked the butcher to
save him the teat-ends of a few ewes, and say no-
thing about it. A couple of days later, while the
pens were being counted out at smoko, Gilligan
pretended to be busy until the last of his sheep
were mixed with the mob. Then he came up and
inquired how many sheep were in his pen. “Four-
teen,” said the boss. “You’re a liar,” yelled Gilli-
gan. “I had a notion that you were rooking me
for a sheep or two, so L Cut a teat off each one as
I shore it, and here TOey are —fifteen of them.”
Gilligan was allowed to go.
“Leo Lear”: J. A. Leach, M.Sc. and
ornithologist, has driven a final 6in. spike into the
“land of songless birds” lie. According- to him —
Australia contains representatives of 28 families
of song-birds. Representatives of but 19 families
have been recorded from Great Britain. The Indian
Empire contains representatives of 22 families, North
America 22, and South America 23. Again, while
89 song-birds have been recorded as permanent resi-
dents of, or regular visitors to, Britain, almost 500
species of song-birds have, so far, been recorded from
Australia and Tasmania.
Considering that Britishers have been the chief
abusers of our birds, it is fitting and proper that the
first should be last and the last should be first, that
the Scriptures may be fulfilled and the Devil con-
founded.
“Billanine”: Has any Aboliar ever sent
in an authentic record of a kangaroo’s pace? If so
I would like to know how it tallies with my timing.
Motoring recently on a road near Manilla (N.S.W.),
we came upon a pair of the now-rare marsupials in a
straight lane. The ’roos set off, and we whizzed
right behind. For quite two miles the beasties
kept their pace and their lead, and when I looked at
the speedometer it registered 35 miles an hour. An-
other turn or two and we passed them after a better
two-mile race than I have ever seen on a racecourse.

“W. King”: Can’t agree with “Blackboy”
that the W.A. wild brumby is easy to catch, and I
have chased him at all seasons of the year. The best
time to gather him is in the dry season when the
waterholes in the hills are gasping. Track him then
to his few watering places, and securely, fence off
the moisture for, say, a week. At the end of that
period let him at the water, and when he has
filled himself he is as helpless as an aged Wowser
after a blow-out at a free tea and bun scuffle.

“10-2-4”: lie this Abo. controversy
as to whether the Australian nigger has a sense of
humor. Some years ago 1 was in a Baclcblocks
courthouse, when a Crown “defender” was repre-
senting a boy of the Binghi
breed on a charge of murder.
As usual in such cases, he
never had the semblance of a
run for his neck. His advo-
cate concluded a lengthy and
earnest, but utterly fatuous
effort, by calling a witness
whose testimony proved fatal
to whatever faint hopes Bing-
hi had of evading the rope.
Exasperated at the onlook for
evidence he was eliciting,
counsel turned, tiger-like, on
his witness, and subjected him
to a searching cross-examina-
tion concerning his past im-
moralities and convictions.
The Judge being almost stone-
deaf, and the Crown Prose-
cutor temporarily stunned by
his learned friend’s unprece-
dented line of action, the
now-maddened witness was
able to voice the views that
his interlocutor was a crim-
son idiot and his client a gory
assassin, before a “Stand
down! ” bumped him once
more into private life. His
sentiments coincided more or
less, seemingly, with those of the jury, who,
without leaving the box, returned a verdict of
“Wilful Murder.” Even as Binghi was being led
away to his doom, he turned, and, waving a sable
paw at his crestfallen defender, shouted cheerily:
“Buck up, boss. Nebber. you mind. You plurry
fool now. Bimeby you grow up and get sense like
plurry old deaf bloke on top. Then you do all
right.” “A callous villain” was the phrase the
local paper used to describe the condemned next
morning. There are some people who cannot recog-
nise a humorist when they meet him.

“T.O’C.”: “Petifi” B. 30/3/T1) asks if
any “leaping brother” can beat his pal’s little free
trip from Sydney to Melbourne and back. Yes, cer-
tainly. A few years ago, finding the climate of the
Mallee, Victoria, uncongenial, a mate and I de-
cided to migrate to the sugar fields of Queensland.
We “jumped the rattler,” where we discovered an-
other traveller under a tarpaulin, and reached Ten-
terfield, New England, with very few breaks. There
a limb of the Law introduced itself, but as it couldn’t
run three ways at once, only one of the syndicate
was caught. The remaining two went on to War-
wick (Q.), and waited there for the third partner to
arrive, when a fresh start was made. At Gympie, we
were all hauled out of a lavatory and handed three
months’ cold cell; and, the irony of it, conveyed
back to Brisbane to serve the sentence. Neverthe-
less, on a dark night two months later, three swags
were unobtrusively lowered from a railway truck
in the Childers railway yard, and our State-assisted
passage of over 1300 miles was accomplished. It
had taken about 13 weeks.
“Mimosa”: After some years’ experience
of both lots, I think the animals of Australia show
more affection for those of their kind than do the
natives of S’Africa. So far I haven’t met any
Australian beast that is forced adrift from its mob,
herd, or pack by its own fellows and relatives when
it becomes qualified for an old-age pension. On the
other hand, in S’Africa practically all animals, from
the jackal to the elephant, “drum out” their has-
beens, and, strangely enough, the veterans show not
the least inclination to return. The “Arctic” treat-
ment received from its own family, coupled with the
solitary life, is probably responsible for the increased
vice of the recluse, not only towards its fellow beings,
but towards man. By the way, I’m not sure about
dingoes: Can anybody tell me how they deal with
their toothless brethren?

“E. J.C.” : When I was at Gympie many
years ago the boys used a fuse and plug to depopu-
late the Mary River and its thickly-timbered creeks,
but I found on returning recently that the practice
had been scrap-heaped in keeping with progressive
ideas. The rifle bullet has supplanted the dynamite
plug. Your small boy waits for the shoal, fires
one shot slantwise, then strips and gropes for sick
and dead mullet. Judging from the bags I saw
it seems as effective as dynamiting, although I can’t
understand the reason. One youthful scientist oblig-
ingly dissected Mr. Mullet, and presented his much-
disturbed and blackened stomach in proof of a com-
pressed-air-concussion theory, but to me it did not
solve the problem as to why the rifle bullet should
sicken the fish. Will some contributor diagnose?
