“Vectis”: “Something to keep blowflies
off a horse’s injured leg?” asks “T.J.M.” (B.
11/5’11). Answer: A bandage. A wound on a part
that can’t be bandaged is a rather harder proposi-
tion. “T.J.M.” had better give up the useless
search for the universal blowfly-baffler. Some of the
winged horrors will attack anything that isn’t red-
hot. But practically it is just a question of taking
enough trouble. (If the horse isn’t worth it, sell
him.) Any strong-smelling antiseptic ( e.g lysol,
in proper dilution) will do the business, but it must
be used often and freely. Keep the animal in a
clean stable, and attend to him three times a day
or oftener. If “T.J.M.” had a wounded back, and
were turned into a paddock unclothed, and with
his hands tied, perhaps the blowflies would have a
good time with him. And he wouldn’t be much
better off if someone dabbed his wound with kero-
sene about twice a week. If only it were recognised
that the principles of surgical treatment are the
same for beasts as for men, there would be less piti-
able suffering.
“Jack” Anybody ever heard of one adult
bird spoon-feeding another? I know of a brown
weka ( Ocydromus earli ) which was born in the Wel-
lington (M.L.) Zoo. It has a malformed beak, its
upper and lower mandibles being crossed in such a
manner that it cannot pick up anything. Neverthe-
less, it is the fattest of a family of three, its plump
condition being due to the assistance of its mates,
who feed it with their own bills, thus setting a
nature-lesson for fat bishops. In Hutton and Drum-
mond’s “Animals of Maoriland” mention is made of
a huia ( Hetralocha acutirostis) , with a beak shaped
like a corkscrew. It could not possibly have picked
up food for itself, and, as it was evidently hatched with
the malformation and was fully grown, it- must have
been attended to in infancy by a pa and ma huia until
it was old enough for matrimony (the birds mate in
pairs), and was afterwards helped through life by
an affectionate hubby. The male and female of
these birds are so unlike that ornithologists at first
thought they belonged to different species, the bill
of the male being short and straight, while that of
the lady has a curve in it like the blade of Father
Time’s scythe. Saw a kaka ( Nestor meridionalis)
once that had had its beak shot off. As it was
fully grown and quite as fat as any other respectable
kaka, I conclude that it, too, must have been tuckered
by its relations. Once my nipper (which species, the
census tells us, is fast becoming extinct in M.L.)
brought home a young thrush (Alerula musica)
in a covered box. The mother was not in sight
when the boy abstracted the young ’un; neverthe-
less, within an hour, ma had arrived on the scene
and begun to feed her chick, at the same time en-
deavoring to persuade sonny to fly, which, owing to
his infancy, he was unable to do. This was con-
tinued for a day or so, the young ’un receiving all
the protection that I could give him, being left at
liberty to fly if he wished. After a while young
M. Musica dropped dead, and Mrs. M. M., with a
few plaintive cries, vanished. Have since been told
that Mrs. Thrush, when she cannot secure the re-
lease of her young, acts Virginius, and supplies her
offspring with poison berries.
* * * *
“Leo Lear” : What chiefly influences the
Aboscribe towards the purchase of a book when he
finds himself confronted with innumerable desirable
specimens ? The normal bloke, in other walks of
lifp, has his favorite author or publisher, but the
Aboriginalist is not normal. He is mostly a rudi-
mentary litterateur, with a strong liking for variety.
For my part, title, illustrations and color of
binding would decide the point, although illustra-
tions such as a Lindsay bear or a boiled “Hop” mis-
sionary would carry a bad title over Kosciusko. But
the color of the publication’s outer garments is the
thing. A dull green or a drab grey looks about
as encouraging as a wood-heap. Give me a good
flare-up like Sid Long’s “Flamingoes” in the Sydney
Art Gallery, interspersed with lines of blue and
stars of yellow—something that will catch the eye,
and compel you to grab the book to see if the
paint has soaked through to the middle. And even
if the reading matter of the volume is mud, there
is some compensation in having a decoration for
the book-slielf.

“Tas.” : Bill had a genial temper, but li n
couldn’t keep it when he lost at cribbage. One night
we set up a four-handed game, and Bill and partner
were badly beaten. “How d’ye feel now ?” I said to
Bill, chaffingly. “Like Lazarus,” he replied, with his
temper in a knot. “Like Lazarus?” I says. “What
do you mean?” “Licked by dogs,” were the laconic
words of William,
“Wang” : This is how I dodge the mos-
quitoes, anyhow. I hang up two or three paper
masks that the boys use on G. Fawkes day, and
smear a bit of honey on them. The airopests, as
they arrive, are attracted by the bee fruit, drive
their torpedoes into the masks, and are held fast.
In the morning I go round with a saw and decapitate
the projections. The owners never return.

CARES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I don’t know if I care or not
(Moreover, I don’t care to know)
About the things that plague a lot
Of simple folk, and give them woe,
Or hopelessness, or fear or doubt;
I’ve not a lot to care about.
My ehiefest care is not to care.
For anything that care can give;
The clothes of life I well can spare:
The naked fact that 1 can live
Is all I want—in nakedness,
The less to care for I possess.
It strikes me as a foolish thing
That he who owes and cannot pay
Should make that cause for worrying;
The care should lie the other way ;
Or, if his creditors don’t care,
Why should he for his debts despair?
And what a simpleton is he
Who cares for one who does not care,
Ami frets his heart out grievously
To do her worship 1 When a pair
A care for one another share,
I do not mind that kind of care.
I do not care for cares ; and though
My carelessness a larger share
Of cares may bring, they give no woe;
Since I refuse to care for care,
I dodge all cares as best I can—
I never was a careful man.
N.S.W.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
John Wood Fleming, said to be the oldest
native of Victoria, was 74 on May 30, He is a
councillor of Brunswick, having been elected 50
years ago. He has been mayor six times.
Two of the three £lO,OOO Dreadnoughters
are now gone—Sam Hordern and “ KiHarney ”
Buchanan. Abbey Dangar alone remains. He
may get the tag yet, though it is a long time
coming.
“X ” : Dapper John Madden was a Wax-
cellency ; the bucolic Carmichael an Oxcellency.
What were the others ? I remember more than
one Hoaxcellency, and here and there a deplor-
able Hiccellency. You may even recollect a
Hogcellency.
R. J. Campbell, the beauty parson of
London, is booked for a trip to Australia. Camp-
bell combines pulpit originality with faded hair
and burning eyes, and makes more than a success
of it. He is the father of the absorbing theory
that Adam didn’t fall, and wasn’t even pushed.
Senator McGregor announces that he is the
27th direct lineal descendant of King Fergus I. of
Ireland and Scotland. King O’Malley, being of
Irish extraction, is, of course, descended from
Brian Boru—every Irishman is. The Australian
Labor Party is getting too aristocratic.
Amongst those who watched N.S.W.
Lieut.-Gov. Cullen “open” his first Parliament
the other day was Mrs. Eliza Jane White, widow
of R. H. D. White, M.L.C., who was a grandson
of Robert Hoddle, the first Surveyor-General of
Victoria and the godfather of the city of Mel-
bourne. Mrs. White is a daughter of the late
Dean Cowper, and mother of the Lieut.-Gov.’s
wife. She married Mr. White in May, 1863, long
before he succeeded in wresting his large fortune
from grandfather Hoddle.
THE ART
CRITIC.
OROVER BILL:
"Not a patch
on the bloke at
La Perouse!”
The most inveterate intei'jectors in the
N.S. W. Parliament, which suffers badly from the
interjector nuisance, are little Fitzparrot, who is
inane; and “Jack ” Meehan, who is minatory;
and Wood, who is spiteful. If a vote were taken,
the House would vote Wood’s interjections the
most intolerable, chiefly because they are the
worst-tempered. His splinters almost always
provoke retorts, though when the other night he
exclaimed ‘‘ I know nothing ! ” there wasn’t a
word said on the other side.
Miss Emilia Hawson, j ust dead in Adelaide
at 74, was a daughter of Henry Hawson, who
had the unusual distinction of coming to
Australia in his own ship, being a man of
wealth, in spite of having six sons and
seven daughters. This was in 1837.
He had a look at Sydney, Perth
and the infant Adelaide, but
finally adopted a widespread
opinion of the day, that Port
Lincoln was S.A.’s de-
stined capital, and took up
land there. There were
“bad niggers” in those
parts then. One brother,
a boy of 12, was found
with two spears in him.
He had lighted a fire,
and was burning the
ends off so as to be
able to ride home !
The wounds were,
however, mortal ;
and Port Lincoln
lias lately put up a
monument over his
remains. TheHamps
were neighbors, and
the sons of that
family, coming home
one night, had the
dreadful experience
of finding their
father’s head in the
camp-oven when
they went to look
there for supper.
Kink O’Malley
lately broke out in a
new spot, and sub-
sisted for 10 days on
three quarts of water
per day as a cure
for headaches, from
which lie has suf-
fered for 20 years.
What O’Malley
really wants, how-
ever, is to learn to
do without water,
not food, in view of
a possible prolonged
residence at Yass-
berra.
Makea, the stout old lady who has for
many years past been queen, or ariki, of Raro-
tonga, Maoriland’s foreign possession, lately died,
aged 72. The Federal Council will probably
appoint Rangi, a former candidate, as her suc-
cessor.
Lecturing lately on “Life in Ancient
Athens,” Professor Tucker, of Melbourne Uni.,
said: “One pleasing scene of quiet domestic life is
recorded for us in the marble of the time.” Then
he tapped for the lantern picture, and the operator
threw upon the screen—a ferocious bout be-
tween two wrestlers, one of whom had got the
other down !
From Sydney Telegraph :
Had he lived with the illustrious brotherhood of the
earlier period mentioned, Gilbert would have frequented
smart clubs and been dined frequently in titled company.
From which it may be assumed that when Dill
Snowball dies the Telegraph will commiserate
with him in that he never managed to secure an
audience with the Pope.
Somebody got away with Police Commis-
sioner O’Callaghan’s overcoat from Moonee
Valley (Melb.) racecourse the other day. Proba-
bly it was the purloiner’s only successful invest-
ment at the meeting, and he simply took it
because he wanted it badly. Therein he differed
from O’Callaghan and his men. They rarely take
anybody or anything, no matter how badly
wanted the body or the thing may be.
“ Diogenes ” looks for the Original Wow-
ser:—
I think he was John Tawell. Anyhow, if John was not
the Original Wowser, he was certainly an O.W. John
was a good young man of London Town, and did his best
to grow a face that looked like a hymn book. He
aspired to the hand of his master's housemaid, and made
good. Just at the time John was most assiduous in his
benevolence towards the collection-plate he got found
out. Tlie incidental discoveries were sufficient to hang
John, but hts previous fervency saved him, and instead
of going to Heaven at the end of a rope he came to Aus-
tralia in a convict ship. John soon got a ticket-of-leave
from Governor Macquarie and set up as a chemist in
Hunter-street. He waxed rich, and was joined by his
wife. Then he started in on the Wowser business again.
He built a church, donated £3O annually to a religious
school, and emptied 600 gallons of rum into Sydney Har-
bor to encourage temperance. Whilst his wife was' ill she
was attended by a nurse. John started aspiring to the
nurse's hand, and aspired with very good success. He
kept the nurse in seclusion till the wife died, when he
started aspiring again, and wedded a widow. Possibly
feeling that he was behaving badly towards the nurse in
keeping her in seclusion, he administered prussic acid.
So John went to glory on his birthday in the year 1845.
A. G. Hales, who once drove an Australian
pen that could paint purple patches but couldn’t
spell to save its life, was at latest seeking color for
more pot-boilers in South America. Hales,
having done a good deal of war-correspondent’s
work, is nowadays a more militant “ Smiler” than
ever, and he writes to a Buenos Ayres paper to
say that the U-S. and Japan must come to holts
shortly, wherefore he wants all Australians in
S. America to get ready to chip in for Uncle Sam: —
We Australians may kick in times of peace against our
Yankee cousins, but in time of trouble we can be counted
on to the last man. In this respect I should be very-
pleased to get a note from every Australian in the Argen-
tine, not for publication, but that I might show the
American authorities that Australian sentiment is solid
in time of danger. If Roosevelt does mobilise his Rough
Riders, there is nothing under Heaven that I would like
better than to take two or three hundred Anglo-Saxons
from these parts to the “ Old Man,” and say, “ Sir, here
we are, such as we are, try us out.”
Whereupon, no doubt, the “Old Man ” would say,
“ Thank you kindly. And now can we sell you
some canned beef ? ”

John James Moikle, the Maorilander who
for many years thumped a drum through the
islands to recruit public sympathy for his claim
to be compensated for false imprisonment, has
gone to London in the hopes of raising more
trouble. Meikle has been paid considerable
amounts by the M.L. Government, and last year
was voted £2500 by Parliament in full and final
payment. But as the Government had proposed
to pay £5OOO, he considers that he has been de-
frauded, and is now seeking a means to drag the
British Government into the argumeiit. This
paper figures it: Meikle + His Chance of Getting
that Extra £2500 = Buckley’s.
Prof. Laurie, who recently resigned the
chair of Logic and Philosophy in Melbourne Uni-
versity, is an old inky-
vvayfarer. He arrived in
Vic., an LL.D. of Edin-
burgh, in the late ’6o’s,
and bought the Warrnam-
bool Standard. He in-
duced the proprietor of the
Examiner to amalgamate
with him, and the twain
became one under the
editorship of the learned
doctor. A little later hjs
partner, one Fairfax,
withdrew, and Laurie be-
came sole proprietor. In
1882 he was chosen to
lecture in Logic at the
University, and three cr
four years later he sat in
the chair of Logic and
Philosophy. He still kept
on the inky way, and until
a short while ago wrote
leaders and reviews for
the Australasian. It is
said that the Prof., who is
still full of mental vigor,
proposes to devote a good deal of time to litera-
ture of a solid brand.
Inclement Wragge scored a point over the
authorised expeditions despatched to various
localities to view the solar eclipse of April 29. He
formed himself into a private expedition to the
Friendly Islands, where he observed the pheno-
mena in a perfect sky what time the official par-
ties elsewhere battled with an obscurity due to
sudden cloud formation. The British expedition
was quite unsuccessful.
South Aus. has a Scotchbyterian, Rev. E.
11. Ross, who believes that the best way to argue
with, a man with a gun is to have a gun yourself,
and know how to use it. Ross therefore applied
for a commission as second lieutenant in the
infantry. But, taking parsons at their own valu-
ation and classifying them with the women and
children, the Soustr ilian Commandant refused to
recommend the Board at Headquarters to grant
the application. Precedent was with the Com-
mandant ; but the Board, at its next meeting, ran
the office sword through the heart of that ancient
warrior, and recorded its opinion that any parson
possessed of the necessary physical and other
qualifications may receive a combatant com-
mission.
In view of his cutting style, the Acting-
Prime Minister is to be christened Billy Hews.
Yung-Liang-Hwang, the new Consul-
General for Australia, went through a University
course in America, and speaks English in a fluent,
florid fashion. He professes Christianity of the
Methodist brand, and may yet be seen on some
Pleasant Sunday Afternoon in double harness
with Joe Cook or P.M.G. Thomas.
L. M. Isitt, Maoriland’s apostle of Cold
Tea and, incidentally, of sly grog, has gained a
verdict with £5OO damages and costs against a
trades protection association of Christchurch.
The society black-listed Isitt on the information
of a chemist, who denounced the Chief Wowser as
a bad pay; and its unavailing defence was
“truth, no malice and privilege.” Some law
points have, however, to be threshed out before
Isitt can wear the plaster on his injured feelings.
The clothes of John Fuller, Victoria’s
new Governor, are not so full of Fuller as they
might be—which is to say that he suffers from
the same sloppy complaint as recent Governor
Carmichael in the early stages of his “govern-
ing ” career. Later on, the whole Carrnike
family spruced up tremendously, and left looking
a brighter, more blooming and more prosperous
bunch than when they arrived.
J. A. Millar, Maoriland’s Minister for
Marine, etc., has broken out as an inventor ; and
his patent covering a collapsible rowlock has been
taken up by Stone and Sons, Admiralty contrac-
tors. The rowlock can’t be lost in a capsize, but
it can be thrown below the level of the gunwale.
Millar has been talking lately of seeking a peace-
ful life outside politics; and perhaps the profits
of his invention will run to a fig-tree and some
cucumbers, with an occasional whisky and half a
sov. for the “tote.”
Died a few days ago Frederick Bevill,
M.A., who in the ’Bo’s opened a school in Dar-
linghurst (Sydney) called Royston College, much
patronised by the New Jerusalem which has
grown up in the adjacent village of Potts Point.
Later on he threw up the college, and became
secretary to the Publicans’ Union. Bevill, who
was about the stoutest man in Sydney in middle
age, and as such was not the ideal dominie for
irreverent schoolboys, began life as a journalist,
and went to Japan as special for a London paper
Norman Cameron, brother of the soldier-
Senator and a one-time stormy petrel of Tas.
Assembly and later of the Representatives, is
again offering himself to his country after four
years’ compulsory retirement. The Camerons
have perhaps as much solid wealth as any family
in Tasmania. Notwithstanding that, the people
who asked him to stand sent the request in a
reply-paid telegram. In opening his campaign
the other night, Norman said this fine ponsidera-
tion had deeply touched him, and helped to
melt his firm resolve not to forgive his country’s
previous base ingratitude.
Sam McCaughey’s action in promptly
handing in his Federal land-tax, “ with gratitude
to the country which had done so much for him,”
brings back the yarn that was prevalent in the
Riverina district when James Ashton fathered
the State land-tax. At that time Ashton was
running the Narrandera Argus ; and when the
big squatter was called upon to pay, the story
was that he wrote to the Argus to the effect
that, owing to the heavy ini oads made upon his
income, he would be no longer able to continue as
a subscriber. Ashton replied that he was sorry
any action of his should cripple the squatter, and
that he had given instructions to have him put on
the free list.
LIGHTENING THEIR DARKNESS.
The Corporation of the City of London, the city
companies and business houses are spending £100,000
on illuminations in connection with the Coronation
festivities.— Cable.

George A. Jury, the Adelaide warehouse-
man, who owned the Flinders-lane (Melb.)
building consumed by fire the other day,
is managing partner of G. and It. Wills.
Thirty-eight years ago Jury joined the
firm as a clerk. There had been only three
managing partners, but in 12 years he had
not only turned himself into a fourth, but had
married the daughter of one of the other three.
As a clerk he worked alongside Hayward, the
founder of the big John Martin firm. “Jimmy 1 ’
Marshall and Taylor, of the next-biggest Rnndle-
street concern, came to Australia at about £vo a
year to work for one Northmore, an early draper,
who fell foul of the daily paper ad. rates and
established his own rag.
RECOMPENSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Obit. 30/5/’ll: Sir W. S. Gilbert, Knight.
He gave a dull and stilted age
A store of quaint, delightful quips ;
Pretentiousness and snobbery
And sham he lashed with scorpion-whips.
His magic people, elfish, gay,
Absurdly sane and gravely mad,
Shared with the sun the gracious gift
Of making all the nations glad.
He proved that England still can breed
A wit whose touch is sure and light;
And, at the end, his country turned
And made her Wonder-Child—a Knight!
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

This bank was in a very small way, though it was
doing a very profitable business, when the dreadful
t’othersider broke into the West, turned up gold,
and started to make Cinderella get busy. In 1890
the capital was only £60,000, and the dividend
was 174 p.e. Naturally, paying a div. of that size,
the bank was able to command a big premium for
any new shares it issued; and every new issue has
thus been made to add to the reserves. The average
paid for the £lO shares has been somewhere about
£26. The present paid capital is £200,000, so
that on the majority of the shares the 20 p.e. divi-
dend really means only a shade over 7{ p.e. Now it
is proposed to send a new lot of shares to market,
the meeting held the other day having authorised
the directors to issue another 5000 ( £50,000),
though the terms have not yet been disclosed. Pre-
sumably, however, the established practice of offer-
ing them to shareholders at a fixed premium won’t
be departed from; the plan of putting them up to
tender has been tried, but doesn’t seem to be popular.
There is really no call to get in more capital so
far as the strength of the bank is concerned. It at
present owes the public altogether £3,273,671, and
its capital and reserves combined total £747,464,
so there is less than £4 Bs. of public money for
every £1 of the shareholders’ money; and that is a
very moderate proportion indeed. Further, even of
the comparatively small amount of public money, no
less than 48 p.e. is in liquid assets ; and, of course,
the greater the proportion of liquid assets the
smaller the risk. Nevertheless, though there may be
no urgent call for more share capital, if share-
holders want to make security the first consideration,
they are following the right policy.
Some stupid person at the meeting the other day,
in proposing that the new shares be issued, said that
if the Referenda vote hadn’t been “No, No,” he
would “certainly not have consented to the increase
of capital or taken up any shares.” The same sort
of foolish talk was heard in the West 12 years ago,
when the same brand of foolish people foretold dis-
aster ; even the late Speaker of the Assembly blath-
ered that Bigjohn Forrest deserved to be boiled in
fat or something of the sort for helping to hand the
State over to the Eastern plunderers. Yet in 10
years this bank has shown the following changes:—
1901. 1911.
Notes and bills .. .. £182,192 £296,465
Free deposits .. .. 1,006,302 1,467,481
Deposits at interest .. 728,702 1,509,725
Liquid assets 1,283,982 1,577,062
Advances 913,134 2,390,072
Premises 53,235' 74,000
And the net profit, which was £11,787 for the half-
year to March, 1901, was £29,066 for the half-year
to March, 1911. However, and notwithstanding such
figures, the foolish person before mentioned has a
perfect right to refuse to put up his capital for
banking purposes. In that case it is just as well
that he and others like him should understand that
banking facilities will be provided even though they
won’t provide them. This sort of gentleman really
intimates to the Labor Government that, if the
Labor policy is adopted, Private Enterprise can’t be
depended upon to put up money for banking. Which,
of course, supplies the Labor Government with all
the argument it needs for establishing a Common-
wealth State Bank. Some people don’t know when
to hold their tongues.
Like most banks, the Westralian has had plenty of
money pushed towards it during the year, and—
mainly on account of agricultural deVelopment—
it has got customers for more than half of it—prac-
tically for all the fixed deposits. There no doubt
will be many farming failures in the West; but the
land prices are at present so low that there can’t be
any bad smash. This bank, moreover, hasn’t shown
much affection for the outlying parts, where success
is most doubtful. Aggregate figures as to March,
1910, and March, 1911, follow:
Mar., TO. Mar.,’ll.
Notes in circulation.. £147.399 £146,661 Dec. £738
Bills, etc 153,140 149,804 Dec. 3,336
Free deposits.. ..1,231,884 1,467,481 Inc. 235,597
Deposits at interest. 1,114,657 1,509,725 Inc. 395,068
Liquid assets .. .. 1,283,754 1,577,062 Inc. 293,308
Advances .. ..2,031,783 2,390,072 Inc. 358,289
Premises .. .. 68,950 74,000 Inc. 6,050
Having the use of comparatively little public money,
the interest return calculated on combined capital
and reserves is only moderate; it was at the rate of
£7 17s. 6d. p.e. for the past half-year. A low in-
terest return often suggests that some of the assets
are over-valued, or are not productive; but there
are plenty of other satisfactory explanations in this
case. Among other things, the low figure at which
premises stand in the books suggests that a poliev
of conservative valuation is followed. And the
policy of strengthening the reserves by issuing all
shares at a stiff premium is evidence of the same
satisfactory kind.

The Sydney City Council began this undertaking
with practically no goodwill. That is to say, all the
capital it borrowed and put into the concern was
represented by substantial and tangible assets. The
only “assets” of the other sort were a few small
preliminary expenses. At that time there were four
private cos. carrying on electric light and power
business in Sydney—the Empire, Strand, Imperial
and Oxford-street, each confined to its own little
section of the city. The municipal and private
mains trailed along in front of the same premises,
and, apart from that waste, there was a ridiculous
loss in administration expenses and in upkeep, etc. ;
for the municipal plant was capable of supplying
the whole lot. An arrangement was, therefore,
made to buy out the private concerns, a start being
made with the Empire Co. The very satisfactory
arrangement made with that concern served as a
basis for the other deals. The council didn’t buy
tile plant, but it bought the business at a fixed price.
The customers were transferred to the council, and
all revenue in excess of Id. per unit is paid to the
co. in extinction of the capital debt; interest on
the unpaid balance is also payable in some cases.
The total amount now owing and repayable in that
way is £56,956. The amounts owing at various
periods are shown next:—
1908. 1909. 1910.
Empire (purchase money) £17,650 £12,222
Strand (ditto and interest) 40,103 39,190 £32,248
Imperial ( „ ~ ) 17,074 18,778 17,422
Oxford-st. ( „ ~ ) 10,086 9,270 7,286
Total owing £84,913 £79,460 £56.956
The bargain with the Imperial Co. turned out a
little better during the past year; in the previous
year the excess revenue from the transferred cus-
tomers did not suffice to pay interest on the capital
owing, so that the indebtedness to the old co. actually
increased.
The reserves, it will be noted, include a Deprecia-
tion Fund; also a Renewals Fund has just been
started. As usual, insufficient provision was made
at the outset to cover depreciation ; but in 1909 the
council thought over its sins, and, getting some good
advice, decided to put by an annual 3 p.c. of the
capital outlay. Figures were quoted here to show
that other and older electric light and power con-
cerns—that of Melbourne, for instance—had found
even 3 p.c. insufficient. Apparently the council, ad-
vised by what seems to be a very good committee,
has come to the conclusion that it should be more;
hence, no doubt, the special reserve for special re-
newals. The only other complaint to make is that
the Depreciation and Renewal Funds are not in-
vested separately, like the Sinking Fund. This paper
admits that all this is in accordance with practice;
but that doesn’t make it right. The object of a de-
preciation or renewal fund is to provide the means
to buy new plant when the old wears out; and you
can’t buy new plant with a fund which has already
been spent in extensions. Anyway, since these funds
are more or less a set-off against actual depreciation,
they are not a true reserve; in other words, the
undertaking isn’t really as much to the good as the
figures indicate. Nevertheless, it has made very
good progress indeed. The object of the very
gradual Sinking Fund (1 p.c. p.a.) is. of course, to
repay the various Cohens who lent the council the
money to build the works, etc. Some time after the
middle of this century, if things go all right, the
city will really own the business. A 1 p.c. sinking
fund, invested at 31- p.c., will wipe out a debt in
43$ years.

and contingencies. It is a reasonable margin.
Suburban municipalities begin to see the advant-

to supply light and power at cost price, plus a
margin for sinking fund and contingencies. So the

So the co. last year got rid of a fair amount of the
old dead horse, and the liabilities have now been re-
duced to £6603. When the smash came, close upon
20 years ago now, the total was £254,000. But the
reduction has cost the shareholders pretty severely.
There are 28,092 shares, which were gradually called
up to £5 ; and then the capital was written down
to 30s. Also, the old debenture-holders had to put
up with the reduction of their interest to 3 p.c.
Things have, however, gradually righted themselves
more or less, and for 1908 the first of the new series
of divs. was paid. Last year, in the hope of pushing
off properties faster, book-values were reduced, and
the reserve fund was also written down to make
things balance. In other words, the reserve fund to
that extent was mere froth, which shows what a re-
serve fund in a concern of this sort may be really
worth. The bulk of the property is leasehold—-
leaseholds represent £47,225 of the total property
item of £56,051 —but the book-value is being re-
duced by an annual appropriation of £2OOO, which
is not shown as profit. Even after making that
standing charge, the leaseholds show a return of
about 7 p.c.; so the co. isn’t by any means in the
same position as one whose assets consist mainly of
the suburban block which merely gapes for someone
to come along and buy it, and meantime only collects
jam tins and rate notices.

This is the wreck of an old land-boom co. Most
others long ago had a revaluation, and cut their
losses by writing off capital. This one hasn’t had a
cleaning-up, though something of the sort now seems
probable. The original trouble was, of course, a big
debt-close upon £300,000. This money was in
shrinkable securities; but, as usual, though the
securities shrank, the debt didn’t, and the share-
holders had to stand the racket. Much of the pro-
perty was suburban land—the sort that brings in
nothing but ratepapers. Hence, though the property
assets are supposed to still represent over £48,000,
the total amount received in rents and interest last
year was only £449. It is all very sad. Also, it is
all very well worth remembering.

1911 .. 11,835 9 „ ~ 6338 100 49,618
The distressful results of Socialistic legislation and
of Federation and Protection are manifest here.
The country never has been so prosperous; and the
city has had a bigger slice out of that prosperity
than it ought to have had. So there has been a very
vigorous suburban building campaign, for which
several things have been responsible: the marriage
rate has improved (a sure sign of prosperity) ; muni-
cipal taxation on unimproved values has made subur-
ban estates available at a much lower price than they
certainly would have soared to if it hadn’t been for
that tax; and increased pay, due to Wages Boards,
has decided many a man to venture into a cabin of
his own. So this lending co. has been having a very
much better time; advances have moved along like
this:—
1908 .. £224,725 I 1910 .. £247,725
1909 .. 232,259 | 1911 .. 262,306
The eo.’s own cauital is only £70,425 ; the balance
(apart from that supplied by reserves) comes from
deposits, £179,242. This large debt, practically
all represented by shrinkable securities, constitutes a
pretty considerable risk, of course; but it means
large profits so long as things go well. When they
don’t go well, the co. is liable to have property
thrown on its hands, and then the large reserves may
come in very handy. Still, the advances are all
understood to be lent out on building-society terms
of repayment; so unless the slump is very sudden
and very sharp, there shouldn’t be much loss. Alto-
gether, the return looks remarkably, good in com-
parison with the risks. The future is in more doubt.
The State has hitherto done practically nothing in
N. S. Wales towards assisting working people to be-
come their own landlords; but it is now following
in the footsteps of S. Australia, and if the result in
N. S. Wales is the same as it is in S. Australia,
there won’t be any 9 or 12 p.c. to be made out of
this class of business by private concerns. As to
the 12 p.c. which appears occasionally in the table,
the co. reviews the position every third year, and,
if it thinks it can afford it, makes a distribution of
part of the surplus.

As to this last line, it may be that or it may be
something else ; the accounts are presented in so un-
satisfactory a way that one can’t be sure. Formerly
there was a Replacement and Reparation Fund,
which 12 months ago was in credit £12,608, and
another £1344 has since been transferred to it. The
furniture was revalued as at March 31 this year,
and the management took the very proper course of
writing down the book value, making the accounts
square by writing a corresponding amount off the
Replacement Fund. Apparently this left a balance
of £ 2400 to the credit of the fund ; and that amount
was no doubt transferred to the formal reserve fund,
thus wiping out the Replacement Fund altogether.
The reserves are now made up as follows: —Mortgage
Reduction Fund, £11,500 ; Assets Contingency
Fund, £10,613; Reserve Fund, £5432; unappro-
priated profit, £3824. The year, then, shows a
profit of £5597 ; but from that something must be
written off for current wear and tear —say, the £1344
which has been appropriated to the Replacement
Fund, as previously mentioned. Like most Mel-
bourne hotel and coffee palace cos., this one carried
a large plaster; 10 years ago the mortgage was
£65,000. The job of shifting it has been very slow ;
so, though £2BlO more capital has been got in, the
debt still amounts to £56,000. The position has
changed in 10 years as follows:
1901. 1911.

That is a reduction of £13,790, of which £10,980
has been from surplus profit and £2BlO from calls;
and meantime some small additions and improve-
ments have been made. The profit for the past year
is much better than the average for recent years,
this being the sort of season when uncle does the
thing properly on his visit to town.

Besides the gas business this co. does a little electric
lighting; but it writes so much off for repairs, re-
newals and depreciation of plant that the electric
light department never shows more than a few
pounds of profit. Curiously, though so much is
written off in this department, the sum invested in
the fixed assets (plant, etc.) never diminishes. Ap-
parently the co. is building up a plant more or less
out of revenue. No doubt the co.’s policy has been
to show that electric lighting isn’t worth bothering
about in Hobart. However, if the scheme for har-
nessing up the Great Lake comes off—and Parlia-
ment has passed the Bill, and J. Bull is to be asked
to join various Hobart people in putting up the
money-—the Gas Co. may have to worry a lot. On
the plans, estimates, etc., prepared, Hobart can be
supplied with electric light and power at a remark-
ably low rate; and the time may come when the
Hobart Gas Co. will find itself in very much the
same sad position as the Launceston Co., which also
had the misfortune to be bumped by cheaply-produced
electricity.

and renewals haven’t been shamefully neglected.
Taranaki (M.L.) is quite sure that it is
going to see a big J. Bull co. formed to buy its Petro-
leum Co.’s property. J. D. Henry has put up £2OOO,
and that settles it. Henry certainly has considerable
influence in London, and there’s been a pot of money
made lately in oil-specs, in Asia Minor and there-
abouts, so the chances are that the co. will be floated.
The price to be paid is a minimum of £50,000 cash
(more is to be paid in certain events), and £50,000
in shares in a co. with not more than £400,000
capital.
J. D. Henry, by-the-way, inspected the Common-
wealth Oil Corporation’s property while he was in
Australia, on his way back from Maoriland, but his
opinions on that bloated enterprise don’t appear to
have been made public. The corporation’s annual
meeting is overdue. Apparently, the London manage-
ment is holding it off until it can have word of the
starting of the new oil-making retorts. Then, no
doubt, there will be fireworks, in the midst of which

should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings o\
flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply to the Australian Agents,
W. & J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. Hurst-
house and Co., of Wellington, the agents in New
Zealand.*

Professor Richards, in the revised edition of his
celebrated edition of “Ore Dressing,” 1909, devotes
11 pages to the Wilfley concentrating table, and,
among other things remarks (p. 1465) : “The first
table sold was installed in the Puzzle Mill, at Breck-
enridge, Colorado. Since then over 12,000 have been
sold. This machine has revolutionised ore-dressing,
and as such is a phenomenal success. The original
form, and forerunner of all modern shaking tables
with longitudinal riffles . . has been greatly improv-
ed.” And further on (p. 1478) remarks: “One feature
of the Wilfley table to which especial attention should
be called is its ability to separate sands from slimes
while making the ordinary concentration. This sep-
aration is more complete and thorough than can be
made by any classifier, and should be taken advant-
age of in designing mills.” In the extensive addi-
tions just made to the Golden Horseshoe Mine (W.A.)
a large number of Wilfley Concentrators have been
installed. The Austral Otis Engineering Company,
Limited, South Melbourne, have the sole manufac-
turing rights.* [1

Heartrending particulars of Golden Horse-
shoe’s backsliding, as set out in the last three
annual reports:—
1908. 1909. 1910.
Ore reserves, tons 1,065,409 1,071,638 803,151
Gold conlents, oz. .. 633,577 693,924

The drop of over a million sterling in the value of
estimated ore reserves since the end of 1908 is bad
enough, but this tremendous shrinkage may be a
mere circumstance compared with the dismal fact
that it is only by making no allowance whatever
for depreciation, by placing all new plant to credit
as an asset, and by putting two-thirds of the amount
expended on development into the balance-sheet as
an asset, that the co. is able to show a surplus of
£75,294 for 1910. That amount is only £394
more than the dividend which the Board unwisely
paid in April, 1910. The cash position also shows
how the co. has gone from bad to worse during the
year just ended. It started with £55,959, and
raised £lOO,OOO on debentures; total, £155,959.
Wiping off the whole of the £70,500 now figuring
as an asset for plant, there is a balance of £85,459 ;
yet the co. winds up with only £29,202 in cash,
and on the other side there are liabilities as fol-
lows: Sundry creditors, £37,526 ; unclaimed divi-
dends, £.1783; and the aforesaid debentures,
£lOO,OOO. Only a few words are said in the direc-
tors’ statement about the exhaustive critical report
on the property by the French expert Ivuss, and no
mention is made of the promised reply by the
general-manager. Moreover, the directors add no-
thing by way of confirmation or otherwise to the re-
mark in their January circular that the latest news
from the mine enabled them to “indulge a confident
hope that such a satisfactory monthly output will
soon be reached as will lead up to an early return
to a dividend-paying position.” Altogether, in fact,
there is a pronounced air of “like it or lump it”
about the whole business; and that gives room for a
suspicion that the real position is stiß being withheld
from shareholders.
North Ivalgurli (W.A.) has abandoned its
option at Bullfinch. A fair quantity of gold is
still being won from the co.’s Kalgoorlie property,
the tributors’ output for the six months ended March
31 aggregating 54060 z., on which the co. pocketed
£3724 royalty.
* * -* *
Oroya Exploration and Oroya-Black
Range (W.A.) have put their heads together and de-
cided to form a separate concern to acquire their
jointly-owned Youanme leases. The new co. will be
known as the Yuanmi Gold Mines, Ltd., and will
have an authorised capital of £350,000 in shares of
£1 each, of which 280,000 shares, credited with
18s. 6d. paid, will be exchanged for the property, and
70,000 shares will be reserved for working capital.
It is intended to erect a 20-head mill now and double
the plant later on. *
Word comes from London that the Wes-
tralia United Goldfields flotation got ice from the
London public. Subscriptions were invited at par
for 700,000 • participating preference shares of 10s.,
but from what The Bulletin can learn the number
of shares applied for was so small that the applica-
tion money may about balance the advertising bill.
Now that the attempt to sell the properties to J.
Bull has failed, they will probably be abandoned, as
no one else is likely to bother about them.
* M * *
Ida 11. (W.A.) has made a change in
mine management, and in view of what the direc-
tors have been told about the value of the ore at
depth, and the necessity for doing certain work in
order to reduce working costs, the co. has decided to
raise £20,000 by the issue of an additional 80,000
ss. shares. The Board has already provisionally
accepted an offer for 20,000 of these shares at Bs.
per share, and shareholders are to have the right of
applying for the remaining 60,000 at the same price.
Should the total number be taken up, £32,000 ad-
ditional capital will be provided, making the total
issued capital £BO,OOO.
Lancefield (W.A.) is said to have issued
a circular in London postponing the shareholders’
meeting until the co. has had time to look around and
find some fresh excuse for its inability to earn pro-
fits. The reorganised plant is now able to treat
10,000 tons monthly, but for various reasons the
mine is behindhand with development, and is not in
a position to keep the mill properly fed. Under the
most favorable conditions the ore is only likely to
yield a small profit per ton, and towards this goal
the co. has been struggling for years. So far, how-
ever, for every step it has taken forward it has slid
two steps or thereabouts backward.
Gwalia South and Golden Pole (W.A.),
which practically belong to the same people, made
two attempts lately to hold half-yearly meetings;
but as nobody except the directors and a few friends
entered an appearance, the gatherings were first ad-
journed and then lapsed.
Accounts from Dunn’s Find (W.A.) are
glum. Elated at the gaudy reports which came to
hand about the gold in sight, one of the directors
went over to the West and engaged an expert en
route. What he discovered led him to upend the
title, and re-name it “Find’s Done.”
* * * *
There are a few big low-grade shows
south of Southern Cross and out to Parker’s Range
(W.A.). The biggest so far is the Great Victoria,
not far south of Marvel Loch and Jaccoletti. Its
lode is of banded iron and laminated quartz, and
is said to be over 100 ft. wide. A number of shafts
are down, and in one crosscut at 50ft., there is
107 ft. of ore showing without a wall, and in another
90ft. of ore. From 1056 tons crushed prior to 1908,
mill recoveries were 2200 z. fine gold, or 4-4dwt. per
ton from the plates. The present owners have got
out 2000 tons, which gave 355. a ton. Oxidised ore
will probably persist to 300 ft.; one shaft that is
down 265 ft. is in ore from top to bottom. The
Great Victoria has a 10-head mill to go on with.
A Water supply for a much bigger tonnage can be
got without great difficulty, and, if it wants any-
thing, the Great Victoria wants stamps by the score.
Present rate of working looks like two ants trying to
tunnel a cannon-ball.
Over in the West the big mining cos. are
reciting how they beat the favorite —in this case the
State Taxation Department—for £40,000 dividend
duty. The dispute arose in this way: In declaring
dividends the cos. distributed the full amount of the
dividend, and paid the 5 p.c. tax to the State out of
other funds. The Taxation Department allowed this
to go on for years, then suddenly billed the cos. for
the sum mentioned. Its contention was that if a
dividend amounted to £20,000, the co. was entitled
to only pay £19,000 to the shareholders, the State
getting the other £lOOO, whereas the co. has habit-
ually paid the £19,000, and handed the State only
5 p.c. of that = £ 950. The claim was for the other
£5O, multiplied, of course, many times. The Golden
Horseshoe was put forward to fight a test case, and
the action was heard before Justice Burnside, who
gave judgment for the State. The Horseshoe then
appealed to the Full Court, which backed up the de-
cision of the Court below. The case was then taken
to the Privy Council, which turned the lower Courts
down, and found for the Horseshoe.
From Randolph Bedford: Recent Bul-
letin remarks, apropos some self-defence of Mr. G.
Williams, that I was “defending my attitude in the
Bullfinch-Chaffinch business.” I was not defending
myself; I have never been put on my defence; in
the Bullfinch matter I was attacking Williams. The
Bullfinch will prove itself the mine I said it was.
As for the Chaffinch, well, the W.A. Government initi-
ated a prosecution which established its own justifica-
tion by proving that the Chaffinch find was genuine;
and all the whitewashing, and all the bluff of asking
the W.A. Government for compensation, won’t alter
the fact that the prosecution established these things
—-the concealment for three weeks of 52 bags of
ore worth 6oz. to the ton, and five bags worth 20oz.
to the ton, all of which came from the trench al-
leged to have been salted ; the opening of the tele-
graph office by certain of the defendants at three in
the morning, and their forward cancellation of a
telegram which could not be handed in till the office
opened four hours later ; the sending of the wire in-
structing the “bear” sale of 10,000 “before lunch,”
with the statement that the official report would not
be despatched till late in the afternoon; and the
finding of the manager bloodless and collapsed after
he had been “lost” in the bush for 24 hours. One
defendant, I am told, has left Australia; it would
be more decent if the others dropped the bluff of
asking for compensation. As to Mr. Williams, he
may be the kind of altruist who is so busy protect-
ing the public that he usually misses lunch and has
only time for a cheese sandwich and a small candy ;
but he can make mistakes like other people. He put
a lot of Melbourne people into a N.S.W. tin show in
200 shares of £25. The stock went to £6O, and
then it was discovered that the “gem stones could
not be separated from the tin,” and now the co. is
down and out. and the first and final dividend is
about 30 bob a share.
* * * *
William Orr, one-time a business man at
Shepparton, then a big promoter at Broken Hill,
later still a leader in the Mt. Lyell float, later still
a copper pioneer in N. Queensland, starts a new
lead. He wants the Chamber of Mines of Victoria
to call on the Murray-Watt Ministry to father steel
and iron works. Orr’s argument is that, as the
Government has established a profitable coal mine
at Wonthaggi, thus saving the State from the private
coal barons, it should now free Victoria from the
exactions of Steel Trust kings, who dump their goods
here. Orr is right, of course. The States build
railways, locomotives, carriages and trucks, which
eat up vast quantities of iron and steel yearly. So
if it is proving good business for the State to mine
its own coal for its own railways, it is likewise good
biz to use the coal for iron and steel making for
public works and railway purposes. Orr, however,
had no hope of getting the council of the Chamber
of Mines to admit it.
Once Mariners Reef, near Maryborough
(Vic.), was a mine. Next it became an eater of
calls. Since the shoot of gold cut out, co. after co.
has played the game of hunt the slipper. Local
talent is now trying its trained hand. If big talk
means anything, divs. ought to be soon pouring out
like slag from a furnace. The stone was giving
only a sniff of gold, but just when that leaked out
the effect was balanced by the news that rich speci-
mens had been fired down. After that there Was
cross-firing between a Maryborough broker and some
of the directors. A charge was made that the direc-
tors trafficked in scrip. To that they owned up so
manfully that the Stinking Fish crowd had the
chagrin of seeing shareholders bestow a blessing on
those who had bought and sold, whether wisely or
foolishly. Still, to the outsider the disclosure points
a moral.
Eaglehawk Consolidated, Maldon (Vic.),
w r ent into the melting pot some 15 months back as
part of a scheme to get cash to sink to test the reefs
at depth. Before that it had paid an occasional 3d.,
but beyond that it never did more than flatter share-
holders with a pleasing tale. In 13 months it suc-
ceeded in turning out gold worth £4363, just about
enough to pay for grease. Now stone has been got
at 1550 ft. ; but the directors don’t go farther than
to hope that further prospecting will disclose good
values. Only British shareholders would be con-
tent with a vague remark like that. To go on with,
the co. is drawing 6dwt. dirt from above the 650 ft.,
and there is a nice looking shoot at 850 ft., which
assays 12dwt. —a value which should pay well at
Maldon.
The invulnerable Tassy O’Connor turns
up again, this time with a request to Melbourne to
put up a few modest hundreds to further prospect
some East Gippsland claims at the head of the Com-
bienbar River. A very favorable report on the new
district has been made by Mines Inspector Hawke,
who thinks “there is a good chance of establishing
a payable field here.” O’Connor says the reef or
ore channel is from 3ft. to 4ft. wide, and he hasn’t
been able to get a sample without gold in it. The
average, he reckons, at 7dwt. to lOdwt. The field is
remote, and the tracks wild; but tunnelling should
make mining costs low.
» * « »
Einasleigk (Q.) has its mill ready, and by
this time it should be at work on the big body of
stuff which is too low-grade to stand freight charges
to the Chillagoe furnaces. On appearances it is
going to call for a good deal of nursing, and the
man who expects least from it will probably be
least disappointed.
* * * *
South Glanmire and Monkland (Gympie)
slumped heavily in front of a 3d. dividend. The
monthly clean-up resulted in 531)0z. gold from 700
tons stone. This includes 740 z. gold from the plates.
The result of prospecting for the Power reef has been
disappointing. Some small leaders are being driven
on in favorable country, and the manager thinks
that these leaders coincide with the Power reef. For-
tunately, the co. also has the Inglewood reef, but it
is not large at present, and it is likely that weekly
amalgam returns will vary a good deal.
At Charters Towers Kelly’s Queen Blocks
have had a large turnover. The last crushing was only
40 tons, but it gave an average of £7 13s. sd. The
crosscut at the 657 ft. level, aiming at the St. Pat-
rick reef, is in the distance where a development
may be expected; further ahead is the St. George
reef, which is being prospected by tributors in the
claim next door (the Brilliant and St. George). The
co. has had a long run of calls, and the market got
so very sick that a large number of shares were for-
feited. But the Towers remembers now that this is
a show that in bygone times has repeatedly made a
quick recovery.
* * * *
The £5OOO deposit put up three years ago
by the Queensland Silver Lead Mines, Ltd., in con-
nection with the construction of the Burketown-
Lilydale (N.Q.) tramway, has been forfeited on ac-
count of the failure of the co. to go on with the
work. The mineral leases have also been forfeited.
Remarkable results are being got at the
Fitzroy (Q.) flotation plant by using eucalyptus oil
in place of the crude mineral oil hitherto applied to
the process.

This scribe hurries in to say he was
wrong in putting Kalumadau’s costs at 375. The
half-year’s revenue was £11,788 from 4509 tons,
which returned a profit of £4353. This shows—
average extraction, £2 12s. 3d.; profit, _ a shade
under £1; total expenses, 335. per ton. Since then,
however, output has increased and costs have been
brought down.
The wire sent south about the revised
Blair Athol (Q.) option didn’t state the position
correctly. The original option mentioned £ 165,000
as the purchase price, payable £65,000 in cash and
the balance in paid shares. The new resolution runs
this way: “To accept, in addition to £65,000 cash,
a further sum of £85,000 in lieu of paid shares.”
A forecast of the Briseis (Tas.) accounts
for 1910 is out in Melbourne, where a hungry crowd
deplores the heavy capitalisation and the wasted
condition of the old Briseis mine. It is said that
trading profits for the 12 months were £32,125,
and that £18,450 undivided profits go into this
year’s current account. Not at all bad. The
co.’s luck has been exceptional, for when yields be-
gan to dwindle, up soared the price of tin to almost
record heights. The London Board keeps the reserve
firmly at £50,000, working the divs. on the margin
allowed by the cash assets. These look fairly
well. In 1908 thev were £39,681, in 1909
£51,489, and now £25,135. Seeing that the co.
has paid up additional funds on the Wallace leases in
Victoria, and is also doing big dead work, the year
has jangled out better than was expected—save with
regard to the Wallace leases. Four months’ work
to the end of April last only brought in £l3OO
profits from the Wallace dredges, which cuts up
about |d. per share on the 600,000 shares with
which little Langford and the London Board endowed
the co. at its birth.
Langford and Northey’s defunct Tas-
manian dredging area, the Weldbroolc, is being re-
suscitated as the Rio Grande by a Launceston syn-
dicate. “Tiger” Wilson is in charge. The thing
may succeed, no doubt; but in view of previous
failure, the public should ask for some very good
reasons for thinking well of it, and for backing the
opinion with its money.
Tasman and Crown Lyell (Tas.) has
bought the old Colonel North milling plant to con-
centrate its silver-lead ore. The chairman talks op-
timistically about getting the plant in going order
by October. The shoot of ore has yet to be proved
as a concentrating proposition, though low costs
should be a great help.
* * * *
Seems that Metallurgist Weinberg pins
his faith to a process for extracting zinc from fur-
nace slugs. The tentative proposal is to amalgamate
the Hercules and Primrose with the Tasmanian
Smelting Co. Ore has been treated at these works,
but the zinc has been slagged off, the object being to
make good recoveries of lead, gold and silver. In
Germany zinc oxide has long been produced from
slags that run fairly high in zinc; but it has been
supposed that the process was applicable only where
fuel is cheap.
* * * *
Tongkah has produced 378 tons 12cwt. of
tin concentrates in five months. The 121) tons for
May is the best this year. Five dredges worked
2414 hours, so the yield is 112.741 b. per hour.
Taking the average selling price at £lOO-—which is
a bit risky in the absence of assays—the total yield
was worth £12,150. Practically, the five dredges
recover £1 per dredge per hour. It is obvious that
under those conditions, good as they appear, money
can’t be wasted. There are 150,000 shares, so each
of the five dredges has to provide a profit for 30,000.
That is why Tongkah requires a fleet of dredges to
pay fair dividends, and a fleet eats up a deal of
money. An optimist may reckon that £12,150 from
tin oxide is as good as 3000 oz. of gold, and that each
dredge, in earning £2430 per month, obtained the
monthly equivalent of 600 oz. of gold. But having
done that much, let the optimist consider the specu-
lative nature of the tin market.

tity is very miaute.
“Skeeter”: After a long and chequered
career, extending over 40 years, the Lyndhurst Rail-
way Gold Mining Co., Junction Reefs (N.S.W.),
owned by J. Bull, has apparently thrown up the
sponge. ” The co. recently applied for six months’
suspension, but the warden made rude remarks, and
wound up by allowing three. Suspension expires on
June 8, but the co. has evidently arrived at the con-
clusion that it is played out, for it has handed the
show over to a locai tributing party. During the
mine’s life it has had about 20 managers, who re-
ported 20 methods of treatment. In addition to the
managers, it has been visited and overhauled by
innumerable experts. W. Burns, now at Reefton
(M.L.), broke the record with a reign of seven years.
Said here the other day that the Y-Water
Tin Co. (N.S.W.) had a capital of £90,000 in
60,000 shares of 30s. Nominally the capital is
£90,000 in 60,000 shares; but only 52,000 were
issued, and 12,000 of them were forfeited in the
days when people didn’t know what was coming.
The paid capital is thus at present £60,000 in
40,000 shares.
* * *
An attempt is to be made to turn the
swarms of game on the coast of the Northern Ter-
ritory to profitable account by preserving and canning
them; and turtle, fish, eggs, etc., are to be included
in the bill of fare. A representative of the scheme
has sailed for the sophisticated south, where they
possess the necessary cash without the necessary
game; and if he can get some of the cash so much
the better. The more industries at the back door the
better for the front one.
* * * *
A manager who was in charge of a big
mine about 100 miles from Melbourne was sum-
moned to appear before the directors to explain how
he contrived to amass so much property on the salary
he was getting. The interview ran this way: Chair-
man : “Well, Mr. Blank, we have asked you to attend
here and enlighten us on certain matters. We hear
that you have been buying some hotel properties, and
that you have built a terrace of houses, that you also
have built a grand place near the mine, and bought
a piano, a carriage and horses and other things, and
we think you cannot do all these things out of your
salary.” Mr. Blank: “Now, Mr. Chairman, you do
know that a man can save a deal out of eight pounds
a week if he is careful.” Chairman: “Yes, we know;
still this appears a little over the odds. Anyhow”^—
after a pause—-“we have decided to dispense with your
services.” Mr. Blank: “Well, I think, Mr. Chairman,
thee’re very foolish. I ’spect thee’ll be puttin’ an-
other man in my place.” Chairman: “Certainly, we
intend to appoint another man.” Mr. Blank: “Well,
doan’t ’ee see, gentlemen, if thee appoint another
man in my place, he’ll have all they things to get.
I’ve got ’em!”
* * *
N. S. Wales will soon rub off its slate the
registration of the Moorlort Goldfields (1906), and
the Victorian Deep Leads. (1906). Though domi-
ciled in Sydney, the home of these busted shows
was near Carisbrook (Vic.). They blossomed first
under Kozminslcy and “Billy” Robinson, Hercules
Robinson’s Governor-brother. Then they fell into
the liquidator’s net, to be resuscitated by 8., M. and
Co. These got the water out of the Berry lead,
but failed at the gold. Then Algernon Moreing
popped a few pounds of the co.’s cash, saved from
the sluice boxes, into the Maikop oil region. The
oil boom duly appeared, and cheap shares got a
market. Now the two one-time half-dead leadites
have each £30,000. This is to be lumped, and a
modest little thing in 2,500,000 shares, called the
Maikop Orient Oil Co., is to swallow them up, and
use the cash and anything the shareholders may put
up to bore for oil-claims got from one of the Lon-
don promoting cos. There are the usual big ads. in
the London papers, and the directors avow the new
co. will turn out a gusher. If it does, N.S.W. won’t
have the pleasure of collecting income tax, as the
domicile is to be in 8., M. and Co.’s London palace.

At home most people only take
tea with their meals, but in a tea-
room they come just especially
for the tea and a gossip—that’s
why, if 1 were running tea-rooms
of my own, I’d give them tea
they’d talk about.
I think the best advertisement
is to get a name for good tea.
At one place 1 used to be in
they gave the people “ Special ”
Grade Robur, and we had to
make a fresh pot for each
customer, and l do assure you
without exaggeration men whose
business places were whole
blocks away used to come to
our room for their afternoon cup,
and women wou!d crowd in
until there wouldn’t be a seat
vacant. Still, it’s my opinion the
No. 1 Grade, made fresh every
time, is quite good enough for
anybody, and people who would
grumble at it would grumble at
anything. 1 should say for family
use it would be just the thing
—it’s awfully saying, you know.
The Waitress.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, June 5, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
George V. made more fuss about his 46th
birthday than most people who see half a
century within coo-ee. Sydney took a holi-
day, and spread itself across a Rand wick
race meeting, a Centennial Dark display of
war wardrobes, and other birthday crackers.
The Review has always been embedded in
the public mind as a gilt-edged affair that
goes off with a bang at noon. The mere
woman has been wont to spruce up for the
big dress rehearsal at 12 o’clock, snatch her
habitual snack at 1 p.m., and turn up
smiling at afternoon sports. That’s her
idea of a holiday. This year the military
show in the Park, at which Lieut.-Gov-
ernor Cullen made a first appearance as
chief War-lord, fell socially as flat as a half-
cooked pancake.
The kilt is _ not extinct. Large plaid
skirts, with boiled lobster jackets, mounted
—rather uneasily—on horseback, jolted
through suburban streets after the march
past Lieut.-Gov. Cullen’s cocked hat.
A few weeks ago an unreliable rumor hit
my letter in its latter end. It swore
that Lady Dudley was booked to leave
immediately for Maoriland by the Ma-
heno. I tracked the foster-parent of
that rumor to his roll-top desk. He
was penitent but firm. He vows he ac-
tually saw Pier Ex.’s name on the passenger
list, though it was afterwards cancelled.
Anyway, Lady Dudley stayed at home, to
come within ten seconds of being killed in a
motor smash.
The Alliance Francaise produced its
best Parisian accent at St. James’s Plall one
chilly night last week. The Admirable
King-Plalls, who are going to tweak the ear
of Culture in this city till it answers back in
several languages, were present. Dr. Thi-
bault, an accomplished Frenchman, served
up Racine’s “Britannicus” (published, by
the way, in 1669) to an audience of chiffons
which was working hard to have its mind
improved. Nero and Agrippina, revolving
round the Racine manner, were adminis-
tered in homoeopathic doses by the learned
lecturer. “Britannicus” is rather a tricky
play to ease off on unsuspecting Australian
Society. “It is,” said one solid-weight
critic, “the piece in which persons of a dilet-
tante turn are seduced by the beauties
which do exist, to discover those that do
not.” Darling Point is now suffering from a
Racine recovery.
Governor Islington, of Maoriland, is hav-
ing an uneasy time in the matter of his gen-
tleman-helps. “Late A.D.C.” is getting in-
to print fairly often. Captain Crichton
Maitland, now in England, wears a “late
A.D.C.” tail. Mr. Kerr-Clark gave notice
before he had time to know much about the
Islington situation. And there are others.
Is it “the Missus,” or the uncertain geyser
service (Waimangu being on strike), or the
shortage of domestic help, that is so hard to
get along with?
The passing of the Macquarie-street Gov-
ernment House into a public museum and
storehouse for books is in the air. A Gov-
ernor-General with a roving commission
may be fishing off a northern reef, opening
a bazaar in Adelaide, signing his name in
Melbourne, or mopping his perspiring brow
in Brisbane. So the handsome old house in
Macquarie-street is generally tenanted by
housemaids’ brooms.
In the picturesque days when tne travel-
ling carriage, the footpad, and the lash
evolved a triangle of mixed results, Governor
Gipps made the first big splash in the Farm
Cove Camp. He gave a ball on May 24,
1843, in torrents of ram, in the then'half-
completed Governor’s nest, and Svuney So-
ciety had some cause to remember it. The
porch and archway wasn’t then even pigeon-
holed on paper, and the occasional hired
vehicle, with the carriages of the Moneyoc-
racy, floundered into red clay. Next morn-
ing dozens of boots and shoes were dug out
of the mud in front of Gov. House, and left
“to be called for.” The agitated owners
may have remembered a couplet that was
current in those days :
If you give him an orange, he’ll hand you the pips;
They’ll grow if you plant ’em,” says Governor
Gipps.
The R.A.A. has just cut another tooth.
In time of peace it is going to spread a
peacock’s tail as the Royal Australian Gar-
rison Artillery. When war’s alarms shake
up this pastoral patch, the R.A.G.A. is likely
to be melted down into plain Gunner with a
tough job.
,A military ball in aid of the Norland In-
stitute is now preparing its blare of trum-
pets for June 28. The Town Hall will be
the place. The R.A.G.A. will shed a mild
radiance over the scene, while militia and
volunteer corps are going to walk out with
ladies in white satin frocks, powdered hair
and patches. No others need apply. The
Gov.-General will lend his presence. Mrs.
Wood-Davies has arranged a Court set;
Mrs. Dabbs is engineering a presentation set
(no chiffon details to hand) ; Mrs. Carver
and Miss Hart are linking up a dazzling ar-
ray of white satin shimmer, with pearl or-
naments and floral top-dressing; and Mrs.
Norman is rounding up a Coronation set.
Major Christian, R.A.G.A., has been im-
pounded as the responsible head of a Field
Battery set. Will he send his battery at a
gallop through the City Fathers’ water-fil-
ter, which advertises its presence in a pas-
sage adjacent to the ballroom, or meekly
hang his horses’ halters on the Town Hall
organ pipes? And will Mr. Waters, who
is adjutant of an Automobile Corps, toot the
horn of command at the rate of 50 miles an
hour, or motor so gently over the early
Victorian set (Mrs. Palmer’s device) that it
won’t know whether rubber is up or down?
Dolores is scampering across the blue
Pacific. She will take a few bars’ rest be-
fore the Hospice concert on June 10, when
the Town Hall is likely to need repairing
with elastic sides. The booking is climbing
up to star-performance limits. Walter
Kirby is coming from Melbourne to lend his
tuneful tenor to the charity. Lemmone on
the flute, Bryce Carter on the fiddle, and
Laurence Godfrey Smith on a grand piano,
are entrees of interest in a bill of fare which
adds to its strong meat the limpid sweetness
of the artistic Margaret Thomas (Mrs. Alex.
Gordon! chirrup.
The Coronation crackers in England will
go off to the accompaniment of free rail-
way tickets to the “colonial” guests. Thir-
teen is "oing to reform its reputation as an
Ishmael in numbers. Thirteen commercial
princes of 13 railway companies will
refuse to take a brass farthing from Austra-
lasian big-wigs.
Mrs. H. B. Irving, as Dorothea Baird,
was picked by Du Maurier partly for the
promise she bespoke when playing with Ox-
ford amateurs, and partly for two shapely
feet that fitted Trilby’s light-hearted romp
through Latin Quarter studios. It has
been mentioned more than twice that Doro-
thea is a sister of Mrs. B. R. Wise. The
Wise return to Sydney recalls a graceful line
I once saw in the autograph confession book
of an old friend, “To know all, is to forgive
all.” Over it were the initials “8.R.W.”
Olive Schreiner’s “Woman and Labor”
(Dymock’s) is txe best-discussed book the
Australian woman fs digging into at the pre-
sent moment. Notably she gets some fine
thunder out of a passage on the “New Wo-
man” :
The truth is, we are not new. We, who lead in
this movement to-day, are of that old, old Teutonic
womanhood which, 20 centuries ago, ploughed its
march through European forests and morasses beside
its male companion; which marched with the
Cimbri to Italy, and with the Franks across the
Rhine. ... We are women of a breed whose racial
ideal was no Helen of Troy, passed passively from
male hand to male hand, as men pass gold or lead ;
but that Brynhild, whom Segurd found, the warrior
maid who “wrought on him to the performing of
great deeds.”
Another gas-light wedding is on the wing.
Mr. J. P. Maiden and Miss Joyce Walford
are to be married this week some time after
sunset. The evening wedding is gradually
putting the day-light pageant into the cold
shades of opposition. There seems to be a
boom in beauty. The brilliant background
the Nice. Girl now feels it necessary to
hang behind her bridal veil is a decorative
luxury that riots in expensive trimmings.
Nigel Barker, who was a few years ago
the darling of the Sydney Uni. sports
grounds, is to be married on Saturday to
Miss Tilley, whose father plays with bulls
and bears and other wild animals in the
Sydney Stock Exchange.
Beerbohm Tree’s Wolsey and Cookson’s
Cardinal Campeius appear in the trial scene
of “Henry VIII.,” wearing their red hats.
As a fact, no cardinal ever wears his hat of
office. One reason is that it is as big as the
average dinner table. A Cardinal couldn’t
even take his hat with him to such a func-
tion as a London cause celebre. The
head covering is permanently in Rome.
Each Cardinal has a church there —for ex-
ample, Cardinal Moran’s is Saint Susannah
—and there his massive millinery is hung
till the day of his death.
Lieut. Harold Nutting, of 17th Lancers,
one of Lord Denman’s gentlemen-in-waiting,
has a cousin already located on this desir-
able earth-heap. Mummer Kirby’s the man,
and he’s in the “Balkan Princess” Co.
Some years ago Mr. Henry Clay Ide, then
Chief Justice of Samoa, used to bring his
two handsome young daughters here for a
holiday trip. According to the Maoriland
Observer, Marjorie lde, who was only let-
ting down the hem of her frock during the
Australian holiday scampers, has been turn-
ing heads in Madrid. Henry Clay Ide has
been located in the city of Dons and bombs,
in some billet connected with U.S.A. Lega-
tion. Marjorie’s good looks went to the
Spanish head, notably to that of one Don
Alfonso de Vallos, who comes from a family
which has been cultivating a crop of mar-
quises since the first land-selection in Spain.
Don Alfonso has persistently played the
guitar under the walls of the U.S.A. Lega-
tion. Henry Clay has played the indig-
nant father. Marjorie, after smasning up
public opinion, by riding through Madrid
with the infatuated Don (no duenna being
tied on to the tail of her horse), is to go to
N’York city or thereabouts for a change.
Altogether, it seems, Henry Clay Ide has
got as much excitement as a hen with one
chick out of his Madrid diplomatic roost.
A Froebel birthday party last week was
arranged by the Sydney Kindergarten
Union. Froebel, as everyone knows, found-
ed the Cult of the Unsmacked Child in Ger-
man. As a slight token of esteem, his
memory was served up in the Manchester
Unity Hall, with sprigs of Teutonic folk-
lore. Forty kindergarten students, in Ger-
man garb, formed the connecting link be-
tween Kaiserland dances and Australian
beeswaxed boards. Miss Elizabeth Jenkins,
who keeps an all-seeing eye on Girl and
Child-gardens in this city; Miss Margaret
Windeyer, who ploughs up the infant mind
with selected literature in her spare mo-
ments ; General Sec. Ellen Desailly; Mr.
Secretary Milner Stephen; and Chaplain
Brook Robinson, who teaches the Powerful
crew to trust in the Lord —a wise precau-
tion if this antiquated cruiser ever gets into
argument with a modern battleship—shewed
the cud of cake-and-coffee at the resurrec-
tion.
Prof. Anderson Stuart presided at the
first annual gathering of the King Edward
Home for Dogs the other day. The Rothery
family has a hard-worked reputation for
speaking severely to those who hurry horses
with crowbars, or wipe the eye of dejected
dogs with a brick. Miss Olga, of this
family, is lending an hon. nib to the new
dogs’ hostel as sec. Mrs. Brown-eyed Mac-
Kinnon, an elegant pillar of the Victoria
Club, is president, and Dr. A. J. Brady
and Mr. Leonard Dodds are vice-presidents.
Mrs. E. A. Irvine, hon. see. to the Waver-
ley stallholders at the recent All Nations’
Pair, had to grapple with one form of the
Lie Malicious that dogs the heels of suc-
cess. “They say” alleged that the stall
showed a deficit of £3O. Was Waverley down-
hearted? No! It gathered itself together, and
got a sworn statement from the treasurer.
Now it wears a smile of bashful apprecia-
tion of its own efforts. The sworn state-
ment affirms and kisses the book to the ef-
fect that £2OO 6s. 2d. was the net profit.
Ethel Turner’s cheerful chatter about the
burnt-umber beggars of Colombo recalls some
of Mrs. Ellis Rowan’s Cuban tales. The
Australian flower-painter went from U.S.A.
to the West Indies a few years ago, and
chased exotic blooms through Dominique and
Cuba. Here she was beset by myriads of
small, gravy-colored beggars, clad chiefly in
fresh air, who followed her easel, or aired
their bare legs on the box-seat of the tumble-
down carriage that bumped her over local
scenery. “One of these,” she told me, “a
bright-eyed urchin with fewer rags and more
audacity than the others, became my de-
voted follower. He asked me if I had come
from America. I told him I had. This was
his opportunity. ‘You see George Washing-
ton? I poor boy—l George Washington’s
own little boy: he my papa—you my
mamma!’” .But George Washington junior
was not in line with the cherry-tree morals
of his adopted parent. “One afternoon,”
said Mrs. Rowan, “I noticed an exquisite
rose growing over the high wall of a private
garden. George Washington, jun., on the
box-seat of my carriage, must have heard me
say I longed to paint it. Next morning it
was on my breakfast table. I thought it
wiser to ask no questions.”
It has taken about 25 years to discover 15
new stars in the velvet-shod vault of night.
Nine have been tracked by Mrs. Wilhelmina
P. Fleming, of the Harvard Observatory.
The American Suefragette drops this
news item with a loud clang on the feet of
any male person who says Woman can’t
find time to record her vote. She can find
heavenly systems, and lost dogs, and water
in the morning milk, and short-weight in
mutton chops, so it’s quite likely she is able
to locate the right square in which to pencil
a remark on a mere ballot-paper.
One who knows, or thinks she does, says
the recent Arthur Allen flower-trimmed
ball cost £lOOO. Allowing several grains of
salt for this quotation—which is given for
what it is worth—£6oo is not an outrageous
estimate for a glittering soiree that plunged
into a specially-built ball-room floor, before
it poured out one glass of champagne, or cut
one slice of turkey.
Captain Verney’s sister, who spent a sea-
son here, found Australia so pleasant a place
that she is likely to make her home-nest in
Queensland. Miss Verney is going to marry
Captain Newton, who hails from Brisbane,
and was formerly one of Lord Chelmsford’s
young gentleman. His present address is
a wool or cattle station out in Queensland’s
great spaces.
“Passionate socks,” a burst of dazzling
color on male ankles, first flurried feminine
emotions about two years ago. The epi-
demic has now spread to female hose. The
short skirt, is the obvious excuse for the
present shriek. Where, I ask you, is whis-
kerless youth to look—under or over the edge
of his evening paper—if he sits in a crowded
tram, bound for fashionable dress-centres,
opposite a pair of trim ankles, boldlv ad-
vertised in saxe-blue hose, with red and
white roses climbing round the insteps?
Twenty years ago, even a piano had its
legs draped. Ten years ago we clothed a
mantelpiece. Tan open-work hose, scaling
the heights of a city tram by daylight, were
once considered mild dissipations in dress.
Somebody has said that morals are merely
a matter of geography. The stocking, se-
verely snubbed (excepting in the ballet),
seemed to have no latitude to call its own.
To-day it swaggers in the public eye, and
expects to be mentioned in the social news.
Miss Edith Currie, whose family tree is
(or was; tied up in wool bales down River-
ma way, was, when last heard of, meditat-
ing a descent on the ancient town of Bruges.
Dora Meeson Coates, of this side, has been
getting herself talked about in the London
standard for her fine poster work. Lord
Roberts has approved of her last Rule Brit-
annia effort, designed for the National Ser-
vice League. The Standard, which isn’t
P r °he to slobber over inferior work, labels
the Meeson Coates poster “noble and im-
pressive.”

tive. Harold Rendall, eldest surviving son
of Walter C. Watson, M.D., of Manly, and
grandson of the late William Bourne Wat-
son, ex-member of N.S.W. Legislative As-
sembly.” It suggests an inquiry into the
pedigree of ex. Is the late respected Wat-
son “ex” because he has crossed the Styx, or
was he “ex” prior to that melancholy free-
ferry service? This reminds me (chiefly
because it is entirely unconnected) of the
query wedged in between sponge cake and
international politics at a Rose Scott tea-
P art y the other day. “Can you tell me,”
aska d Miss Blank, a minx with a persistent
note ot interrogation in her grey matter,
“the present address of Tolstoi?”
