MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, June 5, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Birthday Meeting, at Flemington on
Saturday; drew more women than usual. Or
it may have been the new Governor and his
party that were the magnets. Excellency
Fuller wore spats, astrachan collar, and an
intensely shiny top-hat. The Lady wore
electric-blue feathers .in her spreading hat
and a soft chiffon velvet coat. Her sister,
Miss Phipps, looked the true- sport in a
short skirt, with a sealskin coat well clear
of her ankles and smart shoes. Over her
shoulder were slung a business-like pair of
glasses, and she explored the paddock, at-
tended by a devoted pair of aides. She is
a tall, handsome brunette, with an easy
carriage, and looked smart in a broad black
hat, wreathed about with little Eve’s apples.
In the viceregal box, Mrs. and Miss
Clarke—Apple isle relatives of Rupert—
took an exalted view of things. Mrs.
Clarke was in a magnificent sealskin coat
with a sable collar; Miss Clarke distanced
all the good frockers with her short, navy-
blue tailor-made, a pot-hat in bright blue
satin, and black fur. Beatrice Madden, in
blue and a beatilic smile, was with fiance
Jack Baillieu and his sister. Miss Bail-
lieu wore the cutest small hat, with squirrel
tails cocked up at the back of it. Colonel
Appleton’s girl Tettie sported a paddy-green
sash to her turned-up hat. Mrs. Geddes
chaperoned three or four Toorakian maidens
in a snug party, which kept to one corner
of the grandstand, while the attendant men-
folk “did” the paddock.
Mrs. Howard was followed by positive
cries of .envy. Her sable cape was a criss-
cross affair of perfectly-matched skins, and
shone even among a winter.’s-day show.
Several recent brides paraded. Mrs. Judge
Casey, with a cockade in Her hat, hostessed
a motor party with a specially-served ham-
per tea. Mrs. Jim Purves wore sable in her
flat hat, and white cloth tailorings. The
returned Fenner girl has cultivated a dash-
ing style of frocking and hatting since her
wanderings with Gwladys Chirnside; she
waved some impressive plumes on an acre or
so of hat. Eugenie Duggan Anderson was
in black velvet, with a whole ostrich on her
head. Mrs. Grantham, buried in furs,
had her daughter along, for the last time be-
fore the marrying.
Came to the conclusion after a stroll
round that furs are the salvation of woman;
they make a fresh face glow like-a rose, and
old faces lose, wrinkles and crow’s feet.
Governor Fuller, surrounded by a large as-
trachan collar, kilted Boy Scouts and a
crowd attired for wet weather, proclaimed
the vice-regal suburb of Malvern a city
one moist afternoon lately. In return he
was given -a certain amount of refreshments
and an ornamental key which, so far as 1
am aware, will open nothing. Lady Fuller
sat alongside and. smiled under a Gainsbbr-
ough hat, made of cherry juice, decked
with pheasants’ quills. She gowned her
slender form in black, and muffled in cosy
furs.
Malvern’s Mayoress McKinley waved
Coronation-blue feathers from her hat, and
Miss McKinley was attired in sapphire blue
and squirrel furs. The Best lady wore
mouse-color. Mrs. Knox and Mrs. Carty
Salmon were amongst those present on the
dais, set like a bandstand on the lawn. A
marquee was snugly arranged as a reception
room, and through it the guests strolled
in single file over the nice, damp,
sawdusted grass. It was a day for
thick Soles and the awful things known
as “serviceable shoes.” Subsequently the
Governor and his party inspected the gar-
dens, and the guests followed like sheep.
Turnstiles blocked the uninvited majority
from joining in and disorganising the stately
function. They looked over and through
the fence for someone to present them with
the freedom of the place—with or without
an ornamental key. But of course nobody
attended to them.
Malvern did not intend to stop at the
proclamation and reception; but two dy-
namos stopped somewhere and the electrics
refused to light. A torchlight procession
was thereupon hurriedly cancelled and post-
poned to some evening in the near future.
Torchlight processions along dark streets
would have looked too much like the search
for the missing will.
The day following was Children’s Day,
when the Corporation presented each
imp with a bag containing one bun,
one piece of a heavy plum-cake, and
one slab of iced sweetness. The ravening
infants absorbed the gifts of food, and then
played about in the damp. The weather
had been weeping for days, and was not
yet quite over its hysterics, so an inspired
daily paper reporter wrote that the young
Malvernites _ “swished the raindrops from
the grass with their swinging feet.” It no
doubt looked poetical; but when Jimmie
and Dulcie rush round in wet grass for
the best part of a day, it frequently means
that mother has to put on the kettle and
fight croup most of the night.
The most brilliant dance of the early win-
ter, so far as the younger set is concerned,
was the Bachelors’ Ball on Friday night.
Twenty bachelors with good recommenda-
tions to mammas took the St. Kilda Town
Hall. Each supplied a solid chaperone and
wore the blue badge of a flameless life. To
an.excellent orchestra, and a floor that was
a dream, the Twenty added decorations in
amber-tinted leaves, and the prettiest girls
of their acquaintance. The committee-men,
led by Secretary Leo Crane, gave themselves
no rest and few dances, and came out of the
ordeal of stage management with flying-
colors.
The dressing recipe mostly started : “Take
one and a-lialf yards of duchesse satin,
cover it with three-quarters of a yard of
mnon, and weight the total with several
pounds of expensively veiled beads and cry-
stals.’’ The rest was girl and stockings.
Stockings and shoes are having an innings ;
none of the little-mouse-peeping-in-and-out
business of earlier days. Nearly every girl
wore silken stockings and silken shoon to
match her brief frock, and girded up her
pinned-on curls with a snood to match the
beaded draperies.
There were only about three long frocks
among the dancers. Stately Molly Linden
wore her powder-blue draperies long and
sweeping, and her shoes were powder-grey.
Mrs. Thomas held up several yards of silk
and showed pink silk extremities. Mrs.
Thiele wore a lovely - black envelope, and
brought her big, handsome husband —also in
black. Mrs. Willie Felstead wore gold
tissue and gold shoes. Mrs. Gunn wore
black and silver. Miss Ellis, a small, fair
belle of St. Kilda, put some snap into a black
and gold net cap. a-top of a black and gold
dress. One girl made a castanet accom-
paniment to the two-step by wearing
“bugles” that clashed around her.
The Fuller chickens run so: Bridget,
Patience and Maude, Gerard and Christo-
pher, and baby Victoria.
The willowy Fuller lady has become
President of the Victoria League. So the
household is not going to monopolise her
time.
Henry Bracy has been busy trying voices
for the Melba Opera at' Her Majesty’s.
There has been an overpowering rush of
excellent sopranos. He could easily fill up
the opera-houses of London, Paris and New
York with this line of goods—gems (more
or less) from the field which gave the dia-
mond of Melba and the pearl of Castles.
All the church choirs would willingly tum-
ble in for the opera, but Bracy has to cry,
“Hold, enougu!” to the sopranos. So
nightingales, larks, thrushes, and black-
birds, not to speak of lyrebirds, kooka-
burras, stormbirds, mopokes and magpies,
return to perch in church. The next big-
gest rush, has been of mediocre baritones;
but there are very few srood basses. Con-
traltos are not so many, either; most of
them exult in a bullock-hell tang—the clank
of a frog in a pond. “Oh!” exclaims
Bracy, “please don't!” The grievance is
with the tenors; as to these Bracy is almost
desperate. “You know, my man, you’re not
a tepor at all.” Positively Melbourne has
not, it seems, 13 all told, even though other
parts have been sending ambitious q'uotas.
There is a curious deficiency in this quality
of voice, and Bracy is asking why. There
must be razors in our air, for there are
heaps of chorus tenors in Germany, Italy,
France, England and America.
Three-minute impression of Fuller ( Victoria's
new State Governor) passing along Collins-
street on his arrival.
The St. Kilda house that sheltered the
now-honeymooning Clarke lady has been
done up and re-let, and the chronically
curious confess they are “dying to see what
it looks like inside.” The gossips regard it
as a hallowed spot.
Staunch friends of Mrs. Doubleday and
daughter Leila whipped up a fair-sized mat-
inee benefit for the Doubleday Fund'at'St.
Kilda Theatre last Thursday. There was
only the usual picture programme plus three
or four musical items to set against the
rival attraction of a Martyn Hagan mat-
inee at the Royal; still the smart new hall
in George-street was quite half filled.
Manager Willie Lincoln did the amiable in
front of the house, and ex-Senator Best
made a speech from the stage. He
talked in Parliamentary terms of Leila
Doubieday and her talent, and the lot of
money that had been needed to keep the
gifted maiden at her studies in Vienna for
five years or so, and he delicately suggested
that a little more of the needful remained
to be raised ere she was a self-supporting
artist. The making of a violinist s reputa-
tion is an expensive process. Gentle Leila,
whose portrait in the vestibule showed her
as a charming bud, is to get her first show
m London next week, when she will play
at the Queen’s Hall with a big orchestra be-
hind her. Afterwards she will nave to
complete ner conquest of the critics in Ger-
many, no doubt. These remarks, or some
of them, were delivered by lawyer Best in
an elderly spirit. He spoke paternally of
Leila,” without adding her surname. But
he looked —from a little way off—more
youthful than ever. He might have been a
hard, spry citizen of 30. Somehow he has
contrived to support the indignity of knight-
hood without putting on rotundity, or other-
wise looking conscious of his painful posi-
tion.
Alma Barber, who has been appearing as
a kittenish Miss in “Our Miss Gibbs,” has
left for London for a few sips of fog and a
little recreation. Connie Milne is taking her
place in the Williamson show.
The co. which passed from Hugh Ward to
Reg. Wykeham has a weakness for matri-
. In the journeying round, Celia
Ghilom and Rosie Musgrove annexed part-
ners ; also the advance agent succumbed to
an attack of orange _ blossom. Now Miss
Redfern, a youngster, in her early twenties,
blushingly admits that she has been mar-
ried since January, and that her Sydney
husband is dragging her off the stage. Miss
1 reston, a small, dimpled, fair girl, is re-
hearsing for the bride’s stage shoes. Manager
Vvykeham, who is a nice bachelor in the
thirties, smilingly congratulates his happy
people—and remains single!
The Dog and Poultry Show, which is just
now barking and crowing at the Exhibition
Building, has a compensating circumstance
this year. The concert hall that used to be
deserted is now a People’s Picture Palace,
and the wearied dog-fanciers and hen-experts
can drop in there at concession prices and re-
vive themselves with melodrama on the screen.
After an hour of walking amidst the din of
a dog show’, half an hour’s contemplation of
a silent cinematograph should be refreshing.
Miss Elma Highett, of Armadale, who is
something of an heiress, has whispered the
usual “les” to the youngest son of Admiral
and Mrs. W ake, of Helens, Sidmouth, Devon
(Eng.). The young man w r as terribly dealt
with in his christening. In a moment of
rapture his parents called him Arthur Leofric
St. Aubyn.
The latest engagement links the name of
Dorothy Hinde—a Toorakian maiden with
a dazzling complexion—with that of Lieuten-
ant George Meredith, of Launceston.
Miss Charlotte Hemming gave a sober
little party at the Alexandra Club last week.
Since her mother’s recent death she has
been living very quietly. Now, to add to her
loneliness, sister Addie has departed to
Fogland, and her married sister has gone
with her husband to South America. The
cheer-up party included Mrs. Robert Walker,
who is wanning fame locally for the work
she is doing with the South Melbourne
crhches for children of busy work-a-day
mothers.
The Alexandra Club spreads in ever-in-
creasing content over the Gas Co.’s fine
premises. It has two dozen large bedrooms,
with an extra-special brand of Marianne to
look after the comfort of members. The
amber drawing-room curtains are comfort-
ing to the eyes these bitter grey winter days.
In the whole two flats occupied by the Club
only two footholds are open to male crea-
tures. Members may conduct a man into
the dining-room and afterwards plant him
in a cosy little brown smoking-room. But
from all other parts of the Club’s domain he
is rigidly excluded. At the door of the read-
ing-room a large notice demands Silence in
foot-high letters. I am sure a lot of the
members avert their eyes when they pass it.
Gaud Mayor Davey and his Mayoress will
give their Coronation hop on June 23. Gov-
ernor-General Dudley and the little Coun-
tess, and State Ex. Fuller and his lady,
w’ill be present. The list of guests also glit-
ters with the names of the new Marquis and
Marchioness of Linlithgow.
Melbourne’s polite Bohemian circle was
sorry to hear last week that Mrs. Abrahams,
of South Yarra, had been interviewed by a
surgeon, and w’as lying a-bed with a nurse in
attendance. She is now on the mend after a
somewhat bad turn, but for which she would
have taken the trip to Fiji with her husband,
whose health is also off color of late. Mr.
Abrahams started from Melbourne last Sat-
urday, with his sister-in-law and niece Julie,
and Dr. and Mrs. Joske.
Andy Brennan, the youngest son of Coun-
cillor Brennan, has made a small stir in his
corner by taking his M.D. degree at the
early age of 20 or so. He is now resident at
St. Vincent’s Hospital.
Bachelor girls hereabouts are getting into
the habit of drifting off in pairs for foreign
touring. Leila Armytage, of the woolly
clan, and Cissie O’Loghlen, daughter of the
late baronet Bryan, are the latest travel-
duettists. They left on the China, and will
tour and flat together for a year or more.
They will dive into Ireland during the wan-
der, and pull up at the castle of Cissie’s
brother, who is Lord-Lieutenant of County
Clare.
The most expensive people are beating up
political meetings this winter. Mrs. Les-
lie Stuart invited the surrounding motors
to Woodside,, Toorak, on Tuesday afternoon,
and spoke seriously to their furred owners.
Mrs. Herbert Brookes, who revealed an ex-
quisitely-fitting frock to an admiring circle
when she flung off her furs, wrestled grace-
fully with the eight planks of the Liberal
party’s shaky platform. Allegedly to “edu-
cate” Australian women, monthly meetings
are held in snug Toorakian drawing-rooms.
Mrs. Barrett is president and Mrs. Knox
secretary. A male person was induced to
help this time by speaking severely of Labor.
The befurred darlings rewarded him with
glove-muffled applause. Tea followed this
exertion.
Any woman who wears artificial hair
should keep a gun to shoot the limelight man
if he attempts to direct his beam in her direc-
tion. The concentrated glare of the spot-
light shows up her dead hair as it really is—-
dry, lustreless stuff, which suggests Egyp-
tian tombs, mummies and cere-cloths. I was
at the theatre the other night when the de-
mon at the back of the circle started his fizz-
lantern. A few seats in front was a per-
fect haystack of fair hair which looked the
genuine goods while all the lights were up.
In the_ intense gloom, the white merciless
beam lit up that fraudulent coiffure as it had
never been lit before, and made most of it
look just like chair-stuffing. The unfortu-
nate female beneath it went on chatting to
her escort unconscious of the awful revela-
tion.
Montague Brown has been exhibiting his
works at the Melbourne Athenaeum. Most
of his efforts picture the gum-tree, made
pink or pallid by setting sun or morning
fog; but he has also copied a lot of the cli-
mate in Queensland, Tasmania, and other-
where. His biggest productions are “Even-
ing’s Golden Glow” and “Summer Past-
ures,” both of which show groups of Austra-
lian trees with their customary companions
of shadows and sheep. Some of his minor
bits, picturing the country around Cairns,
are flat, and his frightfully rainy subjects
supply a fit of the miserables. For the rest,
there are Scottish pieces, with some very
fair painting in them.
, A historic cottage in Little Bourke-street
has just been pulled down to make room for
a new wool store. There, in the ’4o's, the
late Justice Redmond Barry practicallv
founded the Public Library. ' In 1 the build-
ing, which was at the rear of his little cottage
home, he had a library, and early Melbourne
residents were free to come in and read of
an evening. An old mulberry tree which
was in the Judge's garden has been spared
the axe.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
Tuesday night’s Empire Ball was for the benefit
of the popular society which exists to missjonise
visiting seamen. It was crowded; also it was one
of the most gorgeous functions that ever struck this
city’s eye. Actually one managed to forget the
gauntness of the Exhibition Building wherein the
parade occurred. Most parts of the Empire (not
even forgetting the Solomons) were represented in
the “sets,” and the dresses did the originators proud.
Hugo Leschen drilled the parades in an effective
march. The promoters even arranged a final comic
item. The way back to privacy at the conclusion of
the march led up a steepish ramp, and as the par-
ticipants’ shoes had collected no small quantity of
French chalk during the evolutions, the effect—
from a spectator’s point of view—was most exhil-
arating. Have you ever tried to negotiate a steep
ascent in slippery shoes? If not—don’t! Some of
Adelaide’s ieunesse doree is busy framing quite un-
necessary excuses for its amazing gymnastic feats
and its poor balancing powers.
Queer what assets the seeker for work lavs claim
to. One jackeroo advertised the other day for a
billet as farm or station hand. Amongst other gifts
he mentioned his “refinement.” No doubt it would
come in useful in clearing mallee scrub and reason-
ing with dilatory oxen.
The S.P.C.A. meeting on Monday was responsible
for a hurried hour for milliners. Many feminine en-
thusiasts, much beplumed as to headgear, were en
route for the meeting, when the news got about that
an eminent K.C. was to make birds the text of his
discourse. The result was a wild rush for the hat
shops to buy harmless velvet toques and smug gear
with only artificial vegetables for their decoration
This society, by the way, has put up a sort of record
Among the audience last week could be counted
quite a number of young male bloods, whereas the
average S.P.C.A. meeting in other parts is attended
by old ladies and thin lath-like gentlemen.
Government House promises a ball in honor of the
Coronation, as well as the lesser joys of a reception
and there are rumors of some private frivols. Also,
Acting-Mayor Bonython promises to entertain. Thus
those of us who are left won’t do so badlv, and aided
by the moving picture-shows we’ll possibly see the
affair far better (for a modest shilling and with no
discomfort) than those who strain sadly on the outer-
most edge at “ ’Ome.”

North Terrace, east of King William-street, long
sacred to local medicos, has received a shock. China
has invaded it in the person of a “specialist,” who
paid £4OOO for a residence formerly occupied by the
late Dr. Ellison. As the “specialist’s” new abode
almost faces the Adelaide Hospital gates, and as he
hangs out a glaring electric sign about 12ft. long
the wrath of the profession is bubbling over at the
spout. Worse still, the learned man of China charges
03. Od. for consultation, and his consulting-room is
already working overtime.

Government Guaranteed Brandy.
THERE are Pure Food Laws and Pure Beverage Laws for the protection of the
people of Australia to-day.
Chateau Tanunda Brandy is distilled under the direct control of the
Federal Government, and from the time the grape juice leaves the presses until the
Brandy is thoroughly matured, the Commonwealth officers watch every process
IN YOUR INTEREST.
The presence of that G. R. label on the bottle means that your Government
guarantees the contents to be distilled WHOLLY FROM GRAPE WINE by a
pot still or similar process, and that the Brandy has been matured by storage in
wood for a period of not less than two years.
In no other part of the world is Brandy made under conditions that offer
such complete protection to the consumer.
Ask for Chateau Tanunda—the Pure Australian Standard Brandy.

HER OBJECTION TO IT.
“The only objection I have to Chamberlain’s
Cough Remedy is that the children are always asking
for it,” writes Mrs. J, S. Phillips, of Rockwell Street,
South Broken Hill, N.S.W. “My kiddies have both
been subject to colds and croup ever since they were
born. My eldest boy was so bad at one time that
he was in bed for a week. In fact we despaired of
his life, but thanks to Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy
he was soon well again.”*
The “King of All” Cigar brings the American
method of “Cigars direct from the maker to the
smokers,” to Australia. Messrs. Craig and Aitken
import the “King of All” direct from the maker,
and thereby assure a very high quality smoke at a
moderate price. The “King of All” is a pure young
leaf cigar of the finest quality, shaped by hand and
dried by natural means. At 3d. each, Messrs. Craig
and Aitken claim the “King of All” Cigar to be the
equal of many selling at 6d., selling in light or
heavy weights. It is stocked by all tobacconists and
good hotels, and is known by the registered brand,
without which none should be accepted as the genuine
“King of All.” If not obtainable in any district, the
“King of All” can be had four a shilling, post free,
or 50 in a box for 9s. 6d. Sample box of 10, 2s. 6d.,
post free, direct from the Australian agents, Messrs.
Craig and Aitken, 654 George Street, Sydney.*

HEART TROUBLE
Distressing Symptoms that
Cause a lot of Worry and
Anxiety,
♦
Symptoms of heart trouble are frequent
in Anaemia and Indigestion. “I can’t hurry,
my heart thumps so,” is the common expres-
sion of every anajmic person. In Indiges-
tion there are wind spasms that send dart-
ing pains about the heart, which make the
victims often think they have heart disease.
When the blood supply is built up or the
Indigestion cured, the heart symptoms natur-
ally disappear, as there is nothing to cause
them. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured
a great many cases of Anmmia and Indiges-
tion and have given sufferers relief from dis-
tressing heart symptoms that caused a lot of
worry and anxiety. They actually make
new blood. The following case shows how
valuable they are in heart trouble.
Mrs. Helene Warnock, 75 Mason Street,
S. Yarra, Melbourne, tells how Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills built up her health, and her
experience should prove of interest to other
sufferers. Mrs. Warnock’s own words are:
“Some time back my health began to fail.
My heart began to give me a great deal of
trouble. I had always had a good consti-
tution previously, but now I noticed my
heart would flutter nearly all the time, and
with any exertion it would begin to thump
furiously, especially if I walked up a hill,
and I’d get such a gone sort of feeling, as if
I were going to die. I dare not hurry for a
tram. My heart would palpitate, as if it
were going to jump out of its place. I’d
get out of breath at once, and have to stop
every few yards to rest. My breathing
would be so quick and hurried I’d have to
gasp. I used to feel sure my heart was ;
seriously affected. I always had a cough,
apparently from the stomach, and I was de-
void of my strength. My face was always
flushed, without cause. I’d have to take a
rest now and then in the day, I felt so
fagged and tired out. I slept and ate pretty
well, but really the food didn’t seem to do me
any good, or nourish me, and I fell away
considerably. Even a little bit of house-
work was a trouble, I got so easily knocked
up. My heart was rarely free from palpi-
tation. My nerves were so weak, and my
blood so thin. I Was very easily startled,
and at times would feel very worried and ir-
ritable. But I found Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills a perfect tonic. They began to tone
me up from the start. My nerves steadied
down, and my heart began to beat less ir-
regularly. I bought another box, and felt
better still, so I kept on with four boxes.
My health was quite restored, and I have
kept well ever since. My strength and en-
ergy gradually came back,' and I feel better
than I have done for years.”
You can judge the shopman’s opinion of
you if he tries to sell you anything else
when you ask for Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
Those “just as good” remedies are never of-
fered unless he thinks the customer easy to
gull. The price is 2s. 9d. per box; six
boxes 15s. 3d., of all dealers, or from the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., of Australasia
Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A cable message states that Ireland had
a surplus of £4,000,000 worth of eggs last
year for export. Yet some politicians won-
der why so many Irish electorates return
Home Rulers unopposed.
SAMPLING THEM.
BUYER: “ This dog, which you told me had a
fond and affectionate disposition, has already
bitten every member of my family."
SELLER: “ And which does he like best?”
Even Holman is beginning to see it now!
In the Assembly the. other night, speaking of
the Referenda, he said :
The people have decided that the task of dealing
with social abuses is not to be taken out of our
unworthy hands and given to an abler body.
Open confession for the good of the mole.
Judging by his public outburst when a
colleague dared to differ from him, the
N.S.W. Acting Premier doesn’t want Min-
isters. He wants automatolmans.
The hero of Mount Kembla is now, it
seems, the hero of Referendum Day. A post-
card has been issued, adorned with a per-
sonal photograph, and subscribed “Thanks
and Congratulations. C. G. Wade. Ap. 20,
1911.” Bill Adams has won the Battle of
Waterloo again.
Victoria is handling its new Elwood Mead
immigrants in a sprightly fashion. One
little lot arrived by the Runic on a recent
Friday* went up to Bamawm the same day,
inspected the land available, chose the blocks
they favored most, returned to town to lodge
their applications, and were back again and
at work on the land on the following Thurs-
day.
The census figures show that Sydney has
a total population of 637,794, and that the
female mnabitants exceed the male by
12,000. In 1901 Melbourne led Sydney by
3000, but the bloated Ma capital’s popula-
tion for 1911 has now got 45,964 ahead;
wherefore Sydney rejoices. At its present
rate of gorge Sydney will reach the million
mark about 1922.
Now it’s Scotland that is feeling the pinch
of the English, Yankee and Jewish land-
grabbing, land-wasting millionaires. The
census just taken shows an appalling de-
cline in rural population—-only 96 McTav-
ishes and Stewarts and the like, where but
10 years ago there were 100. Almost every
Scotch paper is filled with bitterness at the
discovery.
The dames of one Fat Woman’s Political
Association in Victoria undertake to devote
one hour a day to providing meals, knitting
socks, and making underwear for “the re-
spectable poor, ’ which in such cases means
the respectful poor. Tiie Bulletin hopes
the dames will not expect “the respectable
poor” to eat the hash they make of politics:
it won’t matter so much if they are content
to remain merely sisters of mercery.
The divided-control folly in Sydney civic
government has had its worst development
in the fact that 10 or 12 bodies can and do
tear up the streets at any old hour of the
day or night. If you meet a gang of work-
men with pickaxes and spades digging holes
in Sydney, you have to guess which it is—
City Council, Works Department, Water
and Sewerage Board, Harbor Trust, Gas Co.,
Hydraulic Power Co., Telephones, or Rail-
way and Tramway Commissioners. Still,
they might be burglars digging a tunnel into
a bank, as no one notices who they are, the
citizens and police being so used to strange
men coming along with tools and sinking
shafts in the main streets.

cents that it would

Speech ran into 3!)

on the doormat.
There are some Acts of Parliament
through which it is quite possible to drive
a coach and four. Yet any attempt at
carrying one’s swag through would result in
six months, without the option.
Weary’s Government is thinking of tack-
ling the main roads problem in a typically
weary way: it proposes to borrow a million
or thereabouts, and lend the landowners
money to repair some old roads and make a
few new ones. Of course there is to be a
sinking fund ; that is to say, the Govern-
ment which borrows the money to relieve the
landowners from taxing themselves, will
also provide the sinking fund. It is a
beautiful scheme, and if nobody had men-
tioned a general election one could be sure
that such a thing was within smelling dis-
tance.
Walter Bevan, Solicitor-General—ap-
pointed by the McGowen Cabinet—has been
told off to attend the Legislative Council
and assist the representatives of Fat in
wrestling with legal teclinicalil'es. The
reason given is that, owing to t’.' dearth of
legal talent in the Chamber* members are
unable to get expert advice when neecssarv.
Apart from a sprinkling of solicitors, three
Ks.C. are in the Upper House —Pilcher,
Garland, and Gannon—and there are a
couple of barristers who don’t wear silk.
But this array of legal talent isn’t appar-
ently going to take any professional interest
in the measures of a Labor Government.
Certain Right Thinking people at Ren-
mark (S.A.) have been threatening to “take
the law into their own hands” for the good
of their country. A new name offers for
their “national” emotion —Fatriotism.
NOT QUITE HIS WAV.
BILL: “ Say, ’Any, how would you like to go
in for flying?”
HARRY: “No good to me! I’d rather die a
natural death by drinking."
Apropos the proposed N.S.W. legislation
to deal more effectively with wife-deserting
husbands. One of the first laws promul-
gated by Phillip, Australia’s first Governor,
was that “none should be permited to quit
the colony who had wives or children in-
capable of supporting themselves, until they
had found security for the maintenance of
such wives or children.” Nowadays it is
only the merchant who can prevent his credi-
tor quitting: the wife can see the “old man”
packing up, and can’t do more than heave
the crockery at him.
The N.S.W. cow cocky voted No at the
top of his voice the other day, when he was
asked to strengthen the hands of the Com-
monwealth, so that it might deal with rings,
combines, trusts and monopolies. Now he
is getting his reward. Pig-raising is his
chief side-line; and a correspondent to the
S. M. Herald exposes the existence of a
ring of pig-buyers in Sydney, and shows that
the members are raking in about £1 a head
profit on the squealers.
The three ways of doing political party
business in N.S.W.: (1) The Laborites
hold a caucus; (2) the Liberals hold a con-
ference; (3) the members of David Storey’s
party “have an exchange of opinions.” They
all three amount to the same thing, and the
consequences are the same; but for party
purposes the Sydney daily press pretends that
the caucus is ruining the country, while the
conference and the “exchange of opinions”
are intended to save it.
Dear Bulletin,—May I draw Mr.
Wade’s attention to another conspiracy to
benefit Melbourne at the expense of Syd-
ney? In a recently-published table of mar-
ine-route distances which will be shortened
by the opening of the Panama Canal, the
distance of New York and London from the
chief ports of the world is given. In this
table Melbourne is mentioned, but not Syd-
ney. Now, considering that Sydney is the
greatest port in the Southern Hemisphere,
with the most beautiful harbor and the
greatest and noblest statesmen, this is an
insult. The canal will shorten the dis-
tance from N’York to Melbourne by nearly
3000 miles. Why cannot we have the dis-
tance to Sydney also shortened? Why are
we neglected? Where are Wade
kin and Percy Hunter?—Yours, Locust.

The speakers were prac-

couldn’t supply the ar-

deal as though the Cham-
ber had congratulated it-
self upon having knocked
a hole through the bot-
tom of the lifeboat, and
then discovered that it
was impossible to get
ashore on the hencoops.

Oh, why do you leap in the air, Papa?
Why do you leap in the air?
Why don’t you sit down on your chair, Papa?
Why don’t you sit down on your chair?
I want you to tell me a tale, Papa —
Oh, Papa, it’s wicked to swear —
You said that you would without fail, Papa !
Please, why do you leap in the air ?
Oh, why are you grinding your teeth, Papa?
Oh, why are you grinding your teeth?
You know they are false underneath, Papa !
You know they are false underneath 1
If the Labor Government died, Papa,
Would you send them a lovely white wreath?
' You seem to be shaking inside, Papa!
And why are you grinding your teeth?
Oh, why do you dance when you’re old, Papa?
Oh, why do you dance when you’re old?
Your feet are they dreadfully cold, Papa?
Are both feet so terribly cold?
Whenever you’re asked to a bail, Papa,
You always commence to scold.
But now you’ve waltzed twice up the hall, Papa.
Does that warm you up when you’re cold?
Please, why are you wringing my neck, Papa?
Oh, why are you ringing my neck?
Have you got to send someone a cheque, Papa?
Have you got to send someone a cheque?
Will they get a new hangman for you, Papa—
The old one is not now on deck?
Is my tongue all swelled-up and blue, Papa?
Pa! why are you wringing my neck?
N.S.W.
NATHANIEL LOBSTER.
BIG!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Oh, father, dear,” young Willie said (and it was
Empire Day),
“Please tell me why I wave the flag and why I shout
’Ooray !”
And father answered as he spat upon the fender’s
marge:
“You love the Empire, Willie dear, because it’s very
large.
“If you could bend your little back and carry bricks
all day,
And likewise carry bricks all night till you were
worn and grey,
And keep on humping bricks till you were old as
Pharaoh’s bones,
You couldn’t shift one-half the bricks the British
Empire owns.
“And also, if you took your spade and dabbled in the
mud,
And trained your children to the task, and all your
kindred blood,
And all their stout descendants with the shovel made
a spurt,
In forty centuries from now they’d still dig British
dirt.
“Within the British Empire there’s more warts upon
the face,
More wasps and wars and Wowsers than in any other
race;
In writs we hold the record; and our statisticians
claim
That the Empire’s snake-bite figures put our rivals
all to shame.
“The mighty British nation, even foemen have con-
fessed,
In blunders, bugs and Bibles is ahead of all the
rest.;
In navies and in niggers quite decisive is its lead ;
The British Empire, Willie dear, is very large in-
deed.”
So father helps the orators, who bid young Willie
note
How marvellous is Bigness, how magnificent is
Bloat;
And even in his slumbers little Willie won’t forget
The - gross Gargantuan Empire where The Sun Can
Never Set.
VICTORIA.

WILD AUSTRALIA.—SKUTHORP’S SHOW coming
to Brisbane at last. —The Greatest Buck jumping
Show in the World is that of SKLTHOIiP’S, where
some 60 head of Bucking Horses, Bullocks, Ponies
and Mules travel with the Show, and a number of
the World’s Greatest Horsemen, including that King
of the Saddle and Bridle, Lance Skuthorp. He who
repeated Adam Lindsay Gordon’s Leap, and rode
Martini’s BOBS to a standstill. The Proprietor of
the Show has rented the BRISBANE SHOWGROUND
for SATURDAY, JUNE 24. On that day the full
combination will perform. And there will be a £5O
prize for the Quickest Thrower and Tier of a Bul-
lock- —the American Cowboys against the Australian
Stockmen. Also, £lOO prize for the Best Buckjump
Rider in Australia (for men), and a £5O prize
for the Best Lady Buckjump Rider; and a £lO
prize for the Best Camp Rider; £5O for the
Quickest at Catching and Saddling and Riding an
unbroken Colt. The Proprietor intends to take his
Show all round the world. Horsemen and Horse-
women that can prove themselves good enough
to represent Australia on that day can be engaged at
a salary not less than £lO a week. All Letters,
G.P.0., Brisbane.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
First inter-State football match of the
season occurred on Saturday at Sydney Agricultural
Ground. It was a great day for the League, between
thirty-five and forty thousand enthusiastic followers
of the game being present, and fully one-third of
them belonged to the allegedly gentler sex. When the
teams faced, there was nothing to choose between
them in appearance, and an impartial spectator could
have bet his life on a very even game. But in foot-
ball apearanees go for nothing. The Queensland
League crowd were simply not in it. Yet, strangely
enough, the first score was registered by the visitors,
Brackenrigg booting a goal before the game was 10
minutes old. These two points seemed to rile the
’Walers, who immediately broke loose, gallop-
ing through and over the other mob, scored when
and how they liked. The contempt with which the
home team treated the visitors was ghastly; they
juggled the ball about apparently with the view of
making a holy show of the Queenslanders. And yet
it was an interesting game to follow. The passing
was easily the best ever shown on the ground, and
completely broke up the Queenslanders. The goal-
kicking of Messenger was great. Besides landing 10
goals off his own boot, he scored four tries, and, in
all was responsible for 32 points, or half of the total.
In an array of paladins he was the mightiest of them
all, his running, dodging, feinting, and tackling
being about the best he has ever shown. He was
evidently rubbing it in to the Sydney sporting
papers, which last week suggested that he be left
out. When the end came, N.S.W. had scored 65
points to 9.
* T- * *
In the Union Rugby competition Univer-
sity is at the top with 10 points, Newtown running
up with eight, followed by Western Suburbs and
Manly with 7 each. As all the Union teams are
colts," they started fairly even, and North Sydney is
the only club which has the distinction of losing all
five matches played. South Sydney met North Syd-
ney at Rushcutters Bay. The South team developed
a very decent pack ; for a time the Norths made an
excellent defence, but then crumpled up, and South
Sydney won by 8 to 3. At University Oval ’Varsity
found itself attacked by Western Suburbs. Per-
sistent line-kicking completely puzzled the students,
and resulted in long, dull spells. But when ’Varsity
did get hold of the ball, the men from the West
looked very sick, and the game ended in favor of
’Varsity by 24 to 3. West had failed to cross the
line, and its only points were from a penalty goal.
A feature of this match was that over 2000 specta-
tors looked on, and for a time the Union thought the
drought had broken. At Sydney Cricket Ground
Newtown met Eastern Suburbs, and the first half
saw them three all. In the second half Newtown’s
back machine was very much better than that of the
East, and Newtown won by 9 points to 3. The
Manly-Balmain upheaval was a very close thing. On
paper Manly was miles ahead of Balmain, but when
tlie numbers went up Manly had managed to squeeze
home by 8 to 6. Glebe and St. George played a
dull, uninteresting scrap, which Glebe won by 8 to 3.
The Australian Rulers put on two start-
ling games at their enclosed ground, Erskineville
Oval. The opening match was between East Sydney
and Newtown, both of which have the reputation of
being very fast. The attendance was good ; the play
was brilliant. East, attacking, for a time carried all
before it, and then suddenly froze. Newtown came
away in splendid style, and by quarter time was
leading by 27 points to 24. The second quarter saw
a regular turn-and-turn-about, but in each deal Easts
seemed to gain slightly. At half-time they were lead-
ing by 43 to Newtown’s 35. In the second quarter New-
town lost many points through over-eagerness. East de-
clined to get excited, but, nevertheless, Newtown
crept level, with 47 points each. Verjr little scoring
that quarter. When the final quarter commenced,
Fists came with a tremendous rush, and lost it at
the finish. Newtown then had a chance to score,
and failed miserably. With only a few minutes to
go, East was leading by one solitary point, and right
on the tick of time a Newtown forward, with one
last kick, registered a behind and made the score
equal—s 6 points all. The struggle between Y.M.C.A.
and Sydney on the same ground was terrific and in-
describable. At the end of the first quarter Y.M.C.A.
led by 3 goals 1 behind to Sydney’s 1 goal. Second
quarter saw the game grow faster still. But with all
the speed, Sydney was playing a distinct plan of
campaign, and, verily,, it had its reward. In the end
Sydney ran out winners by 52 points to 48, which,
in this game, is close indeed.
Saturday’s most cheerful League game
was that between South Melbourne and Richmond.
Skinner’s hirelings having not yet grasped the fact
that brute force is of little avail when there is a
strict referee around, tossed the game away after
having foreclosed upon it, and Richmond won by 62
to 54. Richmond, playing with much individual
brilliance, was a goal in arrears at half-time, but
had forced a 13-point lead when the lemons came
along. Then the hirelings, by rushes, got two goals,
and looked, as though they were in full blast for
their own. But disregard of the rules lost them
their advantage, and the yellow-and-black made a
last dash to save the game, and did it. Skinner is
understood to have once more spoken firmly to his
darlings. At Victoria Park, ’Wood, acting its old
foolish caper of taking things easily at first, found
Melbourne a tougher proposition than expected, and
was nine points behind at lemon-time. A “Colling-
wood finish,” however, added 15 points, while the
Red Legs added nil; so ’Wood won—49 points to 43.
The Saints, on their own ground, kept Carlton mov-
ing all the time, and lost a strenuous scrap by onjv
10 points—42 to Carlton’s 52. The Students were
at home to Fitzroy, and with the strongest available
team held their own till half-time, after which they
just faded out, and ’Roy won by 76 points to ’Var-
sity’s 42. Essendon went down to Geelong, and ad-
ministered a mild castigation—’Don 59, Sleepy Hol-
low 42.
i,i * * *
Only one Association game - promised excitement,
that between Brunswick and ’Scray, and it was
willing enough to satisfy the most bloodthirsty. The
umpire twice called a halt, and reminded the teams
they were not playing under the rules of the
old Marquis. The riot ended in favor of Brunswick,
with 61 to Footscray’s 49. P’ran downed ’Town
by 53 to 43, enough to swear by, but nothing to
sing about. North gave Brighton cold shivers by
totting up 95 points to 40. Northcote and Pres-
ton accepted their customary defeats in a contrite
frame of mind, ’Cote 42 to Port’s 60, and Preston
27 to Essendon’s 128. Still, this wasn’t the worst
doing of the day; Footscray Juniors dog-walloped
Brunswick ditto by 84 points to 0. That was
awful.
“S.S.”: Minister M’Bride has appealed to
Chief-Commissioner O’Callaghan for a report contain-
ing the best advice of the police for the suppression
of ruffianly conduct at Melbourne football matches.
I am surprised that the authorities have not long
since put a few pertinent questions to the police to
satisfy general inquisitiveness re the astonishing
blindness of John Hop when a footballer starts out
to cripple the Law and his opponent. I’ve seen police-
men looking on at conduct on the football field that
would have resulted in the prompt lumbering of any
street youth who so disported himself for half a tick.
The idea that the ’oofballer in action is a privileged
blackguard is eagerly entertained by J. Hop, whose
sympathetic attitude is in strong contrast to his
readiness to butt .in in the interests of humanity
when a boxing bout takes on an angry flush.
* , * * *
On tlie Parramatta River on Saturday the
brief and inglorious career of Frank Hagnev as a
sculler came to a sudden stop. In a race with Alt'
Felton for £5O aside, the ex-cyclist was donkey-
licked. Felton went to the front with a bound, and
stayed there till the end. Hagney made a valiant
chase of it, but never had a chance. Dick Arnst.
who was present, said “It was a terribly good race,”
and that Felton rowed like a veteran. Harry Floyd,
Arnst’s trainer, said Felton’s display astonished him.
Altogether there seem to be more than mere possi-
bilities about Felton.
* * * *
Five weeks ago Dick Arnst was challen-
ged by Harry Pearce to defend his championship of
the world, and £lOO was passed along as evidence
that Pearce was a true and proper person to chal-
lenge the champion. But, up to the present, no
articles have been signed. First of all Arnst was
waiting for his stake, and after some delay it was
said that it had been safely landed by a Maoriland
steamer. Still the articles were not signed, and
Arnst was reported to have said that he did not
despair of retaining his title. On Wednesday week
an appointment was made at the Royal Hyde Park
Hotel for the purpose of signing up. Pearce arrived,
but there was no trace of Arnst; it was said that,
feeling a slight chill, he had hurried back to his
training camp at Mortlake. Then the Pearce fol-
lowing went mad: “Arnst could not raise his
stake,” “Arnst was trying to side-step Pearce,”
“Arnst had seen some of the marvellous spins put up
by Pearce, and knew that he was as good as licked,”
etc. The chances are that the astute Richard is
angling for long odds.
“X ” : Although Dick Arnst cannot spare
time to clinch Harry Pearce’s challenge for the
world’s sculling championship, the burly Maorilander
still pursues the pigeon-butchering business. At the
N.S.W. Gun Club’s abattoirs last week, he “divided”
three sweepstakes. •
Two Parisian aviators had a curious
smash last Saturday. Loping along about 150 ft.
above the earth, they dashed into a hollow pipe in
the air, down which the aeroplane dropped like a
stone. Fortunately the pipe was narrower at the
bottom than at the top, and they escaped with a
broken leg each. The small aeroplane will never be
safe. Holes in the air and tubes that run down to
within a few feet of the earth render it necessary
to have great wing space, so that the machines may
safely traverse these air spaces, on the same prin-
ciple that a motor-car can safely cross a hole which
would spill a bicycle.
N.S.W. Bowling Association is getting no
better very fast, and last week President Raffan
and his officers got right out. Another secretary and
president have been appointed, and it is averred
that the game will not suffer in the slightest. But
confidence has been shaken —badly shaken.
* * * *
Australian professional cycling recognises
that it is in a bad way, and unless something ex-
tra galvanic happens the next development will be
the funeral. So on June 27 an effort will be
made to resurrect the Federal Cycling Council
when delegates from all the State bodies will fore-
gather in Melbourne. It will be the first meeting
of the Council for many years, and its object will
be to devise ways and means to import bright over-
seas stars, who will shed a glory ovet the, sinking
cause, attract fresh dollars, and bring new life.
But it is a dead hope. The only chance of pro.
cycling is to lie fallow for a few years.
On the other hand, amateur cycling is booming,
especially in N.S.W. On Saturday the Picton to
Ashfield 50-Mile Road Race attracted over 200
wheelers. The long-markers had it all their own
way, and collared the swag. The winner was R.
W. Page (llmin. 40sec.), who led from A. V.
Anderson (15mjn.) and A. C. Gray (14min. 30sec.).
Scratch rider L. A. .Walcott took fastest-time prize,
getting over the distance in 2hr. 7min. 54sec. Last
year hq also scored, the fastest-time prize with the
record dash of 2hr. lmin. 23sec. Handicapping is
a difficult job, but it certainly looks as though the
long starters will have to be clipped a little.
A freak car, a two-cylinder Leon-Pougeot
light voiturette with a bore of 80 millimetres and
a stroke of 280 millimetres (over 11 inches), has
been doing things, and impish things at that. The
flying half-mile was cut out in 24min. 24sec. ; 50
miles in 39min. 47see. ; 100 miles, 89min. 28sec.;
and 78 miles in the hour. A performance which in
that type of car bangs Banagher!
* * * *
Some time ago the English cycling press
clawed at all Australasian records because the Christ-
church Cycling Club had discovered that one of
its road events was short-distanced. It was not
the club’s fault, but the mistake of a sur-
veyor ; and when the Christchurch Club found it
out, it at once made the matter public. A similar
error has now occurred in England, but the cream
of the joke is that the offending club admits that
it knew its hundred miles course was really only
99j miles. As, however, a surveyor had estimated
the hills and corners would be sufficient to make
up the difference, the club is quite satisfied to adver-
tise the course a hundred miles one. Now what
about some of these fast English times?
Melbourne is shortly to have the fastest
racing track in Australasia for motor-cycles. £BOO
is being spent on remodelling and asphalting at the
Friendly Societies’ Ground, and it is believed that
when completed the track will carry a speed of well
over 70 miles an hour. As soon as the warm wea-
ther comes around there will be some hair-raising
runs, as a number of crack motor-cyclists have an-
nounced that they are out after records. Jake de
Rosier’s American times are: Flying mile, 41
l-ssee.; five miles, 3min. 28 l-ssec.; ten miles,
7min. 1 4-ssec; 25 miles, 17min. 44 3-ssec. ; 50
miles, 35min. 41 4-ssee. ; and 83 miles 135 yards
in the hour. So the Australian cracks are up
against something pretty sudden. The great Jake
himself will return from America to England via
Australia, and will probably be seen on the Mel-
bourne track.
The effete Englishman still holds the
world’s record for the all-day motor drive, in spite
of a desperate attempt made in America recently to
secure the record. The winning car, a Fiat, covered
1491 miles, and the second, a Cadillac, 1448 miles.
Both of these, wonderful runs as they were, pale
alongside S. F. Edge’s bewildering 1581 miles 1310
yards, which the Anglo-Australian put up on the
Brooklands track when that wonderful road was first
opened. #
Agar Wynne, big dog of the A .R.C., and
one-time Victorian Attorney-General, has taken on
a big task. He proposes to standardise the rules of
racing in each State. This means that he has to
read, mark, learn, and inwardly digest all the racing
rules of all the States, and then bring the things
into line. The quickest way would be to draft a
new code, label it the Australian Racing Rules, and
have done with it.
* * * *
The marvellous accuracy of the informa-
tion received by Christian Noses is beginning to
worry people. As Sydney Referee puts it: “A horse
may not run for months, but if at its reappearance
it is supposed to have any chance, the books are
generally prepared for it, and it is the same in any-
thing that, after shaping bad on a few occasions, is
expected to show improved form. It may open at
10 to one a dozen times, but at the thirteenth it
will be at a comparatively short price throughout. . .
The wonder is how they succeed in doing so in such
effective fashion.” Of course the secret is the book-
makers have an intelligence department, based on
treachery and bribery, which finds it cheaper to
spend a few pounds among jockeys and stable-boys
than to let the public have an occasional win.
Hard to take seriously the Melbourne
match between bind rum and Reece on even terms.
The Englishman is a pretty player, and some of his
art is distinctly inter-
esting, but the best re-
cords of the two men
stamp it as an unequal
match. Reece is not
even the best of the
English p la y e r s,

figures, would be back-

compatriot, George
Gray. The “discovery”
of the deadly losing
hazard off the red has
revolutionised b i 1 -
liards, and Gray and
Lindrum thus-far are
the pre-eminent leaders
of the revolution.
Against Lindrum’s oc-
casional great breaks
Reece can show nothing
of compensating

The “bookies” are not quite defeated in
Maoriland yet, despite the recent stringent legisla-
tion. One well-known betting firm has issued its
business cards thus: “Skinnum and Fleesum, wool-
brokers,” and there is much indignation—apparently
on the ground that wool-brokers don’t shear the
lambs.
* » * *
Killed the other day, by a fall over a cliff,
the 25-year-old Liberator, about the best all-round
horse M.L. ever saw. He won the Grand National
Hurdles twice, Great Northern Steeple, Dunedin and
Wanganui Cups, and many other events on flat and
over sticks. Ilis most notable owner, Patsy Butler,
pre-deceased him by only a few weeks.
Queensland Turf Club’s Brisbane Cup
meeting, the Northern State’s principal horse carni-
val, was the most successful for some years, in
spite of wet weather on the opening day. Prize
money amounted to £5340, and 39 owners got a
share of it. W. R. Munro, whose Persian Jewel
won the Moreton Handicap and ran second in the
Brisbane ■ Cup and St. Leger, was top score with
£BO5 ; but John Finnie, “the father of the Queens-
land turf,” was close up with £BOO, the result of
Black Paint’s victor in the Cup. McCrimmon,
with four wins, was the most successful rider, and
N. Wood, a Sydney jockey, was first past the post
three times. #
In the V.A.A.A.’s fifteenth 5-mile Second
Teams Race, on Saturday, no less than 276 runners
competed. As a spectacle the episode was pleas-
ing, but as a race it lacked excitement, young
Russell H. Watson (Malvern Harriers), the Public
Schools mile and half-mile champion, winning easily
by 200 yards, in 30min. 24sec. Time is good, but
not up to the performances of G. B. Blake, in ’99,
or G. C. Stillwell, S. G. Hollow, and M. O. McEl-
hinney in ’O7, ’OB, and ’lO respectively.
The inter-University sports meeting,' on
the new ’Varsity Oval in Adelaide, was as easy a win
for Melbourne as it used to be in Nigel Barker’s
time for Sydney. J. L. Davis is the Melbourne
champion; he won the hundred, hurdles, and long
jump, leaving the quarter to his colleague, footballer
Ilartkopf. Sydney had the high jump (Rofe) and
the mile (Mac Neil and Arnold) pretty well to itself,
and Adelaide could only manage seconds and thirds.
Result: Melbourne, 14 points, Sydney 5, Adelaide, 2.
Maorilander W. F. Trembath justified bis
challenge by easily accounting for A. B. Postle in
the 440yds." world’s championship at Rockhampton
(Q.) on Saturday, winning by syds. in 50 l-ssec.
Postle led most of the way, but in the last 50yds.
the youthful Maorilander outpaced the veteran.
Trembath will now pack his. shoes and rush towards
England in search of gold and glory.
The Daily Mail Empire Cup was this
year captured by Wellington (M.L.) Suburbs with the
extraordinary tally of 824 points. This is the same
score with which East London (S’Africa) captured
the trophv in 1908. The runners-up this year
were the "Australian Rifles (Sydney) and the Ibis
Rifle Club of London, who both notched 816. About
700 teams and clubs shot for the coveted utensil,
so the Maorilanders achieve considerable fame by
their win.
Francis Birtles and his cinematographic
mate report progress from Cunnamulla, about 120
miles north of Bourke. The roads, it seems, are in
a horrible state owing to recent heavy rains. They
are likely to work cross-country to Brisbane before
starting for the Northern Territory.
Yankee automobilist Robert Burman has
been at it again. It is not so long since he estab-
lished new figures for the world’s flying mile record,
when he covered the 1760yds. in 26min. 12sec., equal
to over 138 miles per hour. His latest exploit was
on Florida’s wonderful speedway, the Ormond-Day-
tona beach, where his big Benz car whizzed over the
hard, wet sand in 25.45ec., equal to nearly 142 miles
per hour. At the same time he covered the flying
kilometre (1093yd5.) in 15.8 sec. This easily beats
aeroplaning.
liow are the mighty fallen! Jack John-
son is. actually considering the offer of a big guar-
antee from the Mclnstoush for a 20-round fight with
Bombardier Wells. How much Johnson is to receive for
licking his fellow-bruiser is not stated. When the
news hit Johnson he scratched his head and said:
“Waal, I guess there ought to be something doing.
I ain’t got no motor-cars, and I ain’t got no aero-
plane, but I’m not down to the last dollar yet. I
guess I’ll ponder over it, and let you know early.”
“Petramos” : This is in justice to Waddy,
who beat Tom Dunn at Adelaide. The ring at that
fight (of which, by the way, I was promoter) was
not slippery, as “Tykes” suggests (B. 18/5/Y1). It
sufficed Hughie Mehegan and Jim Armstrong a night
or two previously, and Waddy and the referee were
satisfied. Had Snowy Baker refereed the mill, Dunn
would assuredly have gone out on a foul very early
for going down without being hit. Dunn got back
some glory the other night by beating Bob Rollo,
who was helpless throughout, and showed none of
the clever footwork that made Turner remark to the
crowd on an occasion when the two met: “I can beat
this fellow if I can catch him. Somebody bring
me a bicycle.”
Broken Hill (N.S.W.) has come to a pro-
per apreciation of the importance of things,
and will, in the future, pay its football um-
pires £2 per match. That much the referee
is certain of; exactly how many hatpins,
curses, kicks and blows is problematical; and
even at £2 per afternoon it is doubtful if the
game is worth the risk. The Association might at
least have chucked in an armor suit and an acci-
dent insurance policy.

“ Snakebite ” : I’m sorry to have to do it,
but experience forces me to agree with a recent
writer in The Bulletin who 1 darned the working
man’s wife for not “ living white.” She is mainly
responsible for the success of the gimcrack, yellow-
agony furniture on the market, and in other lines
also she gives solid patronage to the black,
brindle or piebald trader. And you don’t need to
go to Queensland to find it out either. Hundreds
of wealthy Syrian, Hindu and other colored
hawkers all over Australia, if they could be coaxed
or flogged into a truthful statement, would prove
that practically the whole of their business is done
with the wife of the white worker. I had a good
example of it on a big construction job in north
Victoria that lasted for some years. Most of the
men had their wives in the camps, which were
scattered 20 miles through the ranges, and were a
happy hunting-ground for the Hindu and his kind.
There were two decent men who used to run
waggons through, loaded with all sorts of drapery
and other merchandise, but they couldn’t make
headway against the niggers. I don’t, know why,
because they sold their goods just as cheaply; but
the opinion I’ve formed is that the white woman
has not the natural aversion to the colored man
that is so strong in the white man.

BARRON GORGE.
MR. DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows: —
** I the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”
CAIRNS
is picturesquely situated, has a healthy
climate, and the temperature in winter
seldom falls below 50°. First-class
hotel accommodation at Cairns and
Kuranda (Barron Falls) can be
obtained at a reasonable tariff, and the
fishing at Cairns is excellent. A trip
to the Barron Falls and gorgeous
scenery about that part of North
Queensland should not be missed.

Miss Jessie Ackerman, who used fcolecfcme
on temperance, “White Slavery,” a :d “How
I Earned My Living in the East End,” is now a
political organiser in Westralia; and, by a turn
of the whirligig, at the last Fremantle election
she supported the “ Liberal ” nominee, who w s
also Bung’s favorite, in opposition to the Labor-
ite, who was an ardent teetotaler. The lady,
however, occasionally graces Methody meetings,
where she can still be eloquent on the evils of
grog and profligacy in general.
“S.T.S.”: Rose Boldrewood—daughter
of Rolf —has written a bush novel of sport and
love-making. The Brownes are all more or less
“sporting,” but that didn’t save her from work-
ing in a betting-list that has not been equalled
since Ouida was in her prime. It was a field of
six, four of them “not likely to have a show
unless accident interfered with the favorites.”
The favorite “ had the support of all the talent,
being backed down to 3 to 1”; and the other,
“ the local favorite, went out at 10 to 1.” Why
don’t some of these good things come our way?
I am not sure that it is not better than Ouida,
for she merely showed odds laid on several
starters, and over-confident backers might just
conceivably be mad enough for that; but the
Man with the Bag is never taken with that form
of insanity.

PAINFUL TROUBLE
WITH FINGER NAILS
Sometimes Three Fingers Without
Nails at One Time, Began 25
Years Ago, No Permanent Cure.
Began to Use Cuticura Soap and
Ointment. In a Short Time Nails
Were Well. No Further Trouble.
“I have suffered from the same trouble
[painful finger nails] at different periods of
my life. The first time of its occurrence,
perhaps twenty-five years ago, after trying
home remedies Without getting helped, I
asked my doctor to prescribe for me, but it
was not for n year or more that my nails
and fingers were well. The inflammation and
suppuration began at the base of the finger
nail. Sometimes it was so painful that I
had to use a poultice to induce suppuration.
After the pus was discharged the swelling
would go down until the next period of
inflammation, possibly not more than a week
or two afterwards. These frequent inflam-
mations resulted in the loss of the nail. I
had sometimes as many as three fingers in
this state at one time.
“Perhaps ten years later, T began again
to suffer from the same trouble. Again I
tried various remedies, among them a pre-
scription from a doctor of a friend of mine,
who had suffered from a like trouble. This
seemed to help somewhat for a time, but it
was not a permanent cure, next tried a pre-
scription from my own doctor, but this was
so irritating to the sensitive, diseased skin
that 1 could not use it. I began to use Cuti-
cura Soap and Ointment. I had used the
Cuticura Ointment previously on my chil-
dren’s scalps with good effect. I did not use
the Soap exclusively, but I rubbed the Cuti-
cura Ointment into the base of the nail every
night thoroughly, and as often beside as I
could. I had not used it but a few weeks
before my nails were better, and in a short
time tliey were apparently well. There was
no more suppuration, nor inflammation, the
nails grew out clean again. One box of Cuti-
cura Ointment was all that I used in effecting
a cure.” (Signed) Mrs. I. J. Horton, Kato-
nah, N. Y., U. S. A., Apr. 13, 1910. On Sept.
21, Mrs. Horton wrote: “1 have had no re-
turn of the trouble with my finger nails.”
Send to R. Towns & Co., Sydney, N. S. W.,
for free sample of Cuticura Ointment, with
32-page book.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“S.”: Smith and Jones were public servants in a
N.S.W. western town—Smith in the telegraph office,
Jones in the Lands. Regularly the cronies took
tickets in “Tatt.’s,” the postal official being respon-
sible for the transit arrangements. They never drew
more than a £ 5-note until a couple of months ago,
when a horse (which meant a cool £2OOO, less the
usual 10 per cent.) came their way. There was much
rejoicing, in the midst of which .Jones became ill
with appendicitis, and, after an operation, died.
Smith mourned the loss of his old chum just when
they had the chance of making things hum; but
when the prize came along, he suspended his mourn-
ing while he called around for Ins whack. A sur-
prised executor met him, and pointed out that his
name did not appear on the ticket. Smith applied
to his Department for special leave of absence to
transact urgent private business ; he hurried to Syd-
ney, and paid awav good money to be told that he
hadn’t a leg to stand on. Now it isn’t safe to ask
Smith to go in even a church sweep for a pair of
hand-worked slippers.
“Xelamon”: Isn’t it high time to put something
on the statute-book to meet such cases as the recent
pitching into the Pacific of 20,000 bunches of ban-
anas, to avoid bringing down the Sydney market
price? The owners of the fruit would probably say
that they could do what they liked with their own ;
but there ought to be a law to hit a man very hard
for wilful destruction of edibles, even when they are
commercially and technically his “own.” The same
game is worked with fish ; but we pretty well deserve
it all for having allowed the Dagoes to capture prac-
tically the whole retail trade in these two important
constituents of a sub-tropical dietary. The street-
hawker, if the police authorities would give him a
chance to live, can sell fruit and fish at just about
half the rate maintained by the Italians for the one
and by the Greeks for the other. The argument that
the Dago’s extortionate tariff must be protected be-
cause he rents a shop merely means that the public
must be victimised in the interests of the fat land-
lord.
“Quidam”: Anjmne who desires to know why Vic-
toria was christened the Cabbage Garden has only
to take a trip up the Bairnsdale line. For miles the
scenery consists chiefly of fields of cabbages, turnips,
pie-melons, and so on. Not content with growing
vegetables up to the railway fences, enthusiastic cab-
bage-producers lease the ground inside the fences
from the Railway Department, so that the succulent
vegetables extend almost up to the metals. Further
out the prevailing industry changes principally to
dairy-farming, and patches of maize, grown mainly
for cattle-fodder, make pleasant light-green patches
on the landscape, although, for that matter, no one
could at present complain about lack of greenness in
the Gippsland landscape anywhere. Judging by the
conversation of knowing residents, there are other
verdant products of the soil. As is the case almost
everywhere in Victoria, the opinion of shrewd people
is that it is practically impossible to make anything
out of land if it has to be purchased at the present
price. The prevailing confidence trick is to sell the
enthusiastic newcomer a block at an exorbitant
figure, but on attractive terms, the usual result being
that the unfortunate purchaser, if not very financial,
eventually forfeits what he has paid, and goes away
“skinned.” The valuable property is in time re-
sold. One particular block is pointed to with pride
as having been recently got rid of five times. This
sort of thing explains why so many Victorians are
leaving their own State in search of land.
* * * *
“Bromide”: Australia is beginning to have its re-
ward. Till recently there was competition in the
condensed milk business, and we got the potted juice
fairly cheap. But ~ now the competitors have com-
bined, and the first notification came when the trav-
ellers quoted an increase from 4s. 7id. to ss. per
dozen. When asked why, they coolly admitted that
a combine had been formed. It is pounds to pen-
nies that this is only the first of the jumps in
price, and when the public finds it has to_ pay more
and more for its milk it ought to go behind a door
and kick itself hard in its pantry.
■K * * *
“C.J.”: The colossal scheme for defrauding the
Customs in the matter of dry goods which Kingston
exposed, and the more recent revelation in the
matter of motor-car importing, point to a Govern-
ment Indent Department with an absolute monopoly.
The private enterpriser is evidently not to be trusted
in the matter of importing. He will swindle —it
seems that he can’t help it. And the cost of catch-
ing him is growing too heavy. The easiest and
cheapest thing to do is to wipe him out and replace
him by a Government Indent Department which will
import everything, and charge a percentage for doing
so. This would not only save a lot of revenue, but
also bring in a tidy penny in the way of commis-
sion ; and it would give the Government a vast
amount of information which would come in handy
when re-shaping its tariff, and be useful also for the
Court of Industrial Arbitration. In fact, there are
stacks of advantages in the idea, not the least being
that it would stop certain swindlers from posing ag
men of brains and standing in the community.
A BUSY AUSTRALIAN DAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Austral man awakes each morning fine,
And, rising from his lowly Sheffield bed,
He softly treads the floor of Swedish pine,
And scans the German ceiling overhead.
He pulls aloft the Yankee roller-blind,
And draws aside the curtain made in France,
When, through the English window-pane he’ll find
The Swiss asphalted pavement meets his glance.
His sleeping suit of Yorkshire flannelette
He doffs, as in his bath of foreign brand
The water bubbles through a water-jet
Imported from the dear old Motherland.
He washes with a soap in London bred.
Which lies upon a Dutch enamel shelf ;
A Turkish towel dries his dripping head,
The only thing Australian is himself.
He dresses in a suit of Scottish tweeds,
In patent boots from far-off New Orleans,
With cotton socks from Lancashire or Leeds —
Then breakfasts on a tin of French sardines.
His Hindoo coffee, from a German pot,
He samples in a vessel Japanese;
God bless the Yankee stove that kept it hot,
His true Australian appetite to please!
His breakfast over, with a morning rush
He grabs a hat with soft Italian crown,
And, on a Yankee tram-car joins the crush
Of fellow-patriots, en route for town.
The tram man smiles a faint Australian smile
As he with Foreign bell-punch checks the fares;
The very rails beneath him, mile on mile,
Are Trust-made steel from Pittsburg millionaires.
Our hero scans his paper carelessly,
And reads of workers waiting for a berth;
'Poor silly fools,” says he, “they’ll have to flee
To some remoter corner of the earth.”
Reflecting thus, and jumping off the car,
His office door, of sturdy British oak,
He opens, as his Mexican cigar
Just ends its brief Australian life in smoke.
A ring upon his Scandinavian ’phone
He answers; then he sits with busy air
Beside a desk from Baltic timber grown,
Upon his pet Vienna office chair.
Some business needs a good Australian think.
He works it out with steady brain, and then
He writes his thoughts in good imported ink,
On Scottish paper with an English pen.
His typiste, on a Birmingham machine,
Raps off his outward letters in a bunch ;
This gives him time to go, for change of scene,
Into a Chinese restaurant for lunch.
The afternoon moves on. With English mail
And German indent orders on his mind,
Alas I ’tis sad to know he’ll surely fail
To buy Australian goods of any kind.
At night again we find him, sleeping fast,
In West of England blankets snugly rolled;
Poetic justice thus comes home at last — .
Australian wool —disguised ! —keeps out the cold.
So, dreaming of imported kangaroos,
And eucalyptus from a Foreign shore,
The sleep of Grecian Morpheus he .woos —
The one thing pure Australian is the snore!
VICTORIA.
ALF LUMSDEN.

A CIGAR is the BEST
SMOKE OF ALL!
But why can’t you get a
Good Cigar in the Country?
a*vY\ t
ALL over Australasia the only places you can
buy a really high-class Cigar is in the big
cities. In the country districts a good Cigar
cannot be had; because it’s only at the pubs that
you can buy Cigars at all in the out-back districts.
The publican knows that selling liquor pays him
better. He reckons that his customers only call
for a Cigar when they -ve got a few drinks in and
want to dodge getting “fu’ the noo.” Any old
Cigar does for that.
So he buys a few hundred at a time from
some “back-yarder"—real “Flor de Cabbage"
with big gold pictures all over the box and
mighty pretty bands round them —made from the
waste leaf and the cheapest growths, but called
by some high-falutiu’ name. (The making of these
Cigars is another story—l’ll tell you about it
another time.)
The storekeeper or tobac-
conist in country towns does
not sell good Cigars either.
If he does, he has to add to
the selling price of the Cigar,
the maker’s profit, the profit
of the warehouseman he
buys from, the profit of the
traveller who calls and sells
them to him, and his own
profit.
So that a good Cigar
that sells in the city at 6d.
he’d have to charge 9d, for,
and not grow fat on it then,
because the number he would
sell would be small; and
good Cigars have to be kept
in a uniform temperature,
free from exposure to the
air, or else they'll go off.
Those are just some of
the reasons why you can’t
get a good Cigar in the
country parts of Australia,
and also why so very few
Cigars are at present smoked
in the country.
Buy a 100-box of my
'* DIADEMAS,” smoke 5 or
6 of them, and, if they’re
not as good as any 6d.
Cigar you’ve ever smoked,
you needn’t pay a penny.
That's the way I’m in-
troducing my Cigars into
Australia. I know Cigar-
making from A to Z. I know
these DIADEMAS Cigars of
mine are just the coolest,
smoothest and most luxu-
rious smoke any man could wish for. Cigar-
smokers of a lifetime say so; and the more you
know about a good Cigar the better you’ll like
them.
My DIADEMAS are made from the very
finest, mild, rich, mellow, Java leaf, the filling or
inside heart of the Cigar being all straight long
Brazilian. The Cigars are hand made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass’me before it
goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed to
burn free and draw easily. The Cigar is 4 inches
long, and the picture at the side is a copy of it,
exact in size and shape.
Now, this DIADEMAS Cigar is as good as
any 6d Cigar, sold in the cities by high-class
tobacconists, as I sell it direct to the customer—
saving the cost of wholesalers’, salesmen’s, and
retailers’ profit. I only charge you 22/6 the box
of 100 DIADEMAS, postage paid right to your
address.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade. Please add exchange to country and
interstate cheques.
My Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN
