IN BANANA LAND
The proposed match for the shearing
championship between Zimmerle and Living-
t- tone, which was to have taken place in Syd-
ney in January, has fallen through for the
time; but Livingstone and Seary are likely to
be matched.
After an effort spread over several weeks,
the. appeal to the Georges of Rockhampton
, to subscribe to a gift to George of England
has resulted in a collection of a small and
as yet unnamed total from six Georges.
a Georges in Rockhampton seem to be few—
• or sensible.
Brisbane Courier agrees cordially with a
local Customs official who says: “There is
no such thing as a slack month now in
Queensland.” What! With a Labor Gov-
vernment running Australia, with two La-
bor Premiers starring the Old Country, and
a grievous land-tax on the back of the Fat
Man ! Tut, tut!
T. M. King, who gave up being Railway
Commissioner the other day, has in his time
been Collector of Customs, Chief Inspector
of Distilleries and Excise, Under-Secretary
in the Treasury, Auditor-General and Dep-
uty Railway Commissioner; and, finally, for
two months, on Thallon’s death, he was
Railway Commissioner.
The “unwritten law” got a lift in Bris-
; bane the other day, when one Cochrane ap-
peared before Supreme Court Justice Chubb
;on a charge of stabbing. He pleaded not
guilty, but admitted the stabbing and
pleaded (lie “unwritten law.” The jury
took the matter out of the Judge’s hand by
bringing in a verdict of not guilty, after less
than a quarter of an hour’s consideration.
“The Prod”: Longreach Show, which is
Central Queensland’s great rally, is over,
and the squatters have departed for their
i dens. This show is, among other things, a
matrimonial fair, and every year a number of
t squatters meet their fates. The just-con-
eluded one is no exception to the rule ; visit-
ing belles have snapped up woolly eligibles,
and on short engagements too. Another de-
velopment was the subscribing of £50,900 to
procure motor wool-waggons if the. carriers
persist in refusing to haul wool at the old
rate after July. The reason the squatters
didn’t rush in without any “if” is that they
like the carriers, because the gentlemen of the
road buy horse-flesh from them and assist to
bulge the squatter purse, especially in
drought time.
“Flinderino” sends off a black brother: —
Died recently on the Yarrabah reserve, in the
North, an old" outlaw named Paddy. Before the
advent of the missionary, Patrick was chief of the
Binghis who speared their wallaby or bandicoot on
the country that is now the Yarrabah mission sta-
tion. In the bad old days he ran a sort of labor
agency: for payments of a kind, he supplied skip-
pers of beche-de-mer craft and pearling luggers with
black, crews. Cape York aboriginals made splendid
sailors. Paddy bitterly resented the coming of the
missionary folk, as it interfered with his dealings
with the white and yellow mariners. Also it knocked the
bottom out of his kingdom. Some of his tribe
joined the mission; the others, all except Pat, cleared
out to new country. The deserted chief spent the
remainder of his days wandering aimlessly over the
reserve, chyacking the black converts, and starting
bush fires where they were least wanted. He in-
variably had a gin with him, but she wasn’t always
the same gin. One time he got so troublesome
that the mission authorities took him over to Fitzroy
Island, where he could do but little damage. He
put up with it for a few weeks, and then escaped to
the mainland by paddling across the intervening
three or four miles of Pacific Ocean on a bit of a
log —a mere sapling.
THE TRUANT
CALLER: “Is your father at home, my little man?"
YOUNG BILL : “ Yes sir, ’e’s inside — ’e’s waggin' it from work ter-day.”
William Pagan, the new Deputy Railway
Commissioner, has moved around more than
most men. Trained in the Caledonian Co.’s
service, he afterwards engineered in Vene-
zuela and the U.S. and Jamaica, and again
in England; then moved out to N. S. Wales,
where his brief job was with the Works De-
partment ; then on to Bananaland, where,
after showing much push and initiative on
construction works, he followed H. C. Stan-
ley as chief engineer 10 years ago.
The man who wrote reporting the death
of the Wild River Times was guilty of a
gross exaggeration. The Times isn’t dead ; all
that has happened is that the paper is leased,
and that J. T. Algar has had to take over
his late partner’s interest. And Algar
tackles the job with heaps of confidence in
both the paper and the rich district in whose
interest it keeps a watchful eye alike on the
big potato and the little potentate/
Our [?]ory Hope.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The country is not clamoring for empirical legisla-
tion and revolutionary enactments during the coming
Parliamentary session. It desires a steady continu-
ance in the present paths of progress.
—Brisbane daily.
We hope you will not interfere
The while we raise the little loan,
Nor smite the Council’s hoary Peer
With Gideon’s sword or Samson’s bone ;
Just let us pour the dollars out
In one unending, shining flood,
And dream no more of strike or rout
Or wading deep in Patman’s blood.
For change the eouhtry does not pine,
She simply clamors “Let me be
At rest beneath my fig and vine,”
Although at large Iniquity
: May stalk, with iron-handed Greed,
A gruesome pair, hard-faced and dour,
Who sweat the helpless bands of Need
And grind the faces of the Poor.
Oh never, never, should we wish
To tread the paths of Anarchy ;
Sir Fat may bide beside his dish,
•And cuddle fair Monopoly;
It is' not ours to pull them down
Or rudely break their soft repose,
Or scowl with evil-boding frown
On Purse’s mid-day glutton doze.
Pour/out, pour out, the borrowed tin,
And bid the people shout “Hooray!”
Let callous Greed, with evil grin,
Crush down the young and grind the grey;
And see with sacrilegious hand
Ye smite no link from off the chain
That holds the poor in ev’ry land,
The while they plead—and plead in vain.
QUEENSLAND.
F.B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, June 3, 1911.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii, —
On Thursday the baby university was
proudly displayed in its swaddling clothes
to admiring uncles and aunts from other
universities. Sir William Macgregor, in
gorgeous robes, read the Chancellor s ad-
dress, which was an hour long. Its tail
was greeted with an outburst of cheering.
Wullie Kidston was handed an honorary
degree, and may now proudly label himself
“Doctor.” His own particular brand is
LL.D. One of the undergraduates whis-
pered to me that this means “Lor’-Love-a-
Duck,” but you never can trust these under-
grads. The' Medicals, in their flowing robes
and hoods edged or lined with scarlet,
looked more le beau sabreur than the staid
family doctor. The undergrads received
them with piercing demands to “say 99,”
and sadly whistled the “Dead March” as
they filed out. The Vision, overcome by
the, perhaps, too oft-rehearsed parental
speech, sank her picturesque head and plac-
idly slumbered, like the sleeping beauty in
the hall of the Ogre of Furthermore. Aud-
ible sighs of relief stirred the ooultry on
the women’s hats as the representatives of
other universities announced that their
speeches should be “taken as read.”
The Hospital Ball on Tuesday was a
huge monetary and social success, and
Mayoress Diddams wore the satisfied air of
one who. sees her efforts crowned with laurel
wreaths and trimmed with banknotes. The
officers from Kaiser Bill’s battle-cutter Cor-
moran rolled up in force, and their rapidly-
filled programmes was satisfactory evidence
that the Brisbane girls look upon the Ger-
man menace as a false alarm.
Last week, when the gas men were on
strike, and a heavy darkness brooded over
this fair city, Mrs. Egley White besought
the many guests expected in honor of Rica
Da.rvall to bring along their own illumin-
ants. As a result, kerosene lamps, motor
head-lights, lanterns and candles were spread
over the White house until it looked like a
chapter from the Arabian Nights.
Rica Darvall became liica Bell on Thurs-
day, and was formally presented to Camboon
as" its future mistress. She accepted the
responsibility in a becoming white satin dress,
set off by a gift of diamonds and pearls
from the bridegroom.
During a month spent among the islands
in the wonderful North, your Mario has
been discovering many things, amongst them
a mission station where the heathen in Ins
blindness is unable to understand the blessed-
ness of Christianity as he finds it. Even
to me it seemed a little strange that, while
some of the mission children are unable to
ou.tain the meat they crave for, the station’s
launch is sent to a neighboring port twice a
week, and generally brings back a supply of
bull-beef for the Saints’ tripe-hound. A she
missionary up that way told me that so eager
were the sinful to do wrong that one of the
little lads had actually dug up and eaten a
portion of meat the hound had hidden. She
said it with the air of one relating a scan-
dal. We were also told in shocked whispers
of a former saint, who bathed surrounded by
dusky Hebes, in the only water available for
the drinking of the multitude. As a monu-
ment of failure the place is a wonder to the
unprejudiced, and if it is the best thfit
the mission can do for its lost black brother,
the sooner it leaves him to his fate, or the
care of the Government, the better.
In our travels we saw into the ways of a
trading-cum-holiness co. which has leased
land for the growing of cocoanut and the
raising of shell. Profit above 5 per cent, upon
subscribed capital (when it arrives) is to go
to the enlightenment of the nigger. Mean-
while, the nigger explains fhat, having
bought his boat on terms from the co., he is
forced to trade with and procure stores
from his benefactors, and, as a consequence,
his pay works out at exactly two-thirds of
that ruling at Thursday Island. His arith-
metic may be wrong; but, anyhow, believing
it to be right, the heathen rages.
We also called upon a sturdy Samoan,
who kings it over the wonderful island of
Nigheer, about five hours’ sail or less from
Thursday Island. He was one of the men
who, during the pearl-shell row some years
ago, ran many tons of pink pyramids into
the continental market and sold them at
a fabulous price, until the trade settled its
little differences. An expert diver in his
time, he worked for Frank Jardine, who
still lives at Somerset, in the beautiful Al-
bany Pass, with his Samoan princess, and is
at present accompanying the royal lady on
a trip to her native island.
The Federal edict having gone forth that
the black diver must go, the Shellers’ Asso-
ciation of Thursday Island, bowing, its head,
is: arranging a school for white divers, on
the principle of a six months’ course. At
the end of that time it is reckoned that cer-
tificates of competency will be issued, and
the association will employ the holders on a
co-operative basis. The association is willing
to pay half the cost of establishing the ih-
structional school (£400), on condition the re-
mainder of the money is provided tom the
amount derived from the registration of
boats. Two pugnacious under-secretaries
are arguing the point, and Thursday Island
is waiting for some information.
Mrs. Ellis Rowan was in the
other day, on her way to the scrubs of North
Queensland, - where tehe’ will track, the. un-
painted flower to its lair, and paint it in
spite of its resistance. She expects to be
two months over the job, and is going alone.
Miss Hermione Frederica Ulrich, lately
lecturer in Modern Languages at Or-
mond College, Melbourne —a talented lady
who has won scholarships of enormous ton-
nage and is an M.A., of Melbourne Univer-
sity—has been appointed sub-lecturer in her
favorite tongues at Brisbane’s seat of learn-
ing. The lady is an Australian.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
A Brisbane man named King is claiming
the Australian long-distance motor record.
He started from the Northern capital, went
to Sydney, thence to Melbourne, Broken
Hill and Cunnamulla, and back to Brisbane.
That is nearly 6000 miles. The same tyree
were used all through the trip, and the only
mishap was a broken back axle near Cunna-
mulla.
Bananaland Government has some notion
of taking over the aboriginal station at Yar-
rabah from the Anglican authorities, who
seem to fail in the matter of making the
place self-supporting. At a recent meeting
of Yarrabah blacks, held to protest against
any break-up or deportation, one “Billy” re-
marked : “We do not want to leave Yarra-
bah, and go out into the world to learn to
drink and swear.” If the Church people
have not taught the aboriginal to work, they
have at least taught him to cant.
Among those present at the outing given
by Walkers Limited, of Maryborough, to
celebrate the delivery of the 150th locomotive
to the Bananaland Railway Department, was
Thomas Braddock, one of the three founders
of the firm of John Walker and Co., which
afterwards became Walkers Limited. The
other two were John Walker and J. F.
-Wood, both of whom are dead. The trio left
Ballarat on account of slackness of work,
pushed north with the object of establishing
a foundry for the manufacture of mining
machinery, and picked upon Maryborough as
the site for their smithy. Gympie was then
beginning to boom, and that gave them such
a lift that within a few years they started
building locomotives.

Life Assurance Society came rather as a surprise.

less than might have been expected. The Busi-
ness of. the Industrial Department has assumed
large proportions. The Report is remarkable for
the magnitude of the New Business in both the
Ordinary and Industrial Departments.”

branches of the business.”
The Argus, Melbourne, May 11, 1911 : —“The
Report of the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance
Society, Ltd., for 1910, shows that great energy
was infused into the management of the Institu-
tion during the year.”
The Register, of Adelaide, May 11, 1911:
“The Bonus of £1 ss. has been declared on the
most conservative principles. It will therefore be
quite understood the solid grounds for the expres-
sion of the Directors’ confidence that ‘the position
will result in the declaration of a still better Bonus
on future distributions.’ ”
The Brisbane Courier, May 11, 1911:—“The
total volume of New Business transacted was
85,231 Policies, assuring £5,554,136. These results
have been produced at a remarkably low cost.”
The Telegraph, of Brisbane, May 11, 1911 :
“Taking the Report in any Department, and as a
whole, both Management and Policy-holders may
be congratulated.
The Times, of Wellington (N.Z.), May 12,
1911'“The Report shows that the year 1910 has
been a record one in the history of the Society.
The New Business was obtained at smaller cost
ratio than in any previous year.”
The Evening Star, Dunedin (N.Z.), May 13,
1911: — “The results.... have surpassed the most
sanguine anticipations. The increase in the amount
of new business can only be characterised as
phenomenal. The affairs of the Colonial Mutual
Life are evidently in the hands of able men.”

PHONOGRAPHS
THE EDISON PHONOGRAPH was invented by Thomas
Alva Edison, and is the only sound-reproducing instrument
that has had the continuous service of the great inventor. It
is still controlled by Mr. Edison, and it still has the constant
benefit of his efforts as an inventor and his advice as a scientist.
No other sound-reproducing instrument has so distinguished a
man at its head.
The Edison is the only type of machine in which the tone volume is right
for the home ; in fact, its tones are modulated so as to be most pleasing when
played in the rooms of the average house. The noisy, scratchy machine
played by your neighbor is not an Edison.
There is nothing in the whole range of music that the Edison Phonograph
cannot reproduce as well, or better than, any other machine. "Mechanically it
has no equal; its construction is a marvel of workmanship and material.
You ought to know more about the Edison. There is a model to suit
everybody’s means, from the Gem at £2/12/6 to the Amberola at £45.
Get complete lists from your dealer, or from us.
NATIONAL PHONOGRAPH CO. OF AUSTRALIA, LTD.

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
Catarrh is inflammation of tiie lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure I If so, try our
great remedy.

ma. Write for booklet free, or send

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, W.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burg-ess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Bath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for’motors
carriages and riding tours. ' ’
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office—or 3-our own residence—direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds. 1 ’
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Caves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices 42 Castlereavh Street
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

CHILD BITTEN BY A SPIDER.
“ For weeks she could not put her foot to the ground.”
REXONA PROMPTLY CURES.
yyi Y little girl, aged five, was bitten on her foot by a big black spider,
with a red spot on its back,” writes Mrs. Fanny Kann, 133 Bridge
Street, Valley, Brisbane, Q. “ Her foot
was very swollen. For weeks she could
not put it to the ground. I thought she
would lose it, there was such a deep
hole in it. I tried all kinds of lotions and
ointments, and poulticed it, but nothing
seemed to reduce the swelling. I was
quite alarmed, it lasted so long. One
day I was at my mother’s place, and she
gave me a little Rexona Ointment to try.
The first time I used it I found it drew
all the poison and pain out of it, and
within a week the foot was cured, and
she was able to wear her boots again.
We never had such a good ointment be=
fore, and shall always keep a tin of it in
the house.”
There are so many uses for Rexona that a pot of this
New Rapid Healing Ointment should be kept in every
home: One day it may be useful to cure the bites of
Insects ; another day to heal the Cuts, Bruises or Scalds
which are for ever occurring: in everyday life. Again,
for the more serious complaints, such as Bad Legs,
Piles, Eczema, Ringworms, and the various forms of
Skin Disease, Rexona is a proved remedy.

Night and the Singer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE sgffron sunset loitered up, the pgss,
I Thep dropped into the day-jit fiinter-
land;
The blotted shadows, thrusting through the
grass,
Showed like the fingers of a Titan s hand ;
And shot with sudden silver, the lagoon,
Flaked with the splendor of the springing
moon.
The rutted side-track where the oxen go;
Lean, ghostly scandent limbs and shrivelled
bark;
And ever and again, beyond the glow,
The still white-columned forest and the
dark
Mottled in light and shade, and otherwhere
The reed-beds pattered like a monk at prayer.
Bull-throated, heavy-shouldered, bare of
breast,
Thewed as an ox is thewed, and tawny-
“ skinned,
The blue-eyed Singer spoke: “Is this the best
Life gives us—this, that men have spurred
and sinned?”
Then following, steady-eyed, the spiring
smoke,
Into the Dawn-Song of the Hewer broke:
“When the reddening ranges show and shape
Like a saw-blade washed with wine,
Ho! For the hills where the white scarves
gape,
And the lopped trunks lie a-line!
For the creeping dawn in the gully-gaps,
the sun-splashed boles aflame,
Hola! Ho!
For the kings that go,
And the pawns that play the game!
“Timed to the swing of the axe-tune's charm
Is the sweep of the gasping saw,
The level throw of the driving arm,
And the lift of the arms that draw,
The blundering crash and the canting fall,
When the bruised earth rocks and groans —
Hola! tie!
For the pawns that play
For the dead king's crown and bones!
“There is danger that whirls from the wheel-
ing dust
In the streets of the thirsty town —
Yet here is the shuddering backward, thrust
Of the brown trunk reeling down;
And a meagre inch is the margin-line
'Twixt, life and the leaping Death —
Hola! Ho!
For the hazard-throw,
And the gamblers' blood and breath!''
The Singer ceased, but twanging through
,oiir' blood
His strenuous music harped tumultuously;
The vast deep-throated valleys, the dim wood,
The rhythmic axe, the swaying white-
gapped tree
Crashing to lie the bearded creek along—
Ran, richly-clamorous, shouting through his
song.
Far down the listening air the Singer’s
rhyme
Drew into silence; yet we know, as then,
That it shall finger, through the aftertime,
The hidden hatp within the hearts of men.
Singing, in scattering chords, that world
that lies
Beyond walled gardens and a woman’s eyes.
VICTORIA.
J. ALEX. ALLAN.
Mr. Maunder’s Mistake.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
road stretched out dusty and unin-
I viting; the sun was setting behind a
bank of purple cloud.
The swag-laden Mr. Sharp, in a mood
made malignant by unaccustomed exercise,
hailed the lagging Mr. Maunder with acer-
bity.
“Carn’t ’elp it, Sam,” said the latter re-
signedly. “This ’ere walkin’s too like ’ard
work fer me. ’Ow much further, mate?”
“Reck’n that’ll be ole Lillicrapp’s shanty
standin’ back in the little bit o’ scrub yon-
der,” replied Mr. Sharp brusquely. “Now,
Jerry, yer know the programme, don’t yer?”
Mr. Maunder nodded. “Me an’ you’s
walkin’ round the world fer a bet o’ five
’undered p’und apiece,” he began, with the
air of one rehearsing a task ; “an’ knowin’
him to ’ave bin a great pede—pede—wot’s-
its-name, in ’is young days, we couldn’t pass
by ’is ’ouse without lookin’ in on him.”
“That's orright as fer as it goes,” said
Mr. Sharp with approval; “but yer mustn’t
fergit we gotter make ourselves sech conjoo-
gal eomp’ny that ’e’ll want us to spend the
night with ’im —’e lives orl alone an’
oughter be ou’y too glad o’ our ’igh-class
sassiety. I dessay ’e’ll be good-’earted
enuff to put up with you fer my sake, Jerry.
Well, I’ll git ’im torkin’, an’ then you’ll chip
in an’ say ’ow terrible tired you are arter
trampin' orl day long, so wot price bed?
Where did Bill say the ole bloke kep’ the
boodle ?”
“In a small wooden box in a little cup-
board openin’ out of ’is room,” prompted Mr.
Maunder.
“Good boy, Jerry,” commented Mr. Sharp
encouragingly. “You’ll slip in there while
me an’ ’im‘s busy yappin’, pinch the box an’
'ump it as fer as the gate, where you’ll ’ide
it among the bushes; that done, you'll come
back to the ’ouse an’ turn in. Presently
I’ll join yer, an’ then when the ole man’s
gone to roost, we’ll mizzle an’ find Bill
Smithers ready waitin’ on us with a ’orse
an’ trap. In two hours’ time—less’n two
hours’ time —we’ll be in Melbourne, safe
with the swag.”
“Reck’n Bill might ha’ drove us,” grumbled
Mr. Maunder. “Me feet are that crool sore
I kin ’ardly walk.”
“That wouldn’t never ’ave done, Jerry,”
said Mr. 'Sharp reprovingly. “Show a bit o’
sense. Ole Lillicrapp ain’t quite a fool, an
we gotter let ’im see we bin ’oofin’ it, else
’e’ll pass us out quick ’n’ lively. Now then,
’ere we are. Pull yerself together an look
as pleasant as that face o’ yers’ll let yer,
matey.”
jfle knocked modestly at the door of the
unpretentious building under whose galvan-
ised iron roof Mr. Jonah Lillicrapp lived a
life of comparative seclusion, a retirement
fostered by popular rumors regarding his im-
mense and hoarded wealth. The summons
was attended by the hermit himself, a tall,
gaunt old man with a bushy grey beard, and
a roving eye which rested with manifest
disfavor upon the applicants for admission.
“Wot d’yer want?” he demanded, aus-
terely. “I ain’t got nothink to say to tramps.
If I was to give to ev’ry cadgin’ loafer wot
come along I should be on the road meselt
in a week’s time.”
Mr. Sharp looked pained, not to say
shocked.
“Yer makin’ a mistake, guv’nor,” he said
with dignity; “but p’raps yer ain’t see’d the
papers lately. My name’s Joe ’Opkins, an’
this ’ere’s Peter Jolly. We’re walkin’ round
the world fer a wager o’ five ’undered p’und
apiece, an’ we couldn’t pass by yer ’ouse
without fust droppin’ in to shake ’ands with
the finest pede —pedestal in the ’ole of Ors-
tralia.”
Mr. Lillicrapp’s hairy countenance under-
went a sudden and remarkable change. He
beamed benevolently upon his athletic visi-
tors.
“Well, now, I take this real good-’earted
of you,” he declared warmly. “Come inside,
mates. No, I ain’t seen the papers the last
few days. Jest fancy—-round the world!
That whips me entirely. My biggest walk
was Melbourne to Sydney—beat orl records.
The noospapers was full of it at the time.
If you like I’ll read you wot they said about
me arter you’ve ’ad some tucker. Come
along inside. You mustn’t go no further to-
day; you’ve made a fairish start, and I can
five you both a shakedown for the night.”
le ushered his guests into a small room,
where a rickety table was littered with the
remains of a meagre repast. “I can’t offer
you much of a spread,” continued Mr. Lilli-
crapp, as he reinforced the table appoint-
ments with a knuckle of cold pork and a
couple of cracked cups; “but wot I ’ave
you’re welcome to. If there’s any think else
you want and don’t see, ask for it; if I’ve
got it you shall ’ave it; if I ain’t, you won’t.
Wot do you gin’rally walk on, mates?”
“Ho, we ain’t partic’lar,” replied Mr.
Sharp, truthfully. “One o’ the principal
clauses in the wager is we ain’t allowed to
carry no money in our pockets—we gotter
live on wot kind-’earted people give us be the
wayside Our steamboat passiges is paid
afore’and,” he added hastily, as he saw the
cold light of suspicion dawn in his host’s
venerable eye. “At Sydney we ’ope to ketch
the Maori fer ’Frisco.”
It was not until he had refreshed himself
with considerable meat and drink that Mr.
Maunder’s tongue was sufficiently loosened
to enable him to participate in a conversa-
tion which dwelt mainly upon the extraord-
inary feats of endurance sometimes accom-
plished by the redoubtable Mr. Lillicrapp.
“I’m a ’ole man, but I’d walk round the
world with ’im not once but twice over,” he
announced, indicating Mr. Sharp with a
stubby thumb. “Me an’ ’im’s been together
now fer I dunno ’ow many year. Never
separated fer more ’n three, or maybe six
months at a stretch, ’cordin’ to the charge
Sam intervened with celerity.
“Yer good feelin’s does yer credit, Jer —
Peter, meantersay,” he said with conviction.
“If ev’rybody on’y ’ad a ’eart like yers, wot
a world this ’ud be! But yer lookin’ dog-
tired, matey ; yer must take care o’ yourself—
yer ain’t as young as yer was twen’y year
agQ. P’r’aps Mr. Lillicrapp ’ere ’ll s’cuse
yer goin’ off to bed while ’e reads me them
interestin’ noospaper cuttin’s he was torkin’
about. Yer know we gotter make a early
start in the mornin’.” He gave Mr. Maun-
der a warning wink.
Nothing loth, Jerry took the hint, and for-
tified by his host’s benediction, retired to
rest in a small iron room, simply furnished
with two infirm stretener-beds.
For three interminable hours did the long-
suffering Mr. Sharp listen to lengthy remin-
iscences of his elderly entertainer’s past
efforts on road and track, a narration inter-
spersed with anecdotes, redounding to the
great personal glorification of the raconteur.
At last, however, Mr. Lillicrapp showed un-
mistakable signs of failing animation, and
Sam’s chastened spirits rose, especially when
the old gentleman unlocked a cupboard and
produced therefrom a bottle and two tum-
blers.
“Limejuice,” exclaimed Mr. Lillicrapp.
“Finest drink in the world if you’re in ’ard
trainin’ and want to keep fit. D’ you like
it sweetened or plain? I drink it plain me-
self. Lord! it’s grand stuff! Keeps me
alive, matey.”
“I wouldn’t git in the ’abit o’ takin’ it, if
I was you—it’s apt to lie a bit sour,” said
the disappointed Mr. Sharp, himself not
without acidity* as he accepted the proffered
glass.
“Nonsense ! Couldn’t ’ave a more whole-
somer drink. I does a tidy bit o’ walkin’ still
—collectin’ speciments for dealers in objecks
o’ nat’ral hist’ry. The curate o’ the Mel-
bourne Zoo is a dear ole cobber o’ mine.
Nex’ time you run acrost ’im, ask ’im if he
knows ole Jonah, and see wot he ses. I’m
takin’ a case inter ’im to-morrer. I’ve bin
up in the ’ills for the last fortnight, and on’y
got ’ome this mornin’. Glad I did, as it give
me the pleasure o’ meetin’ you and yer
mate.”
Mr. Sharp thanked him curtly, and seiz-
ing a favorable opportunity, emptied his
glass out of the window.
“When do you expect to be back in Mel-
bourne again?” inquired Mr. Lillicrapp, as
he, extinguished the lamp by a great effort of
lung power.
“To-morrer mornin’,” responded the ab-
sent-minded Mr. Sharp. “Meantersay to-
morrer mornin’ six year, punctual to the
day,” he corrected himself, grateful to the
darkness for hiding a spasm of unavoidable
confusion.
“This way—mind, there’s a step here.
•Test wait ’arf a moment while I strike a
light,” besought Mr. Lillicrapp, who wasn’t
listening.
He kindled a fag-end of candle, and by its
feeble glimmer the two men became aware
of the disreputable form of Mr. Maunder,

that glided unobtrusively down a narrow

“Hullo!” exclaimed Mr. Lillicrapp rather
gruffly. “I thought your pal was in bed
and asleep this three hours.”
“ ’Ush ! Fer ’Eaven’s sake ’ush !” whis-
pered Sam excitedly. “Don’t take no notice
of ’im—-’e’s walkin’ in ’is sleep. Look at ’is
starin’ eyes. ’E’s that anxious to be movin’
that keep in ’is bed ’e cannot, try ’ow ’e will.
Ev’ry blessed night fer the last month ’e’s
started up orf ’is sleepless pillar the same
way. If you was to wake ’im sudden-like
it ’ud be ’is death. I’ll ’ave to foller ’im
now till ’e drops o’ eggs’austion. So long,
boss; s’cuse me runnin’ orf like this ’ere,
won’t yer? Ain’t it enuff to make a bloke’s
'eart bleed? See, the pore ole man’s ’umpin’
my swag as well as ’is own, sech a ’urry ’e
is to be takin’ the road.”
As the somnambulist vanished into outer
darkness, something distinctly resembling a
hoarse chuckle was drowned by Mr. Sharp
opportunely stumbling over a step that was
lying malevolently in wait for him.
“Well, if you must, you must,” said the
bewildered Mr. Lillicrapp. “I never ’eard
o’ sech a thing, not in orl me born days.
Lord! the ole ehap.’s game to the backbone.
I on’y ’ope he gits ’ome safe and lives to
spend that live ’undered pound—he’ll ha’
earned it honest, my word! So will you,
mate. Well, good luck to you both.”
“You gravy-eyed porkipine, you!” hissed
the incensed Mr. Sharp, as he overtook Mr.
Maunder half-way down the gardep path.
“You nearly botched the ’ole bloomin’ show.
Wodger want to go sneakin’ round with
them swags afore I was ready? If it ’adn’t
a-bin fer me kiddin’, ole Lillicrapp ’ud ha’
smelt a rat.”
“Save yer puff, Sam,” requested the porcu-
pine with acrimony. “Bill’s waitin’ on us
a chain or so along the road. ’E tol’ me
to git yer on the move some’ow. We begun
to think as ’ow you an’ the ole bloke ’ad
fallen asleep in one another’s arms. The
box is in the trap—orl we gotter do is clear,
quick ’n’ lively.”
“ ’Ark!” whispered Sam, holding up an
imperative hand. “Wot’s that? ’Ere,
dodge in among them bushes, sharp now!
Ole Lillicrapp’s twigged the rook. Lie low,
fer (law’s sake!”
Together they crouched behind a flowering
oleander, as a loud voice coming from the di-
rection of a hessian stable was heard angrily
adjuring an invisible recusant to “Come up,
and be damned !” A few moments later, Mr.
Lillicrapp, bestriding a shaggy and self-
opinionated pony, cantered within a yard of
the spot where Sam and Jerry lay ensconced.
“’E’ll run bung inter Bill,” murmured Mr.
Maunder, not without anticipation.
“S’truth! I’d give somethin’ to see Bill’s
ugly mug !”
“Shut yer fat ’ead, carn’t yer?” growled
Mr. Sharp ferociously. “Listen !”
“Wo ho! Stiddy, lass! Hullo! Who
the devil are you?” It was Mr. Lillicrapp
seeking information, menace in his tones.
“Where the blazes yer cornin’ to- —’ave yer
bought the ’ole blanky road?” counter-ques j
tioned Mr. Smithers gruffly. “If yer want
to know ’oo I am, strike a light an’ look at
the name on me collar—you’ll find me iden-
tity there in indelible markin’-ink.”
Mr. Maunder sniggered. “Good ole Bill!”
he remarked approvingly.
“No offence, mate,” resumed Mr. Lilli-
crapp’s voice in somewhat milder accents.
“Did you ’appen to run agin a couple o’
blokes goin’ your way? They’d be carryin’
swags and a small wooden box along o’
them.”
“Yus,” replied Mr. Smithers promptly.
“I didn’t take notice if they was ’umpin’ a
box, but I see’d two men—a dirty-lookin’ ole
chap with white whiskers, an’ a younger
bloke, undersized little monkey of a man—”
(Mr. Sharp muttered strange words beneath
his breath) —“goin’ as ’ard as they could
pelt down the road—that direction. D’yer
want ’em partic’ly?”
“My oath ! Do I not!” said Mr. Lillicrapp
with emphasis. “They’ve snatched a box o’
mine I wouldn’t lose for a ’undered quid.
The Lord knows wot they want with it
either. Told me they was walkin’ round
the world for a wager, and like a silly mug
I believed ’em. Thanks, matey. I’ll nail
them yet. Come up, lass !”
As the sound of cantering hoofs died away
in the distance, Mr. Sharp and his com-
panion emerged from their hiding-place and
made expeditiously for the road.
“Look alive —tumble in!” requested Mr.
Smithers, with an anxious backward glance
over his shoulder. “Wot a time you’ve
bin, Sam. It’s ’ell an’ leather now! I’ve
put the old ’un on the wrong scent, but ’e
won’t git fer afore ’e twigs I was on’y kiddin’
’im. Look slippy, Jerry ! Orl aboard. Ri’o
—git arp, there !”
The trap swung into the centre of the
track, and for sevei’al minutes the pace pre-
cluded anything in the nature of a sustained
conversation. At last, however, Mr. Sharp
rose from an uneasy stooping posture he had
adopted, and spoke: “Rum lookin’ box, Bill;
the lid’s drilled with little ’oles an’ wire-
netted over. S’pose it’s orl right, mate?”
“That’s to keep the yaller-boys from
jumpin’ out o’ nick,” explained Mr. Smithers,
who was in high good-humor at the success
of his scheme. “But ’ow the blazes did
ole man Lillicrapp come to- Look out
there, the road’s up! ’Old tight, Sam, fer
Gawd’s sake! Kim up, blarst yer!”
The moon shot suddenly from behind a
cloud as the trap tilted precariously, and
with a slide and a stumble the bony quad-
ruped in the shafts sank deliberately upon
its knock-knees. At the same instant, Mr.
Sharp, whose curiosity concerning the con-
tents of Mr. Lillicrapp’s alleged strong box
had prompted him to a searching investiga-
tion, uttered a yell of terror, and precipi-
tated himself sidelong into a ditch. Mr.
Smithers, giving vent to a peculiar guttural
exclamation, flung himself recklessly for-
ward, and pitching over the splash-board,
alighted at full length upon his prostrate
steed. Mr. Maunder, after accomplishing a
bodily contortion, remarkable in a man of his
mature years, made a vain but deteiunined
attempt to achieve the impossible, and sit
upon his own feet. Meanwhile half a dozen
lively and robust snakes, issuing from their
cramped place of confinement, flopped one by
one into the roadway, and went wriggling
off in all directions.
“ ’Oly Moses ! S’welp me. I’ll never touch
another drop!” Mr. Smithers’s quavering
voice would have moved the stoniest heart
to pity.
“On’y let me lay ’old o’ yer, Jerry, you
greasy ass! On’y let me lay ’old o’ yer!”
petitioned the enraged Mr. Sharp, as he
crawled painfully out of the ditch, and by
almost superhuman agility avoided the coils
of a reptile of undecided habits.
Mr. Maunder said nothing; fear and
amazement held him palsied and mute.
Every moment the ominous clatter of a
horse’s hoofs, coming at a hand-gallop, drew
nearer.
LAWRENCE B. JUPP.
An Autumn Evening Reverie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
fHMHE gods that hold the ribbons of the
wind
Drive their cool steeds up from the icy
South
Over an Autumn road with sunshine gay;
Swathes of fine light the city’s forehead bind,
And blushing evening turns her face away
With wonder’s kisses burning on her mouth.
(To make my meaning very plain and clear,
The weather has grown colder, and the breeze
Is blowing from Tasmania. All the West
Is striped with red and yellow, and down
here
It’s getting almost dark enough to please
A pair of lovers who sit down to rest.)
With the long whisper of a chill delight
The glowing water ripples on the shore,
Full of the heart-break of all memory —
Dear memory trailing round her ankles white
The gossamer of dreams, and over me
Throwing the worn veils of the Nevermore.
(I know the past was sordid as a Jew,
Penurious, grasping for his cent, per cent.;
Weighing out joy in balances awry,
Eager to take and hold my I O U,
Till credit failed, and nothing but a sigh
Remained to tell the ivay youth's money
went.)
Dear God! Her lips were red, her cheeks
were pale.
What magic robbed the turquoise for her
eyes?
Wind, clear with hope, beneath the apple
bough,
That rained pink blossom o’er an old love
tale?
Ah, sere, brown leaves that o’er a broken
vow
Fell ere the winter made the dead year
wise!
(In truth she was a little commonplace,
And looked consumptive, and her yellotv hair
Gould not redeem her features, which were
plain.
Her love died young, which is a common
case,
And, though our latest parting gave me pain,
A fortnight eased me of my heavy care.)
Gold of the summer! Song of happy bees !
Breeze flaunting poppies in the pride of sun!
Cream in the arbor! Dalliance in the shade!
The wine-warm glances of my nut-brown
maid !
How the lights wane, and the far distance
flies —
Some happier lover all my treasure won!
(A milkmaid with some freckles and brown
eyes.
I well remember her round, shapely arms,
Sinewed with farmwork —how they used to
cling
At evening by the slip-rails! Should I prize
The songs without one H she used to sing,
If I were owner of her present charms?)
Glamor of lights and music, speech and song,
The thunders of the pit and gallery,
Long vigils at the stage door in the lane —
And lo ! She comes 1 Life’s wine is sweet
and strong,
Despite the dregs grown bitter with long-
pain—
Her tresses brush the sleeve of memory!
(A rather clever actress in her way,
I felt the witchery of her ancient spell,
For all her painted youth of fifty years.
1 spent six evenings watching that old play.
Next year I saw a brace of pretty dears,
Her daughter’s children —and I loved them
well.)
Dusk is the long rose clinging to the wall.
Is it a dream? Oh, white arm, round and
bare,
In the wild setting of the summer moon!
Oh, dimpled face that holds the night in
thrall!
Oh, voice of music, to sweet thoughts attune!
Oh, wonder of that raven rippling hair!
(Throgmorton’s tvife. I saw her one hot
night,
Or morning, when the clock was striking
three.
She threw the window up to get some air.
She really lias got splendid eyes, all right,
And any quantity of fine, dark hair;
But she had never any time for me.)
O Memory ! With the pang of parted years,
Like half-forgotten sweetness of dead
dreams,
The old loves thronging cloud the evening
waj's,
Seen through a mist of unavailing tears.
Oh, for the fairer lights and clearer days
Whose sweetness for a moment round me
streams!
(Yet don’t imagine I should care to meet
The mob of all the sweethearts that were
dear.
Fond memory tells me lies of summer’s blue,
But life is mostly things to drink and eat;
Old myrtles mix their wreaths with joyless
rue,
And dead romance re-lives in younger beer!)
N.S.W.
PAT O'MAORI.
